The Treasure of EVONYMVS, conteyninge the wonderful hid secretes of nature,
touching the most apt forms to prepare and destyl Medicines, for the
conservation of health: as Quintessece, Aurum Potabile, Hippocras, Aromatical
wynes, Balms, Oils Perfumes, garnishyng waters, and other manifold excellent
confections. Whereunto are joined the forms of sondry apt Fornaces, and
vessels, required in this art. Translated (with great diligence, labour) out
of Latin, by Peter Morvvyng fellow of Magdaline Colleadge in Oxford.

Imprinted at London by Iohn Day, dvvelling over Aldersgate, beneath Saint
Martines.

Cum priuilegio ad imprimendum solum.

Iohn Day to the Christian Reader.
Considering with myself (loving Reader) many and sundry times, how yt
nothing is so desired, so acceptable, or so necessary in the time of
dangerous infirmities, and pearel of bodily health, as to preserve those yt are
free of sickness fro the violence of the same, and to restore the sick, weak,
and languishing patient unto his former estate and pristinate health, which
thing is performed by the noble and excellent knowledge of Physic: and
again, howe that this Art is exercised, yea of famous and learned men, which
with out any great profit to the patient, or worship to ye minister, because
their medicines are negligently prepared: I thought it my part, by this my
travail and expenses, somewhat to serve herein thy necessity. Wherefore, I have
caused this precious treasure to be translated into our usual, and native
language, that like as all men are subject to sickness: so in likewise all men
may by this occasion learn the way unto health. And because thauthor (whose
name I spare to manifest, for fear of inuious detracters) do in many places of
this his work, send the Reader unto Philippe Vlstadius Brunsvvick: therefore I
will with expedition (if this my labours shall be thankfullye accepted,) also
publish them, sparing neither labour, diligence, or charges for to bring
them both to their perfection, and also thy necessary use. As touching the
excellency of the preparing medicines, the utility springing thereof, and
the argument of the work, because it is exactlye set out by the author in
the preface following, I will leave to trouble the further herein. Fare most
heartily well in Christ. At London the.ii. of May. 1559.

The preface of the author to the Reader.
i Of the invention of liquors and oils destilled. ii Of the scope and end of
this book. Whereof more also shall be spoken about the end of the Preface. iii
The praise of them that can be content to make common the best and most
effectuous medicines they have. iiii How much the dressing and manner of
preparans avails in every thing.
THe Art of destillatio (which they call Chymia, Alchimia, Alkimia, Chemia
Suidas calls it, and Alchemia) hath invented many profitable things for mas
life, and in Physic also certain marvelous things, and praise worthy, if a
man prepare them right and diligently. For the lack of knowledge,
Covetousness or negligence of Physicians and commun Apothecaries is the cause
why many such preparations are set little by: and worthily, if you respect that
which they prepare: unworthily, if ye mark the Art it self, which certes is
most excellent and most profitable: which seems to be the cause why it lay hid
so long, and began to come in ur very late. Some ascribe unto Hierom of
Brunsvvick, which about.lxx. years past practised Physic at Argentyn, the
original of getting out waters, as they cal them, and liquors and oils out of
simple medicines by the strength of fire: but they are much deceived, for this
Art was not invented by him, but written in our Dutch tongue and first set out by
him. In mine opinion this invention is so aucient as the invention of the very
Chymia, which I suppose was celebrated first and put in writing by men that
used the Punicall or else the Arabic tongue, a little after the age of the
Grecian Phisicions, Of those I speak that writ almooste the latest, as Aetius,
Oribasius, Actuarius, Psellus. In certain libraries in Italy even at this day,
are there extant certain wrytinges of Chymia by certain late Grecians, namely a
certain Philosopher called Stephan: al so a book entitled of the changing of
metals, which comunlye are called Chymia, or Arcymia. There is also a book to
be had of Alchymia made by Auicenna unto Assis a Philosopher. Geber (the Nepheu
of I canne not tell what great Mahomet) who is celebrated as a captain and
prince of this science, what age he lived in I can not easily say. Although I
judge him not to have been the first in uentor of this Art, but one that
brought it to light and renowned it. This man in his work entitled Summae
perfectionis, discoursing excellently and disputing many things of
destillacion generally, writes that diverse manners of destilling are known
almost of all men, even as an ancient inuencio, yea in his time also, no new
thing. Certain late wryters declare the wrytinges of Alchymia not only of
Albertus Magnus, S. Thomas, Rhaza, and Auicenna, Arabic Physicians, but also of
Aristotle, Plato and Salomon, at the least wise mention to be made by the of
it: to whom I give small credence, not that I think this study to be the
newer, but certes that it was ether unknown to these philosophers or mentioned
in no place. Some there be that expound feetly and wittily al the feinings of
the Poets, and chieflye that of the golden fleece, sought by the Argonautae,
unto the multiplying of Gold or Art of enterchaunging of metalles. Some also
ascend higher, and make the first men by and by after the beginnidg of the
world, authors thereof. Some simplely make it most ancient, and affirm that no
certainty is to be had of the first inueters. At Padvvay in Italy in our time
was found a most ancient monument, namely an erthen pot, having written upon
it this Hexasticon.

Plutoni sacrum munus no attingite fures,
Ignotum est vobis hoc quod in vrna latet.
Nanque elementa graui clausit digesta labore,
Vase sub hoc modico, Maximus Olibius:
Adsit foecundo custos sibi copia cornu,
No praetium tanti depereat laticis.
This sacred to God Pluto (thieves) ware that ye touch not,
Unknown is it to you all, this that is hid in a pot.
For the elements hath ishut up digested with much pain,
In this small vessel the great Olibius certain.
Plenty with thy fruteful horn as a guard be thou present,
Least the price pearish of this liquor most excellent.
Within this pot was an other little pot with the inscription of these verses.

Abite hinc pessimi fures
Vos quid voltis cum vostris oculis emissitiis?
Abite hic vostro cu Mercurio petasato caduceato{que}
Maximus maximo donum Plotoni hoc sacru facit
Away fro hence ye mighty thieves, trudge else where go by,
What seek ye with your spying eyes, why do ye por pry
Hens with your hatted Mercury, and with his rod also.
This gift is sacred by the greatst unto the greatest Ploto.
Again, within this little pot was found a light yet burning between two
Phials, the one of Gould, the other of Silver, full of a certain most pure
liquor, by the virtue whereof they believe that this light had burnt many a
year, as did noote in their collections and gatherings of old ancient
inscriptios or Poesies Petrus Appianus and Bartholom eus Amantius Hermolaus
Barbarus also in his Corollarium or addition upon Dioscorides made mention of
this same thing, whereas he entreats of waters in comun. There is also (saith
he) a heavenly water, or rather divine of the Chymistes, which boothe
Democritus and Mercury Trimegistus knew: calling it sometimes a divine water,
sometimes a Seythicall liquor, sometimes pneuma, that is, spirit of the nature of
the firmament, and of y^ fifth essens or substance of things: whereof potable
gold, and that philosophers stone much spoken of, but not yet fond, consists.
Hereupon also is the name given unto the art calling it Psammurgicall, and
mystical, and Annophysiall and holy, and greatest: as though it had certain
secret letters, and such as it should be convenient to keep and restrein the
profane commun people from. This kind of liquor, as I suppose, doth the
Epigram signify of late found within the field of Padua nie unto the village
called Atesta, made upon earthen or brick mettle, and therefore frail, and
broken unawares by the hands of a man of the country digging the ground in the
same place. The remembrance whereof least it should pearish we have added
hereunto the very words. This holy gift to the God, c. as before. Therefore it
appears that the study of this Art was of most antiquity amongst the
Barbarus nations, but derived unto the Romains and Greeks somewhat late, nether
before perauenture that the Romains obtained the dominion of a great part of
the world: or if sooner yet covertly retained amongst a very few. Cicendulae
y^ shine in the night (saith Cardane) teach us that a liquor may be made that
may shine in the dark. It is made certes by the putrefying of those things that
have most erceding brightness, light perspicuity. I dout not but it is possible
to be made, whereof and how, I am uncertain. But these things are almost besides
our matter, saving that it might seem utterly that the way to sublimate
destil, as they term it, hath had his original of the Chimists, continued also
always with their art: was opened unto the me of our part of the world the
first, when as ye world being made known bithimpery of the Romains, sundry kinds
of spices also diners medicines unknown before to the men of Europe, to the
Greeks Latins also, began to be brought in: and afterward also more, what time
as the Mauritans me learned in the Arabic tongue doctrine, possessed a great part
of Spain, whereby certain books of learned me cam unto us: amongst the latter
sort, as I think, the works of one Bulcasis Benaberazeris who in the place where
he intreteth of the preparation of medicines, teaches also of many things to be
sublimated destilled. About the age of the same ma almost, I think Iohannes
Mesue was famous, who we read to have lived about ye year of our Lord. 1158.
But be mecioneth none other destild waters the of Roses Wormwod. Me say
Auicenna flourished the year of our Lord. 1149* who also speaks of Rose water
destild. The vsa of metalli things sublimated is far more ancient, at the
least wise touching the tradition or tretises of authors, like wise is the use
of oils that are made by descension or downward as in Rasis Aetius. A certain
ma (whose name I spare to rehers,) writ of late that these manner of destilled
liquors were not unknown to the latter Greek writers, using this argument, that
destilled liquors are sometimes named in the writings works of Actuarius, And
in dede he names in so places Rhodostagma, as in his Iulep against ye cogh: a
little following in an other Iulep intybostagma. But with these words is signified
nothing else the a simple syrup of Roses or Endiue. Rhodostactum doth Agenita
describe in his. 7. book, yt. 15. cha. with these words. Sis.ii. sextars of
the juice of Roses yt nails whereof are take away, one sertar of honey skimming
it till the.iiii. part be consumed. Ye a little before also he describes
Hydrorosatu rosed water, that also far differing fro a destiled liquor, or
water destild for it consists of.iiii. pounds of Roses the nailes take
away.v. sextaries of water: ii. sextaries of honey. The Arabians also or their
expositors, when they speak of the water of any plant, they understand the
decoctio of it in water: and likewise Nicolas Myrepsus that writ in Greek of
the compositions of medicines, whom it appears to be a very late writer, by
the barbarous words that he uses very oft: wherein I marvel this, that no
metion is made by him of waters or oils prepared in Chymistical instruments.
Only the oil Capnistum or smoked, that is destilled by descention, doth he
describe, as Aetius also. Moreover, the things that the Chimistes make may I
comprehend in.ii. kinds for the most part, that they be ether liquors or
Massy things, the liquors again are ether watery or oily: and these ether
aerie or fiery. The Massy things, are ether such as rest in the bottom: or such
as are carried upward: and that.ii. ways, ether as pure bodies and substauces,
as they which they cal sublimated, quick silver. c. or Sout for the remedies of
the eyes with Phisicions. There be also other artificial manner of preparings,
wherewith the purest and most effectual part in medicines is drawn out, and as
it were the form is separated from the matter.

And although I am not greatly travailed nether in the Chymistical nor in the
other manner of preparings, but by the way have got the kuowledge of certain
things, partly by mine own experience in some things, partly of myfrends: yet
what so ever and how much so ever this is, I will communicate it with the
studions of Physic: not as though I entend to teach perfectly and absolutely
the very Art of destilling and preparing of other things, but as it were
writing unto such as now already are not all void and without knowledge of
these things, having ether experimented them selves or red other men's wrytings.
For I have nothing so exrellet or secrete, but I covet it might ge abroad to
the publik and common profit, and although in small wealth, yet have I ben ever
of nature ready to communicate and make any man privy of any cunning I had,
which some refer unto simplicity or childishnes, other some better to the
liberality of my disposition. There be some that do utterly cloak and keep
secrete their things, and that diverse kind of men: some for Ambition, that
they may have wherewith to excel and pas other men: some also for
covetousness, to get gain thereby: other thorough the ignorance of the
ancient wryters, as though there were not put in wrytinge ether the same or
far better by men of antiquity, which now are despised of many, and a foolish
and vnsaciable lust is there always to find out new things. There wants not such
as think, great and effectual medicines should be kept secrete for this cause,
least vncunning men, as many practicioners be, without al reason almost, and al
learning, might abuse them, and convert those things which are invented for
the health of me, to their destruction. To such wold I answer, As no kind of
evil ought to be committed for this intent that any good thing might follow
thereof: so that which is good ought not to be left undone, least some evil
might ensue, for the world shall never be without them that will abuse good
things and profitable inuencions. But good men, simplely such good things as
they have, they make them common, and will not stick nor cese to do so because
they fear, that certain lewd persons may do hurt thereby. But I will leave of
this disputation, thy do I protest sincerely as I think withal my hart, that
I covet by my example to stur up learned and good Physions, that laying apart
al Ambition, Covetousness, Ignorance, Envy, if they can bring any excellent
thing unto our profession, that they wold do it gently and publicly. As for
the unlearned certes, they in ministering not only these great and effectual
medicines, of which sort we shall put diverse in wrytinge hear, but meates
also and most common drinks, out of time, do hasard and bring men's lives
many times to diseases and death: and it is well known that Hippocrates writ
how our Ptisan ministered not in due time, was the cause of death to a certain
man sick of the Pleurisy. These therefore ought to be left partly to their
ignorance, partly to their lewd malapartnes. But they that have need of the
Physician are to be admonished to do that in Physic, that all men are wont to
do in al other Arts: that is, to choose good Physicians, and learned, and such
as exercise and practise this Art by their profession, both with reasos, as a
part of Philosophy and also in the very actions and doings of cures. But I
return to the purpose. The preparing therefore (as I say) is of the greatest
moment and weight in every matter. In Rhetorik vtterans, gesture, and
pronunciation more almost move the minds of the hearers, then the very
argument that is handled. Hereupo it comes that things put in meter and in
numbers, do delight so greatly: which if a man utter in proofs, they shall be taken
for cold, dnl and solishe. Hereof comes it that one being asked the question
what was the chiefest thing in an orator, answered pronunciation: what was the
next and the third: the same. Shows and plays, that they delight the hearers
and beholders so greatly, it is much more long of the form and manner of their
forniture and preparance then of the matter played or showed. In like manner in
the very things and works, the form, the fashion, figure, manner, as well of
nature as of Art, finally a certain preparation is more marked and commended
then the matter. Likewise in physsk, the conninge to prepare things aright,
and the descression to minister them aptly, have the greatest moment and skill
most. Nether is it of greater force what thou ministrest, then in what manner.
And although there be many circircumstances to be considered, that thou may
exhibit the medicin aright, yet the manner, and the preparing is with in the
medicine, and is at it were the form thereof and part the other circumstances
are without it, as time, place, and such things as are to be considred about
the sickman. But letting pas other ways of dressing and preparing at this
preset, we will onli touch those, bi the which al the virtue and faculti or
operation is separated from the substance of the medicines, so that ye more
liquid and moist, the more pure and subtle part of every remedy or medicin,
may be bad severed and drawn out from the gross and erthy part: whether it be
gathered into a liquor drawn out of the sam remedy or medicin or into an other
certain ertarnall. c. which the barbarous writer Arnold calls Eruirtuare to
outuerteuat, or Excorporare, to outcorporate. Furthermore, if sumthings shall
seem to be written somewhat curiously or double diligently, ye must consider
that suthe pertain not to the Physicians of the common people and pore fooke,
but to such as abound more with riches and ease, have plenty of servants, or
such as remain in Princes or Kings courts: or also to Philosophers, that
busilye search out the marvelous mutation and virtues of natural things, and
in them delight them selves. To conclude, let no man marvel that so great and
lunge commendacions and praises are added to certain medicines, as unto the
Quintessences as they call them, to Aqua vities, made ba^mes, and that unto
some marvelous virtues are attributed, as to sharpen the wit and memory, to
coserue the senses and youth. c. When as even by the ancient Greeks Latins
also we read such effects and operacions to be ascribed to Triacle and other
preseruatiues and compositions, specially by the Arabians unto diverse things,
yea even of Galen also unto Triacle. There be some that I will not avow, and whose
credit I leave to the authors, whose names I write every where. But it is said
before already, that we wryt all these things for the learned and discreate
men, which for the most part shall judge more easily how much credit is to be
given to every thing. Although in very many it is not sufficient to be
furnished with learning and judgment, except experience and practise be
joined also therewith. But I make an end hear of my Preface.

things. I have.ii. written copies, and other.ii. I saw with a friend of mine,
which al did differ amongst them selves from the prynted. I did se also the
book of Quintessence of Ioannis de Rupe scissa, almost throughout word for word
agreinge with the same, that it might seem that Lullus writ out of his, or
else that some man fatherd it falsely upon Lullus, if so be it he writ before
Lullus, as we read in the Dialog of Ioannes Brasescus. Yet Symphorianus
Camperius noted that Lullus or Lullus flourished the year of our Lord. 1311.
But Io. de Rupe scissa the year. 1340. Trite.

What Destillation is, and of diverse forms and kinds.
DESTILLATION not distillatio (as learned Siluius. Destillation by ascentio or
going upward, is when the vapours or fume is carried up and be there gathered
together into water so drop down: The same author. Moist things put into a
body (for so do they cal the bigger Siluius. Certain like things natur hath
wrought, both in exhalatios above in the air, specially the the lower parts.
Therefore of a plant or any other substance ordained to be destilled: what part
of it is most meet to be extenuated and fynet (that is the purest part, the
lightest, the thinnest, the moistest, and the most superficial part next unto
the uttermost parts of the body) being first of all fined by the force of
heet, is lifted up: next such other parts as in puernes cum nie to the first
and last such a moisture of the things as is more gross that held together
the earthly parts, a certain (Plinie, I think, writes of the wode that is
called Smilax, how it willet sype through water mixed with wine, and it true)
but this is no destillation in deed, except unto such as speak improprely.
For that which is proprely called destilation is done by heet: and that from
the son, or of fire, corruption and rot tennes: By the son, as certain men
have inueted, to draw of flowers a kind of water, very ne'er to them selves in
smell and other pleasant qualities. By fire, that is, by flame that come of
aere and of aereall bodies: or else by burning cools, that are made of earth or
earthly bodies kindled,

Morouer the flame it self, as well as the cool, is diverse, not only in
respect of that it is great and little, but also of the wood whether it be
rotten and doted, or sound, stinking, or well smelling: grene or dry. Besides
this it is a great matter what bigness the furnace be of, what fashion, Syluius.
ascend up in their order: the lightest, subtilst, and hoatest first, that is,
the fire: secondarily the air: thirdly water: the earth remains in y^
bottom: and likewise I judge in the destillation of vinegar.

The nature of fire is to deminishe (as Cardane Balneum Mariae. The best kind
of destillation next unto this, is in horse dong. Then by ashes: the best in

because the substance of the olives is thicker fatter. But none

Of the virtues of licores destilled generally.
MAnarde in his Epistles. 15. book 15. chapter, saith thus: when I perceived
in the commun waters that are destilled out of plants by fire, nether the smell
nor the taste remain, but many times the contrary (for the water of wormwode I
perceived to be chymici, by what devise both the smell and the taste might be
preserved in this waters, that is found in the whole herb. It were lunge to
write all the ways, which I proved to find out this things: yet

Syluius. Waters destilled, reteyne and kiepe still the virtues of the simples
wheroute they be drawn: saving that they are more pure strong, the oftener
they be destilled, which we prove in the water of wine, for the most part,
very seldom in other things.

The virtues of licore destilled, some do alter with some matter, as honey,
Ladanum and such like, or else by putting it in ye nose of the still, which
serves for the most part, to the grace of the smell: for they bind musk,
cloves, Caphura and such like

A doubt. If so be it yt fire makes al things hot and dry: all waters
gotten by destillation, ought to be hot and dry. Nether doth the watery
substance disprove this, for when as brenning water is such, it doth both bren
and mightily make hot and dry men's bodies. Again, al waters be cold and
moyste, as long as the substance hath the upper hand. But nether of this is
always true: yea, some of this are more like unto

Therefore men be wont to axe whether these waters reteyne and kiep their own
propre virtues and strength. We once said, while we entreated of the evil
manner of curing, that they are no strengthes nor virtues, because they are
destitute of savour and taste. For water of wormwode, nether smells like
wormwode, nor is bitter yea that is more wonder, it is somewhat swiete. Yet
burning water (to speak nothing of Rose water) well declares that there is a
certain virtue and efficacy in waters. For if you will say it is such
because of fire: how comes it to pas that none Cardan.

But me thinks this things would be more diligently considered and weighed.
First, where he true, if a man destill it negligently as the comun apothecaries
are wont, in leaden limbeckes. but destill it in Balneo Mariae, and see whether
it shall lack his smell and taste, or no. Yea, it shall retain boeth, and that
effectuously, not only this herb, but also any other plant whatsoever it be,
having any savour or taste: if it be dried and weikte or stiept in wine a few
days, then destilled in Balneo Mariae or by ashes gentely, as I shall show
further within. Now when certain things have Theophrastus writes, do retain
their savour very long: other sum be unlike to their plants. for such waters
as have their virtue and force in the uttermost and superficial parts, they
fume or else more nie to the midest or utter part of the same. I am surely of
that mind with Raimunde Lullus, that water of the same quality may be gotten
out of any plant whatsoever it be, of cold plants, cold water: of hot,
hot: of dry, dry: of moist, moyste. But I will not grant that the same
virtue remains, except like savour, or like taste or both, (as in smelling
things (be left.

The cause why the smell of certain flowers as of Iasmin, of the flowers of
cloves, remains not Cardane, where as we entreat of Balneu Mariae generally.

It were good if every water once destilled, were powered again, yea or the
third time upon his own dreggees beaten, and to be suffered to putrify two or
three dais, and so to be destilled again: or rather, not upon his own dregs
that remain, but upon other like herbs of the same kind, and to putrefy in a
circulatory or a blind limbeck, and to be destilled: Brunsvvick

I saw once an alchemist, that destilled not the very herbs renewing certain
times the destillatio, and powering again ye water upon ye dregs groude upon
a marble moler: Gnaynerius.

Oats (whereof drink may be made as Ale or Bear of barley) do warm make drunk
no less then wine. Men say that in Tartaria, water of milk destilled makes
men drunk. But every water, not an element (that is alone without any mixture)
but liquor or juice mixed and compounded, being oft destilled may do the same:
for it Cardane.

Also a liquor or other thing be destilled, the thicker it is, the more it
seems to conceive heate and fire, if it be oft destilled.

It is manifest (saith Cardane) that a water may be made which shall
incotenent break the stone in the bladdar, if it be put in by a squirt or
syring. Tecolittius, or of the stones of crabes. for so may a water be made, to
break also the red marble. Moreover, that it shall not hurt the bladder, is
brought thus to pas, if the mattier out of which the water is taken be void of
any salt kind of thing, or alum, or coperoos, or of wine lies, but some of them
that we mecioned erewhyle. But there is nied of diligent trial, in cofirming a
subtle means, that such things which we have searched out so subtly, being
surely confirmed first by experience and proof, we may then deduce and bring
them to the commodity of man.

In dede I know that pigeos donge and paritary ether the one or tother,
destilled by this means, is able too break the hardest stoone that ever
Cardane. Perauenture also Chrysocolla would help unto this art, being
artificiusly made, and without sharpnes, such as is also praised of the
goldsmiths: wherefore to make Borace sum use rain water destilled, and milk
destilled, sum also honey marow: c. I hard of late, a certain practicer cured
the stone Borace mixed with burning water, to the thickness almost of honey,
mingling also Tartar, punned, or a stone cut out of a man or the grounds of
peace out of a pispot. He commanded that a man shall use this medicine by the
space of fourtene days, so that he should always mixed some with his wine when
he drunk, yea both at diner and supper. I remember I have red of certain
liquores, in which if a man put a stone or flint, it should be resolved. The
Chymistes and destillers use destilled vinegar, and

They dissolve with strong vinegar, chiefly destilled, or with the juice of
limons, perles, egg shells, stones of the reins, of the bladder, both the
corals, and they afterward dried, are quickly readily cromed betwixt ones
fingers. Siluius.

I can not let pas here to speak of the water of Epiphanius the practicionar,
which is such. Re. Antalis et dentalis, boracis, sarcocollae whyt coral, white
crystal, clay, anessede, rys, meel of orob{us} trochiscos (little roules or
balls) with water of beans made with musk. The use of it is for women to
make their faces whyt and fair, but the face must be perfumed before with
water of a decoction of barley oats: then let one baul be steeped and consumed in
bean water, and anoint the face before you goo to bed, but in the morning wash
it away with water of a decoctio of beans and bran: and again with coold
water. If the more beautify the face: for limones roasted and anointed upon the
face, they alone do beautify y^ face. If a man drink this water fasting and
anoint the place of his privities where hear grows therewith, it breketh the
stone: which is prouid by this, that if a man lay Porcellanas in it the space
of a night, the next day he shall order them with his fingers like warmed wax
(Porcellanas men call certain shells, and also precious earthen vessels.) I
have used the mo words in this, to give

Of the manifold use of lyquors destilled, both in physic, otherwise.
I Do perciue a manifold use of destilled waters, but chiefly and most of all
for physicians, which use such stilled chymistically dressed and prepared, with
oyntmentes, ether that they may have the better savour, or else to make them
hoater, and that they may pierce the sooner, they put moist linen clothes in
this voaters to cool and refresh the parts of the body, parts about the
arms, hot fury swellinges. Surgeons use such waters as dry mightily, to
wash wounds withal. But the most common use, of lunge time hath been in
mixture of syrups to be drunk, and to zulapia or iulebs, chiefly of roses
violets. There be that make diverse kinds of liquors and oils alonly for the
good savour. Glasiars also that paint glass in baking in their colours, they
do use burning waters. Goldsmiths use aqua fortis (as they call it) which
signifies a Raymund Lullus writes of y^ marvelous use and commodity of
burning water even in wars, a little before the joining of battle, to styre
and encourage destillation is necessary. Sweet water may be separated from the
salt, in a great caudron with a great and hie keuer having a beacke or nose.

A way to purge and make clean troubled waters, out of Bulcasis.


FIll a great pot with the puddled water A putting B lay two sticks or mo a
crose C upon the pot brinkes, and upon the sticks lay clean wol D well washed:
the whatsoever the woll drinks of ye vapors that ascend up, wring it out and
kiep it, and amilu or meel: for this things go dounwarde, and draw with them
to the bottom of the vessel, the gross mattier of the water.

Of Balneum Mariae generally, and of those destillations that be done by vapors
of hot
Hot water or else ye vapour of the same, send les strength into the thing that
is to be destilled, then other fire alone or else such other dry means as are
mecioned before. for yt cause as Galen saith Diploma, that is, a double vessel
(the Apothecaries as the men that still lyquors also, cal it Balneum Mariae)
melts, heats, seatheth those things whose strengthes the violence of fire
will not disperse nor separate: so, such things as be tender and gentle,



You must have a fornace A of this sor, upon the which you shall set a great
brazen vessel B full of water, C in a circle as many as it will receive, in the
botome, of the which vessels the things that you will destill, must be put.
Other build ye fornace A otherwise as though it were a vessels: B in their
broad bottoms stading inward, they contain the things that they will destill
the mouth without as though it were a bottle being covered, C in the which ye
vapour carried up by his open belly gathered together, and by the long mouth of
the same drops down. Syluius.



But why remains not the smell of certain flowers in the waters but in
Iasmin, Cariophillum, and le^is, the water comes forth wtoute savour? the
reason is declared otherwher: because yt unto so sclender and thin
substance, no substancial and thick part is joined. There Cardan. It appears
without doubt that those flowers should be destilled in Balneo Mariae, or in
vessels of glass

Balneum Mariae may be handsomely made hoate with a great pipe of copper A set in
the midst, in the bottom whereof is a grate for ye B ashes to avoid at: men
call it communly a stowe harry. Vlstadius names it a furnace of sloth. Upon
that pipe, do they make a cover of copper G with a small pipe coming out a
shore, to cary and convey the smoke out at a window or some hoale. (So do they
also make warm house flors now a days to bathe in.) The commodity of this
pipe D is then E, a ten or twelve at once, to spare time, labour and cost.



Some use brazen cupping boxes to still withal in the Balneo Mariae, glassen
limbeckes, whose noses if they be to short or broken, they join other

The herbs yt be to be destilled in a bathe or otherwise, some do pun them,
and let the remain so a while, (perauenture for certain days) before they
still them, thinking to have more plenty of water in a hot place, it were well:
but the most apothecaries and other, that sieke most for lucre gain thereby,
leave it in cold places in open confers, till the herbs lose their smell, and
be corrupted with a moist and gros air.

Some there be that put some sand also in the water of Balneum Mariae, to
the intent the heat my be the greater and more vehement: as Mathaeol{us} of Sena
in ye water that is called aqua philosophica against the frensh pocks. And he
affirms that

The water of Balneum Mariae ought to be no hoater, then that a man may suffer
his finger in it: Brunsuicensis.

Vlstadius prescribes the destillation of a certain kind of aqua vitae in
Balneo Mariae, to be done with so slow a fire, that a man may tell one, two,
three, until seven, before a drop fall.

Of such things as pertain to the commun wai of Balneum Mariae, you shall read
more with in where we make mention of aqua Camphorae out of Bulcasis, and of
Rose water out of the same.

This way of destillation in Balneo Mariae, is used also to the rectifying (as
they term it) of oils, to draw and purge the fleam from them, for only the
waters and nothing else may be lifted up and drawn out by the heat of the bath,
the oil remaining still in the bottom.

When as I perceived that waters, as they are accustomed commonly to be
destilled out of plats, they kiep nether their taste nor the same smell: I
began carefully to think upon the matter and to try many ways, yt I might
find a means how to preserve and kiep in the waters the same qualities. It
should be log to rehearse al the ways yt I proved. Onli one will I touch, which
seemed to me to be the best and ye easyest. That is this, to work on this wise
in a double vessel, after the manner that Galen would all oyntmentes to he
made, of that vessel A wherein the herb is contained B, do not touch the water
C that boils in the bigger D, but that it wax hot with nothing else then the
fuming moisture lifted up from the same. Manardus. Albeit things destilled in
this wise, although they of the plants (as the former smell, yet for as much as
they be corruptuous, they can not be long kept: Syluius.

The chief use of dong, or as some term it, a horse belly, is such: that the
matter which is to be destilled in a glasen vessel set in the dong may be
prepared by the heat thereof, as we shall declare more at large in his place,
where we shall make mentio of putrifactio and rotting. It is possible
notwithstanding for destillation to be brought to pas in y^ Brunsvvick
supposes to exceed in the middle degree, the heat of Balneum Mariae.

If you desire to have a water destilled of the flesh of any beast, you shall
strangle the beast, yt it bleed not in any wise, then take away al the fat
Brunsvvick.

The parts of beast or excrements, as blud, the liver, the lights, eggs, gall,
and ox dong, ought to be destilled in horse dong, with vessels not very close
stopped (but honey and milk a man may stop the close) least the water stink. But
if it chance to in Balneo Mariae, specially the water of excre mets of oxen
whose first destillatio is scarce found without stench: Brunsvvick. It wold
frame better if a man put to a little curtsy of salt in the putrefying or
destilling of dong, that they may corrupt the less.

Absinthium.


OF Worm wode water Iohannes Mesuae hath made mention: and of it and of Rooses
only, as the Munks that writ upon Mesuae have noted, that it is Balneo Mariae,
or if yet stroger, in ashes. But such as be destilled first stiept in any

Which commonly they call Morsus Gallen

Caepa.




THe whyt Onion destilled breketh the stone. Martianus Sanctus.

Cerasus.


Cheri water of what kind soever they be, is drunk against to much heate, is
ministered with out ye body: but particularly of sweet black cherries, which
also is commended of many against the palsy, if it be poured into the mouth:
and the mouth be well washed therewith, that it restores the use of the tongue
lost. They destill the flesh of it alone, or the kernels also beaten together,
that ye liquor destilled thereof may entye out the stony matter of the reins
and bladdar. The black and sower are called Visula, they yield agues which
couleth and confirms the strengthes: it is profitable also against thirst and
bluddye flixe: Ryffius. The water destilled of the swiete black and fresh
Cherries, is marvelously commended of Remaclus F. of Lymburg: As soon (saith he)
as it shall be powered into the mouth of one sick of the falling evil already
taken with ye fit (the potion of it is.iiii. drammes or moor) by by he revives
refreshed, neither is he any moor drawn together with any cramp, till in the
ordinary

Camphora: water thereof or oyll is thus made. Take one of the vessels for Rose
water, that is called baten (that is a belly) fill it with the sticks or cips of
pynappull tree which hath great and brood leaves, and let it be filled.
Siluius takes it so as though the roose water should be poured to the chips of
the pynappull tree, but me thinks yt cucurbita or belly, that it may be a
certain repetition of the same which he had spoken before, and let it be
covered with a vessel having a nose: then put the belly into a brazen vessel
full of water over the fire, till it begin to boil: for an oyll shall destill
(and yet they deny that oyll may be lifted up by the heat of water) subtle of
a good odour, which is called water of Camphora. Or if ye list destill it
destilled. Bulcasis. But Belluensis saith that water of Camphora according to
the Arabians (saith he) is a water that runs out of the tree that brings
the Camphora: which as his tree also is of a hoot nature in third degree: so
Camphora it self is cold: Monachi in Mesuae. Put three little bies in the vessel
of glass where the Camphora is, which shall so be turned into water.

Fragaria.


SStraweberies shalt y^ putrify in a Vessel of bras (peraueture salt may be
put to it, or sugar) and destill them. This water (saith Lullus in his.ii.
book of quintessence) is holso a woman having newly all over her face blesters
or wheals by the struck of a ston with heet, which the only washing of this
water was straight way healed with great admiration. But the virtue there of is a
hundred foold mor marvelous and stronger in operation with burning water, and
much moor with quintessence. Yea, this water mixed with quintessence or with
burning water cures the Leprosye.

Fraxinus.


Three vnces of the liquor destilled of the inner bark of ash, with as many
vnces of whyt wine is druk against the pestilence, and the same drink after.
Halicacabus is commended of some against ye stone of the reins and bladder, if
it be drounke ons or twyes a week.

Helxines.




Which we cal Parietary or Pelitory, the water thereof is profitable against
the stone, ried before in the virtues of waters destilled generally, out of

DEntdelion, in frech with us it is called Dandelion, the water thereof siemeth

WAters of the flowers of lavender, is Remaclus.

Iuglans.


THe water of walnuts not ripe made about saint Ihons tide, ministered without,
is good for wounds and hot boils, and the pestilent anthrax. Also being
drunk a two or three vnces, it cools and resists the pestilence. A water
also is destilled of the utter husks of walnutes (ether ripe, so that they be
new burst and left of the nut, or not yet at all) in the month of September,
nether skills it if they be black, so yt they be not rotten: yet the black
are counted the best. A moderate potion if it invade a man with heet) letting
blood first, is given to drink against the pestilence, as a certain
experiment. It is praised also for ye noise in the ears and the difficulty of
heering, for the diseases in the throat called angine being gargild. A water
destilled of ye leues of walnut tree about the end of May, is marvelously
commended for the drying and knitting of sores, and to bring them to a skar, if
they be washed therewith morning and evening, and a linen cloth moystened
therein be Brunsvvicencis.

Orchis.


SAtyrion is destiled roots and al, good for ye falling evil (as men say.)

Rosa,


ROos water seems to be first invented: for I find mention of it in Auicen
the.ii. book, in the chapter of rooses: Roosewater drounke (saith he) is good
in a Geleniabin, and helps to digest. And the roos and his juice are good
for a stomach to mich hoot. And although the Arabias for the most part are wont
to say water for juice or Mesue in the chapter of Rooses. And again, The Roos
and his oil and water sublimated comfort the hart. c. The same speeketh of
rooswater destilled, in the.vi. destinction, whereas he describes the iuleb of
roses, as the Munkes his interpreters do prove: of whom also this was and nomo
mentioned in Mesue, that is of rooses and wormwod. They make no epithe or
outward medicine at this day, but they put roosewater in it. In sharp vehement
and greet inflammations, to strengthen the principal members, it may be
commodiously used. It is good for the flixe of the bowels and vomiting. It
helps the inflammation of the eyes very much in the beginning. Sum are wot to
mixed with it a little thucia and sugercady. It strengthens the eyes and
sharpens the Remaclus F. Of wine mixed with roswater in time of meet, see in
Arnold in his book of wine.

Rooswater although it be made diverse ways yet the best is made by Balneum
Mariae: Matthaeolus. If thoos rooses which we call commonly carnacion, dried
and moystened with the vapoure Syluius. They may be holden in a linen cloth
over hot water, till they have drunk enough of the vapoure.

Of Roose water out of Bulcasis.


THe making of Roosewater is known in most coutires. It is better made with
water then without: better also by fire of cools then of Aharach. A. Thou
shalt make therefore in a large house by a wall a little berchile B so doth he
cal the vessel that is filled with water the bottom and sides shall be of leede,
so well closed, that it leek not in any place. Then make meet a and make two
or three round hooles in it C C C moore or les, according to the largnesse of
the vessels, and as you desire to ether much or little water D, then make a
pot of bras like to the pot made in Balneo Mariae, which thou shalt set upon the
fornaice, so that the Berchile above the furnaice be lower in situation then
the pot, (so that it may conduit the heate of the fire of the Berchyle, to
the pot: but me thinks this words do ether redound or be depraved.)
Thou shalt also is finished, put away the first rooses, put in fresh: thus
saith Bulcasis. Some man would marvel, that in such a making of the fornace,
where the fire is not put under the duble vessel or berchile (as I conjecture,
seeing he makes the bottom and sides of lead) but at the side of the fornace,
wherefore he should think it to skill any thing, whether the fire put under
the pot be made of wood or cools. Moorouer it is known (saith Bulcasis in the
same place) that rosewater of wyld

There is a destilled water made of this with us a shorter way then that
before, is this wise.



A A brazen pot such as diers use, is set to a wall whereunto a cover B made
meet with hooles, wherein the be D it of the croppinges of vynes or such like.
But in process of the destillation thou shalt close the mouth of the furnace till
the hot destillation be finished. if thou burn cools, the water shall be the
swieter. (Here is more reason then in the former, because the fire here is made
immediately under the stillatory vessels.) The second way of destillacion with
out water with fire of coals is such.





MAke a square or round fornace A with a cover C made of glased earth, so that
they may abide the fire: And when the cools are kindled, the water begins
to destill shut the mouth of the fornace* and leave holes open for the smoke to
go out at. There is an other bridgemente of the third and fourth way.



A Brazen pot A full of water is set over a fornace, with a cover bored B through
C more or les. Put under fyr of the cropping of vines or cools till the water
sis.

SAge water keeps retains his former smell. Remaclus. F.

Scabiosa.


SCabiose water is profitable given to drink to them that are diseased with any

Sedum.




THe water of ye least syngrien or houslieke, Remaclus. F.

Brunsvvick reports, that ye water of Scabiose drunk twyse or thryse a day
an ounce and a half, helps the stiches in the side, he says further, it
heals wounds inward outward, being drunk.ix. days together two ounces at
once fasting. Loke in the same author.

Solanum.




THe water of Solanum or Morrella is good against all agewes if the sick the
day of his fit abstain from all meat and drink: and a cloth dipped in it
be laid upon. It helps also the liver that is hot, and the lunges that are
dried and grieved with an Hecticke fever. Against all these diseases it ought
to be drunk with the third part of wine. Lullus in his book of waters.

Tilia.


OF the flowers of Tilia whose smell Tilia beaten. The dosis or quantity of
ministration is one or one and a half. It asswageth the griefs of the eyes:
and heals the places brent with fire or any hoot matter, that tiliae be
stiept in this water or in stead thereof the grains or kernels of quinces, or
psyllium, and the places be anointed with the horines or mouldines that bredeth
there upon: Ryffius.

Some use against the pestilere a liquor drawn by the force of fire out of the
blood of a gray or badger.

Also of the blud of ducks against poison. c. whereof thou shalt read more in
the book of destillarions of Ryffius, written in Dutch.

Some mixed the blood of a goat with medicines against the stone to be
destilled.

I would adioyne here a table of waters destilled of plants, which are
described in the Dutche books of destillacions of Hierom of Brunsvvick, for the
most part all that Gaulterus Ryffius has borrowed of him: saving that I study
to be short. as liquors also may, and are wont to be destilled of all such
plants whereof there is any use in physic. But to reckon up also the virtues
and faculties of every of them as some do, it is superfluous when as none other
for the most part, but even the very same also be attributed and ascribed to
ye waters which are unto the plants: so that these repetycions move
irckesomenesse to the reader, yea even if it be but meanly learned, never a
whit les then colewortes twice sod. Yet because Remaclus F. hath described. I
will ad hither a rehearsal and table of them, in like order as he uses and hath
recited them him self.
