Of kowes donge loke in the same.

Some say that water destilled of mans donge will heal fistulaes, also fretting
soores, and such Tinea or matering of the head: that it will also make skarres
like unto the other skin: and put away the spots or white webs of the eyes.
If it be drunk, it is good for them that have the falling sickness: it helps
them that have the gowte, it drives away the stone out of ye rains and
bladdar, it is a preservative against the biting of a mad dog or other
venomous beast. But the oil of the same which destilleth after the water, with
a greater fire, is much better to fistulaes, and cacres, Matthaeolus of Sena,
and other, you shall read certain marvelous things in Brunsvvick.

Man urine destilled, the Chemists do use to resolve certain mettalles:
Printers to make their printinge ink, but these destill it in Rose
stillatoryes.

Of vessels and diverse instruments belonging to destellation.
WHen men name a stillatory vessel, they understand the body of the still or
the Cucurbita, which the dutch men in their language, because of the fashion of
it, do cal clauam (ein kolben.) The body or gross vessel some cal the greater
vessel or Cucurbita wherein they put that which is to be destilled. Syluius for
this vessel compared to the limbeck or head, is Arabians name it, as Bulcasis
wytnesseth, Beten Batan, that is a belly. It appears also that Atanor in ye
same Bulcasis, where he prescribes the destillation of vinegar, signifies a
Cucurbita. (A furnaice, Athanor or a fixed instrument Geber describes in his
book of fornaces. The Cucurbita some barbarously do call bocciam a box. Of
the same ^igure and fashion the greater vessels are meet to digest and
putrify: thereupon when they must be destilled, the matter digested is
distributed into some les Cucurbit or boxes. The limbecke is taken, as I
suppose, both for the body and for the head, but moore properlye for the
head, as I said, is the upper vessel, that has a certain similitude of
the head, in respect of the nether, that is bigger and longar. The same vessel
(late authors call a bell and (ein helm) a salet or helmet. Tubus which comes
forth of the limbeck a length turning downward, is called a nose, a beak,
simple as it is a pipe: because the thorough the holownes thereof, the drops
gathered together in the limbeck, which all come together fold about the
lowest part of the limbecke, running forth do drop down into a vessel set
underneath, which commonly is called a receiver and a urinal. The nose and
beak are termed of y^ like figure, because this part stands out, after the
same manner that beaks do out of the noses of fouls, noses of.iiii. foted
beasts. The receiving vessel when as oils are destilled out of metals, as of
Vitriol or Corporoos, whereas there is need of a great fire, and continual,
must be very large: for

A blind limbek is that which hath no nose nor beak, nor limb or hembe which
serves for preparation and rectification or circulation, that is, perfection.
vitrial.

Such heads as be put upon bodies not standing upright, but as though they
were lying, they lack the limb folded skirt, and be like to commune earthen
Syluius.

I have sign also two heads one upon the other, that by the nether which was
bored through, part of the matter might be carried into the upper, which was
joined to the nether with nails, least y^ nether that is stopped with clay should
be taken away, when as

Some times the fashio of the head is like to a spyre ascending upward aloft,
sometime edged: sometime it waxes brood in the highest part of the head, that
it may receive the more of the vapours, Syluius.

Sublimation is taken of many simpely for destillation: other take it
particularly to lift up or cause to ascend up into the limbeck by the force of
fire, the matter that is to befixed in it: as many metally things are
sublimated.

Some join glass or earthen bottels together, which they call horned pypes,
the one whereof is set and cemented or clayed in the fire containing y^ things
to be destilled: the the other is a pretty way distant from Syluius. This
instruments are called commonly crooked: the frenchemen call them also horned.

This horned pipe is a musical instrument, which the Germans commonly call

The receiving vessel for the most part is a phiall of glass with is put, and
some times the belly thereof is set in a pot or some other holowe vessel that it
may stand the stedfaster in his place, or else for the commodity of the thing,
is set one way or other.

Vessels for circulation as they wherein the vapours ascend by course, and
again turned into liquor, desced go dounward as we shall declare within where we
shall make mentio of putrificatio Of al this the most excellent vessel is that
which is named Hermes vessel, because he was the inue bill: eve so this vessel
as though it were eared on both sides, hath pypes that beginning at ye head,
that is the upper part, descend in manner of a half a circle and are bowed
again and fastened as it were into the beginning of the Cucurbita with a blind
limbeck without a folded skirt or else a vessel of a hooll glass, that is
somewhat straiter in the midst, and out of the beginning of the belly it hath
looking out a short pipe, by the which liquor may be powered in and out. We
shall put the figures of both this within in the chapter of quintessence:
fornaces every where in this work.

The forms and fashions of vessels are diverse, and almost without number
commonly used with the chymistes and destillers, Syluius. Who so desires the
figures and names of diverse vessels, let him look in Brunsvvick. Ryffius
Andro. Louicerus and other. A man may cause to be made, of what fashion he will
in the glass makers shops, such as be in Heluetia nie unto Scaphusia, and not
far from Basil and Solodourn. But the best

Of the vessel called aludel^s, look in Geber in his book of chief
perfection. 1. 4. 44. chapter, and again in his book of fornaces. Albertus
writes it a luttell, and expounds it a vessel appointed for sublimation:
likewise Bulcasis. The same wills arsnick to be sublimated in an earthen
dyshe glased, the fashion whereof he describes in Belluencis.

Of the matter for vessels of destillacion: and first against leaden and brazen
vessels.
WAters destilled in Balneo Mariae, do so far pas those that are made simply
by the tire in leaden limbeckes, as gold Balneo Mariae, with large chaplets,
limbeckes or heads, and somewhat great and of glass, do give the natural savour
and taste of the herbs flowers, wherout they be taken, without any noisome
smell of smoke or burning, which chances not al in the common leaden stills.
For the waters that are made in them, very few and seldom, specially whiles
they be new, are without notable lothsomnes of smoke and burntnes, which unto
sickmen that drink, it is not only grievous, but also hurtful: Balncum
Mariae, and although it shall be a little more chargeable unto them and
painful, yet so shall they satisfy and please both God and man. Matheolus.

Such things as are destilled in lead, I judge them altogether to be
disalowed, because of ye Cerusse and other malicious qualities of the lead,
lead, Galen condemns, because it bredeth diseases in the entrails. Syluius.

Moreover the vessel alters much, as well in destillacions as in things
sodden. Wherefore a man must take great hied in these things, that the vessels
be rather earthen or glass then of metal, and those that be of earth, yt they
be of a very pure earth, and well baked, such as be the earthen vessels of
Paris, Bellonaca, England and Spain. But glasen vessels are the best because
they be more Galens reason in every one almost of these kinds of metals. For
if he affirm that water only conveyed in leaden pipes doth breed diseases of
the bowels, howe much more ought we to fear waters destilled in a leaden
limbecke or still. Besides that no small cerusse remains cleauynge to the
inner side of a leaden head, as in destillinge of Vinegar is gathered, so by
the force of the vehement heate or brentnesse also tart, that is of sharpe
and tart plants, which infects the very wa^ers, and for the most part
makes them white like milcke, till that it be settled and sunk to the
bottom. Vessels of other metalles that bried other roust or canker, are so
much the more pearilous, as rouste or canker is more hurtful the^cerusse.
Vessels made of pure silver or gold, as they be least hurtful, so are they
more harde to be gotten because of the cost. Syluius.

The best vessels be of glass: the next, earthen that be glased both within
and without: then limbeckes of tin, the bodies (in destillinge of Roses) of
lead. Thirdly, the limbeckes and the bodies also of lead. For the strength
of the fire if it be moderate, it hurts not the lead, but these bodies of
lead must be set in syfted ashes, a handful thick, not in sand.
Fourthlye limbeckes of copper tinned within. Fifthlye brazen. But copper and
brass have two discommodities, Christophorus de Honestis, admonishes to take
heed and avoid them: Brunsvvick. Therefore saying all the most learned
Phisicions, and experience it self, do utterly disalowe waters destylled in
vessels of lead, Brass and other unclean metals, matter to the Magistrates,
that it might not be lawful for Apothecaries other to prepare any more or
sell such waters partly hurtful, partly unprofitable. Bulcasis in dede,
uses leaden bodies in destillinge of dry Roses, which perauenture may be
granted for such waters as ought to be used only without the body. Some find
no fault with vessels made of pure and best iron, because no evil quality
(as they say) can issue thereoute into the matter or liquor: and troth it is,
that certain Berchile. Bulcasis doth call a vessel in a furnace for Rose
water, which receives the boiling water derived out of an other standing
by it, in the which vessel, the body of the still containing the Roses, is set.

Of Fornaces, c.
I Declared before and described two manner of fornaces meet for destillation
by ashes, and Balneum Mariae also out of Bulcasis.

Hear will I trace out and descrybe another manner of fournayce, the use
chiefly when as any thing is to be destiled by a great fire, so that no mean
be betwixt the fire the cucurbite or pan wherein is contained the matter that
is to be destilled: after which manner oils or certain liquors that burn the
skyn and flesh are gotten out of metals, as aqua fortis. c. The use thereof
shall be also to sublimation: and if a man will set a kettle in at the top upon
theyron cross, then fill it with ashes or water and put clay about it, he shall
be gin what destillation he will. aqua fortis or such like, he shall set a
cucurbite or pan daubed with clay by by, right upon the cross. Yea this fornace
may be made also upon a strong board of wode, as I have made my self, that you
may remove it from one place to an other at your pleasure. Therefore upon a
woden table you shall buyl a four square fornace, of such bigness, that it may
be sufficient to set in a mean circubite. The walls you shall make of brick,
joined with mortar very well made, the hight shall O. where as the ashes may be
taken out, which fall from the coals from the grate builded upon it. If so
be it that an other grate were made also for the ashes to fall thorough (and
that lowest space to be somewhat dieper) a man should not niede to take out the
ashes so oft, and the fire should be the more vehement.

The letter. I. shows an other door, straight way above the grate, which I
have markte with certain pricks set overthwart in order. That door as the
nether also, it is sufficient if it be only so big as a man may easily put in
his hand at it. V. shows the iron cross, that is.ii. bars of iron, ye nether
whereof must have a bought downward in tee midst to receive the other in that is
laid overthwart him. A. and. E. declare the highest corners of the fornace, in
which so many breathing hooles (or vents as they commonly call them) ought to
B. the board upon which the hole fornace is builded is noted with. C. as appears
in the figure here present. An other kind of fornace, for oil of vitriol or
corporoos, Balneum Mariae, y^ hast already described before, whereas we entreat
of Balneum Mariae generally out of Syluius. Also in yt making of Rose water,
out of Bulcasis. Of divers and destillacions, there is a book of one called
Geber. Concerning the making of a fornace, loke in his book entitled Summae
perfectionis. that is, of chief perfection. 1. 4. 43. Of commune Rose stills
in earth pottars fornaces, although some make them also of bras, we shall
speak with in, by and by after the destillation by ashes.

Of slow Harry, or the fornace of sloth, that is, a pipe wherewith the Balneo
Mariae is made hot, we have written above where as we have entreated Balneo
Mariae.

The differences of coals shall we know in Theophrastus, but better of our
own smiths, specially goldsmiths, and of the very experience it self. Such
coals as are dug out of the ground are condemned forbidden because of their
foul smell. They are counted the best that are made of bieche or fir tree.

Dioptra or a little skrien I call an instrument, which some men hold before
their face with their

Lyquors destilled should be kept in glasen phialles with narow mouths. Let
the mouth be shit with a stopple of wod, or rather of cork, and besides that,
a man must cover it with wax and bind a parchement about it also.

Howe to close vessels and to defend them, both with clay and otherwise.
CLaying of a thing, men do commonly say for yt which is to daub with clai, to
plaster, to pariet. Tothintet we may the better keep the smell and qualities
of such things as are destilled, we must close diligently and join together
with Vlstadius chap. x If the head be not miet enough for the body but somewhat
to big, you shall put paper round about between it and the clay. Without also
whereas about moist paper about it, or else a linen cloth, and after clay it.

The mortar to join the cucurbita with the limbecke withal. c. ought to be
made with clay and hear, and to be dry before fire be put under, Bulcasis
entreating of oil of tilestones. The mortar shall be the stronger if it be
tempered not only with hear or woll, but also with gotes blood, and the more
also to be made with that is dros of iron, beaten as is found about smiths in
fields.

Of Hermes seal, that is, of joining together the mouths of glasen vessels
with a pair of hot burning tongues softly thrusting them together, and how
after they ought to be opened again, read Vlstadius. cap. 20.

Let the cucurbita also be fensed round about with clay, specially those that
must abide the fire directly put under them and nothing betwixt, and that aqua
fortis and oil of Vitrial are destilled. For the destillation in ashes, there
needs no such mortering nor claying: albeit if a man will provide for the worst
considering the frailnes of the vessels, specially where many vessels are
set together in one fornace, it shall be sufficient once or twice to pariet
the. Those cucurbitas which they use in Balneo Mariae, specially when many are
set together, some men use to like as it were house or cases.

They say glasen vessels will abide the fire better and longer, if they be
wared, that is, if they be warmed don over twice or thrice with molten wax.

Of the preparation for destillation.
FOr as much as in destillacio we seek ye separation of the elemets either for
one or mo of them, or else to thintet that they quintessens: separation truly
can not be don without heate. For heat unites and gathers together such
things as be of one kind and nature, and they that do differ and disagree it
separateth to the intent this might duly be brought to pass, both the Chemists
and Phisicions have invented diverse means and ways. They term this
preparation diversely and give it sundry names, digestion, leauening,
putrefaction or rotening. Digestion they call it respectinge, the concoction
that a certain inward virtue, but increased or styred up by an outward heat,
comes unto an humour or moisture, so that a certain comune and general
quality be mixed and spread throughout the h^ole body, with a hot spirit, one
that moves and breads bubles, as it is in ye mixture of true leaven in dede
knode with meal: also in vinegar powered upon the earth. And this affect is the
beginning of rotennes in such things as have superfluous humour. That which is
called putrifactio

The savour or other quality of ye putrefying matter, in my mind, it is not
possible it should infect the mixture that is put in a glasen cucurbita with
glass is: for the common grene glass will gather a certain duskishnes and as
it were a skin.

Destillation that is done by rottennes, or with horse dong both alone and with
lime be sprinkled and oft changed, or with barks watered, or other rottening
things, how so ever it is profitable to the Chymistes and destillers many
ways, a physician not withstanding ought not to receive it, for a cause in
rotten wod and evil smelling, thorough the rottennes or some other cause:
specially if the Syluius.

Of this kind of destillation in horse dong read before in the latter end of
those things which we writ of Balneum Mariae generally. At this present we
should entreat rather of rotening then of destillatio. But because of rotening
also in horse donge seems to be disalowed, if destillacion in it be reproved,
it is not altogether from our purpose

Putrification or rotening in horse dong with lime is otherwise done on this wise,
that the dong every day or every other day or by longar distance, be
sprinkled with warm water: otherwise, without sprinkling, when as the dung of
it self serves to be hot enough. A pit or hole being digd in some corner of
the house, they lai one course of horse dog about a foot thick, that is three
handbread: then an for the by course. There is need of three burden for the most
part of dong, as much as a porter is hable to cary.

Some put the things that are to be destylled (specially such as be
stiepte in wine) into a tin botel, and that they do set in vnslaki lime closed,
which they quench at certain times now a little and then a little, with
rain water. Some mixte Oaten straw with the horse donge, and sprinkling it with
hot water, set in their vessel, Cardanus teaches. If a manne covet but a
light heate, it shall be sufficient to put it simply in chopte straw.
Certain of the Germans that live in stouffes that is hot houses, the winter
time, make A. and in the vpperparte they set two or three glasen pots. C.
upon little iron bars and pariet and rub them over diligently with clay.
In this filled full of fine sand or ashes, both things may be destilled
which I would should be tried, and also certain lyquors may be rectified or
prepared. c, and by this means a man shall save both labour and cost.

Some to digest the matter which they will destill in the son, provide a
holowe lokinge glass. D. beams. B. which they received: and direct them into
the vessel C. wherein the matter is contained: for this purpose (as I am
informed) the figure of a holowe parabolae should be more apt and meet: a
parabolae conus. Read Archimedes in his Book of burnnynge glasses.

The same also may be done very well with balls of Crystal. A* hanged between
y^ vessel B. so yt the beams of the sun may com unto the vessel. C by the
balls. The only end of all these preparations is, that the liquor may be
drawn out more easily, more aboundatly, and more

The time of this digestio doth vary according to the matter, which the more
massy it is, the longar time doth it require: new herbs need the less time,
when they are stiept or weekt in wine or other liquor: for they be the
tenderer, and if they should be let stand long, they would have a certain
hoarinesse: the same dried must stand a little longar: then the seeds: last
of all the rotes: so that almost double time is necessary for them that follow
to yt which goes before them: as to new

The putrification of herbs to be destilled in horse donge, is wont to be
done almost by the space of.xiiii. days, but in Balneo Mariae. ii. or.iii.
natural days. Brunsvvick.

Such things require chiefly putrification, whose substance is somewhat dry,
gros, thick: raw have les nied which are contrary wise, and are digested

Putrification is sooner done in cloudy wheather then in fair. Vlstadius.

To the most part of things that are prepared in horse dong, some salt is
cast, as unto beasts blood, to flesh, to fyshe: Guainerius.

To digest, take a blind lymbeck: if the matter be pure, as pure liquor, then
take a vessel for circulation, of which sort the pelecan is the best:
although it can skarsly be made, and not without great cost. c.

But in digestion or putrification, the hoal vessel would be hid with horse
dong or with the refuse of grapes. c. To circulation the half of the vessel,
or at the least the third part, must loke out into the free and cold air (as
also to destillation.) Vlstadius chap.viii. where as he teaches at large of
putrification in horse dong.

Of the rectification of liquors destilled.
WAters destilled in Balneo Mariae, ought to be set in the sun and to be
rectified, namely in a glasen vessel, (the mouth whereof let it be bound
about and stopped with leather) full of the destilled water, so that the third
part must be left empty. then set it in hot sand by the space of forty
days, that all the fleme may be consumed, the third part of the vessel must
poured first into wine or burning water, least they should hurt a man by the
means of their over much heat, let them stand for the space of a month or
more if the water be hotter, in moyste sand, in a cold place. Likewise the
third part of the vessel hid in the sand: or two parts of the vessel (two
third parts) let them be digd in the earth in some wine cellar. Brunsvvick.
Every water if it be destilled again, specially upon the dregs and grounds
well pund and broken, it may so be rectified: quintessens must be added.

But ye the brentnes yt is in these, may brethe out it shall be best to keep open
a while the phials wherein the liquors stand: not withstanding you must take
heed that the grace of the smell in the mean season with it much of the
strength and virtue thereof, do fume out. Syluius. Destilled liquors of diverse
sorts if a man mixte them together sometimes they become troubled and pudled
like thin milk or why, and for the most part an old liquor make them clear
and pure again, if into three pounds, you cast six or eight drops of white
vinegar very sharp: for they will drive down the pudly matter to the bottom:
Brunsvvick.

Destillacion by a Filter or a list of Woollen cloth.
DEstillation by a filter is more in ur with the last vessel only and lowest
shall have no lists, which must only receive, not yield forth. The
destillation by a filter is done also in two crooked vessels of glass well
clayed, the one where of must stand higher then the other, when you will draw
precious and swiet smelling liquors from the matter (digested first in Balneo
Mariae,) as Vlstadius makes mention. Chap. 56.

To the sirrup which is made of the sower juice of the citron, that juice must
a man destill by a filter, or elsse it will gather into a courd when it waxes
cold, and likewise ye juice of Lemons and Orenges.

By a list some destill also the decoctions of herbs sod with a soft fire,
which afterward by little and little they sis till it be as thick as honey,
as of the rotes of black Elleborus or bears foot. Other think it sufficient
only to sethe it or strain it.

Of burning water or single Aqua vitae, and of the strength thereof, and
manifold use.
BVrnynge water or Aqua vitae is drawn out of wine, but with us out of the
wine lies only, specially of them that sell it, and by this onli almost get
their living. And parauenture it is never a whit ye worse that it is drawn
out of lies: for Lullus teaches it be destilled often, it shall be made the
more effectual, that is to say, hotter and drier: for dregs also or lies
brent, are very hot, and give the hottest oil, which they call oil de
Tartaro, but that burning water that is destilled out of wine, me thinks it
shall be the swieter, and more pleasant both in smell and taste, and without all
brent taste: besides that more apt also for quintessens. Burning water is
called also water of sapa, of which oil, mingled together soope is made, for
it brenneth ye Monachus in Mesuaem.

Burning water oft destilled, is brought into such sharpnes yt it can not be
drunk. Cardan. But this is commonly known: they that sell it commoly serpentins
or writhen or crouked vessels or in other accustomed vessels (with pipes of
brass as big as a mans arm) may afterward be perfected in ashes, until all
the steam be put away: by the which means it is made not only hoter and more
subtle and finned, but also more pleasant both form or fashion of the
bodies and heads which they call limbeckse, to destill all ^uyces: for some
draw the water out of wine put in a great brazen vessel, standing upon the
fire, the neck or mouth whereof is great and broad, upon a three foot, and
upon this neck stands the head of brass also with a top like a spire
steple, a thing like a pail or bucket. D. compassing it, full of cold water,
that the large vapoure may be thickened the sooner by the head made cold, and
least the water of ye wine Syluius. We did see diverse fashyons of vessels set
out in figures to destill burning water or aqua vitae, in Gualter Ryffius Andro
Lo nicerus. Bulcasis saith wine may be destilled after the same manner, as
vinegar, the destillacion where of he described. Read within, straight after the
title of destillacion in ashes.

Some make a lunge round pipe. A. going upright from the cover of the
Caudron containing the wines or lies, which pipe is larger beneathe after the
fashion of a figure in Geometrye called conus which they bow at the top
almost with a straight corner, and by a bucket full of water not a crouked nor
wri then course: and so they say, shall a man have more plenty of water. Such
like little instruments certain apothecaries do make, and call them bladdars,
for this purpose, that if they need

Aqua vitae is thought then to be stilled enough, that is, after it be stilled
four times at the least, and pourged from all his fleam, when as if a Aqua
vitae so oft destylled should attain, ought too be lighter it should also
become thin and pure, more more, and when every fire, the thicker and grosser
it is, the more it burns: contrariwise, the thinner, the less: that which is
most thin should burn least of all. By the same cause it comes to pass,
that a drop of Aqua vitae poured in the palm of a mans hand, the better it is,
the sooner it vanishes away and is consumed. I hear that this is a laudable
note of this water, if succinum amber being set on fire and put to it into a
spoon do burn. When wine Vlstadius. 10* chapter.) is destilled twyse or thrice,
you shall cut a fine sponge into pieces, so great, that on every side they may
touch the cucur bita or body within the upper part (that is in the inner
circuit) the spog must be tied in three or four strings hanging without, when
you shall set on the limbeck, so that the sponge can not fall into the bottom
of the still: let the sponge be dipped in oil olive, and crushed out a little
again, least pexauenture the oil should fall into the pot or body of the y^ me
of our time call it dead water: for it is unsavoury and stinking. Such, that is
the stinking water, whether it be yeilden of lies only, or also of wine, I
have not tried.

For the destillation of aqua vitae or burning water, a man must choose the
best wine, black, red, or white. Black and old, yields more plenty and also
better water, then any other. The fire at y^ first must be light and soft,
after more vehement: but you must take heed in any wise yt the wine sethe
Albertus as some allege.

Of five parts of lies, when it is twice destilled I hear, one remains or
somewhat les. When y^ fleam is one clean consumed, nothing is lost after in
the destilling, but the hole is yielded again. Of.iiii. measures of wine, for
the most part one measure of burning water is gathered in the first
destillacion or there about according to the goodness for the most part we are
content with the third or the fourth destillacion, and call it perfect. And
surely it rests much in the instrument, that the water should be perfected
sooner or slower. For the vapour is more destilled and perfected in the first
destillacion in a serpentin (that is in a crooked and bowed pipe) the thrice
destilled by a straight pipe. Note further that there diverse waters may be
gathered in the first destillacion, whereof the first is perfect, that is, that
which burns and is consumed Lullus.

Of the strengthes and virtues of Aqua vitae, in the book of Arnold, De Villa
nova, which is written
ARnold in his book of Aqua vitae describes many qualities thereof both of it
alone, and also mixed with other medicines after the destillacion, observing the
order of the.xii. signs, from the head to the fete, for as he saith, a man
must minister much more effectual remedies to the head, if a man have need, at
such time as the moan is in the ram: and likewise in the other, which thing
how true I judge it, I will not say at this time: notwithstanding this will
Arabians have brought into physic. Aqua vitae simple and alone (saith
Arnold out of one Theoricus I can not tel whom) breaks an impostume or
recourse of matter both within the body, if it be drunk, and without, as
botch, if it be anointed therewith. It helps read and duskish eyes. It
stauncheth the running and watering of the eyes. It is good for them that have
the falling sickns if they drink it. It cures the palsy iuila fallen down with
humours, also the salt fleme, the rose drop the touth ache. It is marvelous
profitable for fretik me such as be melacholy. It erpelleth poyso, The smell
thereof burnt, kills flies fishes from roating. It restores wine that is
turned or putrified. It draws forth the virtues of herbs and rotes, if they
be laid in it.iiii. days (otherwise.iiii. hours) except only the Violet. It
ought to be set up in a glasen or silver vessel and to be well stopped. There is
more of it better made of old wine pure and read. This he. He that desires
more concerning the virtues of Aqua vitae, let him read that which we write
above of ye virtues of certain liquors, which our men call gold, Aqua vitae,
as to the other, specially to simple Aqua vitae, much more to compound or Aqua
composita, but chieflye to the quintessens thereof. Read more over Lullus in
the book of waters. I have scene also a certain broken work ascrtbed to
Albertus, of the virtues of Aqua vitae. But practicioners, ignorant of things
and times, or else of a purpose to deceive me, as many as they could, have most
impudently fathered Galen, Hippocrates and Aristotle of the virtues of Aqua
vitae, or burning water, as written by them. All kind of cold passions or
greues, that be curable, it helps in short space, specially diseases of the
brain, sinews and joints. Also worms in the belly, biles and all scabbines,
if it be washed oft therewith, it heals it. It helps ye spleen, it kills
all worms. It medeth a stinking breath. It takes away the disease of the
loins. The hurt members if they be anointed health. It preserves fish and
flesh from corrupting, but before they be eaten, they must be washte with
common water. Camphora put into it, will dissolve. If common water be poured
into it, it goes to the bottom: and likewise oil. This writes Albertus as
some allege.

The taste of it excedeth all other tastes, and the smell all other smells.
Lullus, It comfort the natural heat more then any other remeadye: it is most
holsom for the stomach, the heart and the liver: Lullus. Anoynte the head
therewith and it helps it of the head ache: it kills the worms drunken
fasting: it puts away away diverse kind of touth ache. It puts away the
blemishes and whit spots in the eyes, if it be poured into them: and avoids
the running and watering of the same, if a man do but hold it in his mouth. It
lets the leprosy a space and hides it It helps rotten and materinge
biles, if linen clouts be dipped therein and laid upon them. With a little
decoction of parcely, it drives away the stones in the bladder. It makes women
apt to conceive, but annoys them that be great with Lullus.

I knew an old woman, that was cold and Cathectica, to be restored by a
handkerchief made warm with Aqua vitae set a fire within it.

Some allege the testimony of Constantine, of

Aqua vitae besides other things is commodious and profitable for the strangury
and other diseases of the bladder, for the tertian ague and quartain also which
are joined with cold humours: against the disease called the Wulfe: against
worms: against the sens or painful fealinge of a man body like unto
biles. It furthers and provokes women's flowers. It breaks and puts
away the stone in the reins, and expels a of the belly and fluxes, it
helps, what so ever they be. They say, that Aqua vitae is perfect when often
measures is made one, (which I suppose will come to pass in the third or
fourth destillacion). Annoynt the head therewith, and it heals worms, the
scuruinesse and scabs, pusculs and skailes. It puts away the spots in
the face and other where. Broken eggs or eggs that that lack a shell, put
them in Aqua vitae and it will sis them. It healpeth the numnesse of a
Apoplexia, swellings, steames, tenesmum or desire to the stool and weariness.
It amends the morphew being drunken or anointed theruppon. It makes the
skin soft and pure. To conclude, it is good against the biting of a mad
dog. It will heal any would excellently, if it be washte therewith, and so
that no evil effect shall come thereafter by the means of the same wound.

I perceiving Aqua vitae to be hot and to dry, apomel^, which with us
commonly they make of honey cobs. c. Some wet figs there in set them on fire, then
eat them hot. A ma may also mixed any sirup therewith, one or other, according as
ye disease shall require. To preserve wine y^ it be not troubled nor putrified,

purpose, but it bewrays the craft and the smell there of. The same will alum
do, but being both hurtful, they are very noisome to the health. Cardane.

If wine by the means of the savour of the vessels or taste of the grapes be
corrupt and soured, pour Aqua vitae into it and it will restore it. The same
purges haging wine that is new: also wine that is putrified and soured also:

therein.

Wine that is made to have any taste or smell, out of hand in a moment, is

of wine: and also profitable. For the qualities Aqua vitae for the space
of.xxiiii. hours for so the strength of them shall be drawn out. Then ye waters

Of such things as be destilled dry, put into any liquor.
THe things that be dry can not be destilled, except they be stiept in some
liquor. Of their will I write in this place. Of those that whiles they be new,
be stiept and wet in some liquor before they be destilled, I will speak
hereafter amongst waters Aqua vitae rectified seems to agree best to the
stieping of spices, except we will avoid to much heat, wherewith the things
destilled in them might be infected withal, when the liquor is drawn away.

Some still Cinnamon beaten with spring water poured to it, as we shall show
amongst Oils. Sedes also and other spices, wheroute oil is taken, are stiept
in some of the forsaid liquors. It is best according to my judgment to destill
all these into anything in ashes with a soft fire. Of waters of virtues or
golden waters, that are wont to be made with saudge other smelling herbs
spices, set in wine, I will speak within amongst the copounded waters: of them
that are put in burning water, amongst the composed waters of life. It is to be
considered also how long they stad to soak in wine: for new things, they yt be
thin or open not massy, need les time the dry, gross and thick, beaten things,
the hole: the rotes then the gentian, astrantia, ye flour deluce, y^ beries of
juniper other: I wold more allow ye liquors of drithinges set in wine or other
where: but if ye things be thin or slendar, or of no savor, or ought to could
moist they should be destilled rather new. Sometimes the time of the year
causes a man to take dryethinges because of the lack or scarsity of fresh.

Gentian water. Take.iiii. pounds of the fresh and new rotes or rather dry cut
in small pieces, of great Gentian or white Gentian, (whose rotes are most fat,
and being set in wine, they nether corrupt them selves nor suffer the wine to
corrupt, as I have tried my self by the space of these ii* years) set in a
couple of great glasses in a stoufnie the fornace, or hang them if you will, the
glasses diligently stopped, and put into them pure good wine, so much that the
wine be.ii. fingers above the: you shall ever put new wine unto them still,
cucurbitas, ye wine and the rotes destributed together, destil them in Balneo
Mariae, or else in ashes with a soft fire. Of the water of Centory y^ les and
gentian together destilled in wine, read within amongst composed waters.

Water of Roses with dry Roses is so made. Pour water to dry Roses, not more
the is sufficient to stiep them in: then put them from thence into glasen
cucurbitas or leaden, and destill them by little and little. This water shall
be profitable in medicines, and also to garnishing and trimming, Bulcasis. If
the Roses which we call commonly incarnation Roses, dried moystned with the
vapor of hot water be destilled, they will give good Rose water. Syluius.

The water of the nux vomica or spueinge nut, or the juice gotten out with
fire, is like the water Cardanus in his second buke de subtilitate. And a
little after. If the poison newly taken remain yet in the stomach, the best
kind of remedies be they that provoke much to vomit strogly, milk, lie, oil,
the water of the spewing nut, I suppose he means by the juice gotten out by
the fire, nothing else but the water destilled thereof. For he names water
that is like it in colour, not in smell nor taste, which agrees with destilled
waters. most hard and dry, it appears that his shell must be sookt in some
liquor as water, wine, vinegar, Aqua vitae. I wold soak it rather in vinegar,
which by it self resists poison, and is good to provoke vomitting.

Waters destilled of new and fresh plats (saith Brunsvvick) ought to be
preferred, which if they can not be had, ether for some other cause or because
they be brought out of strange countries only dry, as spicknarde, lavender,
stichae, scoenantum, Balneum Mariae, and rectifye it also in the sun, (that
is in hot sand, for the space of: xl. days) and keep it a year. The what time
of the year so ever thou lakst liquor of dry herbs, do thus. Take as many
herbs as thou list dried in the shadow (the leaves being taken away and kept
apart by the selves) put them in a glass, and pour upon them that first thou
power on so much, that the herbs be sufficiently overcovered with water: and
again twis so much. Then set it in horse dung two or three days, still it,
and putrefy it again, so that it be thrys putrified and destilled by course
in order. This water surely shall be much better, then if a man, as Bulcasis
writes, to one pound of dry roses (or other herbs or flowers) shall put ten
poud of common water and destill it by and by. A certain cunning phisicion
writes that if water be aqua vitae. Hitherto Brunsvvick.

And in the dew it self also there is a certain medicinal virtue as

Of quint essence of remedies.
QVint essence they name to be, the chief and the heauenliest power or virtue
in any plant, me and purity of the hoale substance, not by any elimentall or
sensible quality (although it be not without qualities) conserveth the good
health of mans body, prolongs a mans youth, differs age, and puts away
all manner of diseases. Of this first of al me writ Raimudus Lull{us}, although
it were unknown to al the physicians of his time, nether written of in any
book, nor tried or gone about in any use. After him follows besides other
Iohannes de Rupe scissa, (whom one certain Lullus, as I writ before, I judge
they were both in one time) Hieronimus Brunsvvick, Philippus Vlstadius, and
perauenture a few other which writ somewhat of the same. Sum kind thereof is
simple, as yt most famous quint essence of wine or Aqua vitae, of chelidony or
selandyn, of mans blood, of strawberys, of Antimonii. c. Other are copounde that
is when certain remedies are put to sum quint essence now perfited, yt it may
draw out yt virtues of the, whereunto

But here springs a dout, saith Cardane, whether a man may make ye water
teperat, which they cal quint essence: It will be, as I shall prove, of the nature
of the firmamet, that is a most pure thin substance moveable, which by the
moving retains a temperate heat, very much thereof. This thefor is of power to
coserue al strengths, topro long life. For being most subtle, it mixith it self
with the first moistur, piercing the massy things, separateth And because it
hath much heat, it expels what soever is unclean, and therefore restores
the natural heat. For age is nothing else then a lessening and diminishing of
natural heat, which is therefore diminished, because the mouinge is hindered: as
I said of fire: for there is like reason of this unto that. Moving is hindered,
because of ye abundance of earthy matter, because the earth only hath very
much matter, and is destitute of all moving. Therefore that water being of so
teperate Aqua vitae reteininge his purity and subtle matter: by the lunge
mocyon of circulacio, hath put of and rid away his heat and sharpnes of taste
and smell, it is becomed of the nature of the firmament: and then first of all
it smells swiet and fragantly. For a fragrat smell is nothing else then a sharp
smell when it is moderate. Aqua vitae, if it come once to a temperatnes, the
thinnes and purity remaining still, it must needs be made fragrat and swiet
smelling: and if it be once fragrant, it must necessarilye be turned into the
nature of the firmament. There is also a water made of the flesh tyr{us} or an
adder, that restores youth: there is made olso of Elleborus, which I saw at
my fathers. But these vex and unquiet the body, and firmamentall water doth
it in dede, which retains long that which a man has and adourneth the
same. But is the firmament more pure then the fire which is most hot* It is
surely so: for it is next unto the heaven, and therefore most light. For by
his circulacion it moderateth the heat caused in it by the stars: and after
ye same manner this water, brought to the most puritye by the heat of the
fire, by motion it self is coled again and obteineth a temper. Wherefore both
Stoikes believe mans soul to be.) Al this write Cardane.

The virtue of every thing rests in the quintessens quintessens. Somthings it
is sufficient to destil the few days, if any part of the earth or lies remain
in the boto of the circulating vessel, to put it awai pouring it forth. The
quintessens of wine will no more burn the mouth, nether is it burning wine any
more, but more subtle, and is called heaven: whereunto we say his stars is
added, when as herbs of diverse qualities, and such as be excellent against
diverse sicknesses, are sokte stiept therein, as we shall show hereafter in Aqua
vitae compounded. To be solificate or made gold, is Lullus book of
quintessens.

The quintessens of any medicine has a thousand times the greater virtue,
then it had before, whiles the thing had yet the element in it. Vlsta.

How the quintessens of all things Lullus, intreatinge of quintessene.
THe quintessence of any thing may be drawn out from it, as of wode, fruit,
flowers, rotes, leaves, sedes, stones, metals, flesh, and of what spices a man
will, in this sort.

Any thing that you will separate the quintessence from, you shall put it into
the quintessence of shall set it forth to be solified sonned in ye spring, or
else in a vessel to a light fire, and within three hours you shall have the
quintessence of ye thing mixed with the quintessence of the wine, which shall be
converted into such a nature as is of ye thing that is put in, whether it be
hot or cold, moist or dry, purge or what other condition or smell so ever it be
of: and it shall not only have the same operation, but also in the same degre,
yt is nether more nor les. c. After this he rehearses remedies one quintessens.
Rogerius Bacho also writ a little book of the qualities strengthes of Aqua
vitae, throughout the.xii. signs according to the diseases from the head to
the fete, adding waters and medicines according to the nature of every part and
disease, for one, one, for an other, an other. This book some ascribe to
Arnold de villa nova.

Ihon Brasescus a man of our age most exercysed in the mysteries of the Art
of liquors, as his writings do testify, in a certain dialoge, the spekers
whereof be Raymund Lullus and Demogorgon, (which ye author set forth in
Italian, with an other also upon the exposicion of the books of Geber. Ihon
Petreius at Norinberg Prynted them both in Latin without the authors name)
affirming that quintessens which serves for the conserving and lengthening
of man life, is not to be lokt for out of plants, beasts or precious
Raymund. Seeing it is necessary that this medicine should be utterly
vncorruptible, and in this thing it ought to pass and exceed al things that
have any part of the elements in them: it must needs be drawn out of such a
matter as is most far from all corrupcion, and least subject to the same.
Demogorgo. Yet thou said in ye book which thou writst of the secrets of
nature, yt it should be drawn out of red wine, ye same cofirmeth Iohn de rupa
scissa, other suppose it should be made of Celedonie, other wold take mans blud.
Ray. Thou art not a little deceived, if thou think ye writings of philosophers
ought to be taken vnderstaded according to ye bare letter, chiefly in this
science, ye more clearly plainly they speak, so much ye more darkness have their
writings, for they have spoke by similitudes ridles in ye darkest form of
speech. Demo. In this thing what similitude have they used? Ray. Senior the
philosopher saith this medicine and from taste to taste, and from nature to
nature: and therefore the names thereof are multiplied. Also Minois a
philosopher, if a man ask (says he) why is it made rede before it received
whyt: Answer, that it was twys made black, twys turnid into orrenge colour,
and received twys red colour. Seeing now that it receives twys red colour,
as red wine and mans blood, that is to say, after the putrefaction and in the
destillation: therefore the old men speaking by quintessence now perfect,
which Io. de Rupescissa doth: that is, that al men entering into the house may
be marnei lously allured unto it set in a corner of the house: an other. that
all birds that perceine that swietnes of savour, may flock together unto it
set in the top of a tour: this is expounded allegorically, that by the tour,
the fornace set in a corner of the Rasis also was. Thus saith he.

Of the dravvyng out of the quint essence from wine, out of Vlstadius and
Raymund Lullus.
THou shalt take the best wine thou can get, what colour soever it be, let it
be wyny rather then swiet, destill it.iiii. times in a lembeck, so as commonly
burning water is made. If so be it, it be ofter destilled, it shall be the
better. To know when it is sufficiently destilled, you shall consider, side
one, which also some call the vessel of Mercurius, by the hoal whereof in the
top, the matter that is to be circulated, may be put in and out. And when the
quint essence. Then the wine so destilled as we have said, when as it hath all
the four elemets, yet by the oft moving agitacton and going up and down, it
passes and is turned from corruptible into almost vncorruptible. If so be it
that by the often ascention and descention of destillation, the is separated,
how much more doth it come to pas in a still where it is tossed up down a
thousand thousand times. By this moving therefore, it is to be supposed, that an
elemental matier is turned in to a substance void of all elements and a body
incorruptible, so that this quint assence is of so much the further from the
corruption of a body that hath part of the elements in it, as the heaven it
self is more vncorruptible the the four elemets. But when this circulation is
many times done, quint essence. For if it be perfectly done, there will issue
forth a most fragrant and pleasant smell, so excellent that they that feel it,
shall think them selue to be rapt from the earth into heaven, and to have the
fruicion of a certain heavenly savour. This vapour if it chance to insinuate
it self into some close place

If it be set in the top of a tour, it will allure all kind of birds unto
it, that be there about. But if that excellent grace of savour appear not
yet, then thou shalt shit it close again in to the Pelecane, when it is
diligently clayed, fet it in again to the circulating destillation, until the
perfect quint essence it self appear, or else as Raymundus in his first book
the second chapter, calls it quick Mercury. And it hath not only that most
excellent savour and taste, but also a certain vncorruptible nature to be
occupied about other medicines.

And surely it hath no brentnes in the mouth as aqua vitae, nether any moisture
or fleam, because all the earthy elemental matter is settled down to the
bottom.

How qvint essence may be dravven


PVtt Horse dung A in great deep vessel B or in a pit made for this
purpose: and in midst of the dung set C full until two third parts of the
matter to be destilled, so that the third part remain without the dung
empty, that the matter may ascend descend, and so to be turned into clear
water. But the dung must be renewed once at the least every wieke. The same
after they be pressed in the wine harvest: or else in the dog days with the
sun. It is possible also to draw quintessens out of troubled, unclean and
corrupt wine, if it be not sower or like Vinegar: For we see even of corrupt
wine, if it grew in a good place, although it be troubled and of an ill
taste, yet very good Aqua vitae may be destylled therout.

There is also an other way to draw it out without fire and labour. Take Aqua
vitae the best with a lunge neck, in the top whereof must be a hole, why the
ought to be closed and stopped with wax prepared accordingly (as Vlstadius
teaches after in the teeth chapter.) Then let it be turned upside down, and
with the neck let it be put into the dung, so the grosser matter shall settle
and lie in the bottom of the that is, the very quintessente, stop the whole
with thy finger turn the glass upright. So hast thou quintessence, but les
precious then tother before. In like manner may burning water be put in a
glasen vessel, not turned, but buried upright in dung for a certain space.
After by streinynge may you separate the groser matter which is settled to the
bottom, from the pure that is above: but this is yet les precyus.
Notwithstanding it draws out all the virtues of the herbs, and other

Of the extracting and dravvyng forth of all the virtues of Chelidonia or
selandine: by the which example, every man of any understanding, may use to
draw out the virtues
BY the quint essence of selandin, Ioan. Brasescus thinks somewhat else to be
allegorically vnderstaded, as I declared before where we entreated of quint
essence generally.

Chelidonia, Seladin (saith Vlst.) hath innumerable virtues: and the quint
essence thereof, which we will teach here to get out, goes cucurbitam or body
of a still of earth glased: when the body is full shit it close, and clay it
round about, then set it in now horse dung, for the space of iii. weeks. After
put it in a limbeck and destill it in Balneo Mariae, with a slow fire, and the
fleam shall avoid out of it. Then shall you draw out the dregs, and stone,
put the again in a cucurbita with a blind lymbeck, and let them stand in
Balneo Mariae a seuennight, or in horse dung mo days. Afterward the matter by
little and little being couled put on a nosed limbeck, and destill it in ashes,
according as in the.x. chap. of the separation of oil from the earth, we have
spoken: and there shall issue out a clear water containing in it self air
and water. Thou shalt separate the water from the air in a new cucurbita by
Balneo Mariae, with an easy fire: Balneo Mariae seven days at the last thou
halt destill the in sand with a great fire, and the fleam shall issue out
first, then a radish water, or rather an oil, which is the element of fire,
from the which thou shalt separate the Balneo Mariae, as is before said. But
the dregs that left, which conteyne nothing else but earth, must be urged
with a strong fire and brought unto lyme, by the space of ten days. (that is
in a fornace of calcination or reverberation, or a very lyme kiln, as I have
taught in the tenth chap.) Then grind them again upon a marble stoone and sooke
them in the fleam, and let them be destilled in a limbeck, until you see in
the matter little white stones like salt. And out of which you have destilled
it, and after, you shall destill it again, and again so oft, until the earth
chance and put away from it all unclean and earthy colour, be brought to a
very white (to the whytnes of wax) and so it shall be rectified earth. The other
elements also ought to be rectified, so that every one be destilled seven
times, pouring every time the fleam to the air and fire, and after separating
them as is before said. When as thou wilt do this an easier way, dissolve every
quintessence, but it must be presupposed that every element be first justified.
Then let it be put in a vessel of circulation in horse dung or in Balneo Mariae.
xxx. days, and then destill it again. So shall the very body as a gros matter
be changed into spirit or most subtle and pure substance. quintessence. The
matter is judged to be sufficiently circulated, when the quintessence swims
above the other matter.

Of the virtues of every one of the liquors of Selandine. The element of the
water is good for al the diseases of the body, both hot cold. It tempers all
ve nom from the hart: it cures al the diseases that chance unto the lunges.
It purges the blood, and preserves a man from all corruption of the natural
strength and power. At once, it is good for all sick men in what disease so
ever they be.

The element of air, like unto oil, confirms and increases the strength and
beauty of young persons, if they use it sometimes with meat, for it lets the
blood fro corrupting by any means.

The elemet of fire, if so much as a wheat corn in quantity be mixed with the
best wine ye can get and poured into a sick mans mouth, yea if he be half
dead, it restores and refreshes al the stregthes of the body: for it pierces
unto the hart, and makes it warm: and expels all poisons and moist
superfluities from the hart. Lullus with the quint essence of wine mixes a
little drop of this oil, to restore the that are about to die, and past al
hope, in that within the.xx. part of an hour.

Some draw out the quint essence fro Selandine an other way and shorter. They
cut Selandine together with the rote and flour in small pieces they wey it,
pouring well water upon it, they sethe it till it be brought to the same weighte.
Then they pun it in a stone mortar, and when the juice is streined out through
a linen clothe and purged it be ad consistenciam mell is as thick as honey.
After, they put it in a cucurbita so that it be half full, by destillacion in
Balneo Mariae, they gather ye water or fieme. Then translating the vessel into
ashes, they receive the aerie oil, whereupon when they se an other kind of oil
swim above (the fire being encresed) they set under an other receiving vessel,
wherein the element of fire is gathered. Every one of these liquors must be
rectified, that it may be mete for the medicines of mans body, that Balneo
Mariae, so yt at every time the cucurbita be diligently washed, (made clean)
from the dregs which remain, which ought to be mixed with the element of
thearth, which remains in the bottom of the cucurbita after ye destillation
of ye fiery liquor. Likewise thou shalt rectify thair, destilling it in
ashes.vii. times, mixting the dregs with thearth. Afterward ye liquor of fire
likewise. The earthy matter, in such manner Selandine puts away al heats
poisons out of y^ breast. It is good for the stoppinges of the liver and lunges:
for it consumes ye superfiuous humours fleme: Tie conclude, it delivers a ma
within the space of.ix. days, free fro all infirmities. choler, no bitter, nor
fleme in ye body to get ye moisture. It encreseth blud, destributeth it into
all the parts of the body by his piercing. Wherefore they that use this oil,
do let blud the ofter. If a ma be in ieoperdy ^f losing of an eye, let him drop
in a drop or.ii. thereof every day by the space of xxx. dais, it shall do him
merueilus much good. The firye liquor is much more effectuous then the watery
or aerie, helps where they fail. It conserveth the youth, it makes age
lively and elipir of life. Moreover ye earthy matter rectified by dissolucios,
coagulations ielyings: calinatios sub till salt of ye earth, wherewith al metals
may be turned into stone, al spirits may be fixed: having radicable natural
moisture. It nourishes lepre me. Of this ye aunciet philosophers made a stone
which they called ye philosophers stone. The manner to receive y^ for said
liquors within the body is Seladine, iii. sponeful of rosewater, put to it a
little sponeful of y^ sanguine air (y^ is the liquor of the air) give it to be
drunken fasting, if the disease be hot, with wine: and if the man be
past.xxiiii. years of age give it him with Aqua vite. In hot agues it ought not
to be taken in no wise. Al this writes Vlstadius.

Of quint essence of mans blood, eggs, flesh, and honey.
HOwe quint essence is gotten out of mas blood, eggs, and flesh, read
Vlstadi' the xiiii. chap. They put unto them the tenth part of salt, wherewith
they are well mingled, putrified and destilled and that four times, length they
are perfited by long circulation, until they come to the most swietnes of
savour, and pure fynnes of substance: Lullus also in his first book the.iiii.
chap. mencioneth of quint essence, but the printed books left out that, that
salt must be added unto it. It seems that salt may ryghtly be added to the
destilling of moyste things, specially those that would easily corrupt,
such as chiefly the parts of beasts are.

Io. Bracescus, is of this opinion that the old wryters would signify
allegorically some other thing, that of metal, when they speak of mans blood:
as I recited before, where I write of quint essence generally. Vlstadius in
the.x. chap. of his teaches how quint essence of wine is made, every element
drawn out apart by him self: And this (saith he) which is destilled in the
seventh time, is called man blood, which the destillers, chieflye search,
and it is very air. This saith he. In deed the liquor of the air, which
in the most part of destilled things is oylye, seems to be called by the
name of mans blood, for as much as our body consists of four humours as
elements, whereof the blud is compared Ihon Brasescus, mans blud is a certain
metally thing, so called of the color.

Of quint essence of metals.
IHon Brasesc{us} in the dialog of Raimund and Demogorgon, when he had affyrmed
that quint essence which is profitable to the preservation and lengethening of
man life, can not be had of any other thing the of metals only, he adds
at length. When as according to the opinion of the ancient philosophers, every
metal after their simi^itude, in every metal, as it is plainly declared, in
the book of the expositio of Gebrus books: therefore this our medicin also
although it be extracted and drawn out of some one metal only, yet nevertheless
it shall have the virtue of al metals and plants, and the virtue over the hole
body of man to heal many infirmities that be curable. Ioan de Rupescissa
speaking of our radical and natural moisture, and of quint essence under the
name of Aqua vitae, would signify the same, for he Brasescus, wherein he
expounds Gebrus.

Of the drawing out of quint essence from Antimonia, lead, white lead out of
Vlstadius.
QVint essence of antimonio is thus made. Incorporate and mingle the powder of
antimonii most finely beaten, with most sharpe vinegar destilled, and let it
stand till the vinegar be turned into a very red colour. Then drive the
vinegar out, and in a clean vessel put other destilled vinegar upon the
antimonium, and set over a little fire till the vinegar be coloured* This shall
you repeat so oft, till the vinegar will be coloured thou shalt destill it in a
limbecke in ashes. First the vinegar it self will run out, after this thou
shalt see a matter issue forth of a thousand couloures, and this is that
quint essence which is called of the Philosophers. Philosophers lead, and of
some virgins milk (it differs notwithstanding from it, whereof shall be
spoken here after, saith Vlstadius) it is almost like blessed oil in
colour. Put this in a Pelican to be circulated for the space of forty days.
It dries

Yea also quint essence is extracted and drawn out of lead or white lead
after the same manner as out of antimonio, pouringe destilled vinegar upon it,
that the vinegar be over it four fyngers deep: after let it be digested in
dung, as it is said of quint essence of herbs and flowers. quint essence of
lead: and it has in it a certain swietnesse like* as the oil of antimonii.
It is good against all burnynges with fire and hot water, as also against
itches, as ringewormes and choleric bladders.

But a manne must noote, that the white lead ought first to be washed often
with water until none of the powder of the white lead remain in it. Then when it
is dried, reserve it to your use. So doth Bulcasis Ioan of Sanct Amandus upon
the Andidotarie of Io. Mesuae, will to be done.

Of diverse kinds of Aqua composed.
VVAters of life composed are proprelye called, when as certain medicines are
stieped in the very Aqua vitae and destilled together, improperly when as they
are put to after not destilled together. There be also waters of virtue or
golden (as they cal the) when as the medicines stieptfirst not in Aqua vitae but
in wine, but of which we will write hereafter: those that we describe here:
saving that they are les vehement and les hot and dry.

Sum sorts of aqua vitae are commanded to be made simpely, putting the
medicines into y^ aqua vitae: other into aqua vitae thrys or four times
destilled. Sum there be also to whom a certain time of infusion or digestio
is prescribed, as. xiiii days. c. Sum are put in punned in the beginning*
other hoal, and broken after a certain days fermentacion and stieping. Sum are
destilled on Balneo Mariae: other in Balneo Mariae once ortwys thirdly in
ashes, as that which Vlsta. describes xlvi. chap. although there is nothing
else done but infusio in wine. In the same author.xliiii. chap. Aqua vitae is
described to be destilled thrys, the first water whereof is called Blessed, the
second Aqua vitae, the third, the mother of balm. Again the destillatio of
certain is repeated twys or thrys, that the water first destilled, be always
powered Vlstad. teaches in the.xlix. and.lvi. chap. Also sum are commanded to
be drawn out with a slow fire in Balneo Mariae. Other in the same with a
strong fire, as the the water described in Vlstadius. xlvi. chap. Many times in
the first destillacion, only fresh herbs, and fresh seeds are put with the
aqua vitae: and dry swiet smelling things, spices and other, also honey is
added after to the water drawn out of the herbs or seeds, and then are
camphora put unto it, thirdly, folding the limbeck every foot with moist
clothes with the whytes of eggs and bran together. Read Vlstad. li. chap.
and.lvi. Concerning the matter, none almost is composed without spices: unto
diverse also swiet smelling herbs are added: unto some, both the flowers and
the siedes, sumtymes also other destilled waters: Also malmsey, honey, sugar,
figes. To conclude, unto some hoale pieces of gold: I suppose it to be added in
vain except it be queshed Vlstadius described.xiiii. kinds of aqua vitae
composed, which we let pas because of brevity shortness. We read in Vlsta.
the.liii. chap. of aqua vitae, with spices and honey, c to be destilled in
Balneo Mariae, and an other likewise, but without honey, the.liiii. chap.

Vlstadius describes a certain marvelous aqua vitae in the.lvi. chap. First
he bids to digest diverse fresh and new medicines, in Balneo Mariae. xiii. aqua
vitae powered unto them: then to be destilled in ashes with a slow fire. After
he adds spices and other dry things: and bids digest the xiiii. days: and
destill them again in Balneo Mariae. Afterward he added camphoram, rhabarb,
safron, amber and mosch. If so be it (saith he) thou wilt have the water yet
better, put unto it certain precious and costly powders or spices of
confections made of amber, made of mosen, made of precious stones. c. and a
few ducats of the finest digest it three days in warm water of Balneo
Mariae, afterward destill it by a filtrum or list of woollen cloth in crooked
glases well clayed, so that the one glass stand higher then the other. And this
(saith he) is the most excellent Aqua vitae amongst all other.

gold, (which will be never a whit the aqua vitae, four times destilled. Use
this water.iiii. times destilled, morning and evening before slepee. It drives
away divers kinds of diseases, and restores youth.

Aqua vite against pestilence, proved and used with great and marvelous success
by a certain physician of our time of Solodurn in Heluetia the year of our

TAke the best perles, Hyacinct of the east, mother one.ii. ounces, half an

the falling of one drop, till the falling of an other, thou maiste tell one.ii.
iii.iiii. till thou come at ten. For thou must in any wise, take heed that the

water that is drawn out of them in again, and destill them again with a slow
fire as before. When this destillacion is once ended, pour out all the dregs,
cucurbits, into.ii. cucurbits, of.iii. great ale quarts a piece, and destill
them in Balneo Marie gentlelye. So hast thou a treasure and an incomparable
remedy against the pestilence. The use thereof is both to preserve, and also
to cure provoking a sweet after the drinking thereof. So soon as any manne

sick is risen, let him eat sodden Barlye, or a little meal of Oats broiled,

with his meat also for the space of a sennyghte. For his drink he shall take
a decoction of Barlye with raisons and Licoris, when it is well couled, let him
dryncke as much thereof as he list. Let him abstein from wine.iii. or.iiii.
days: after let sambucus, Eldar, or Acetosa. And if so authrax or bubo do rise
as it chances often times, then thou shalt use these medicines. Cut an Onion
on the one side, make it hollow and put triacle into it, tormentil, Dictamni
diligently broken and punde, in equal parts, and put the cap that you cut
of the Onion on again, fold it in moist tow, then roast it under the ashes
by the space of.vi. hours, then punne it in a mortar, and wrynge it through a
lynnen clothe, putting

then lay the moist cloth to the place, and as oft as it dries, moysten it
again. If a man drink, once a month, half an uncle of this liquor and sweat
upon it, he shall be preserved. It is very good also, if a man hold a drop or
two thereof every day in his mouth in the morning.

An aqua vitae or quint essence, whose effect is ready Matthaeolus Senensis, in
his sixth book of his commentaries upon Dioscorides. A pound of our antidotu
now described, (the description whereof we let pas for briefnes sake: a man may
use good triacle in the place of it, or Mithridatu, or an other effectual
antidotum) and a pound of syrup of the barks of citron, mixed them with five
pounds of aqua vitae so oft destilled, till it cum to quint essence, and put
cucurbita of glass as big again as the things do occupy, (that is of.xiiii.
pounds,) and when it is well clayed move it moderately softly so long till the
antidotus be holly resolved mixed with the liquor. So let it stand a month,
moving and chafing it in like manner twys a wieke. The month being ended power
out softly by little and little the clear water which is ascended above the
antidotus which is sattled in the bottom, into an other vessel of glass, and
reseruith very well closed. proved by innumerable tryales, that if half an
uncle thereof be drunken with wine, or with any convenient water destilled, or else
alone, it restores a man infected with the bytyng or stinging of any beast
althoug he have lost both his voice and his senses, and calls him again to
the great wondering of all mem. For the most part also the humours now
infected are avoided by vomit. The same virtue hath it against poisons which a
ma hath received in meats or drinks. For ye strength Mattheolus.

Aqua vitae for the diseases of the colike. Take a great ale quart of aqua
vitae rectified, put therein half an uncle of cinnamon.ii. or.iii. nutmegs,
cloves. Andro Furnerius.

Aqua vitae devised by George Alapide. Take cinnamon cubebarum, ginger,
nutmegs, cloves, galangal, of every one an ounce, fresh sage.iiii. vnces,
wyn that is sublimated six times, made of the best of the wine, not of the
lies, as much in weight as all the rest, that is.x. vnces. Take so as a
filberd nut will hold. They say that M. Gallus the physician of the Emperor
Charles used this, and lived. Cxxiiii. years.

with a slow fire in Balneo Marie thrice always pouring the water drawn forth
upon y^ cardiaca: also against poyso gout of the hands or fete, gutta,
arthritis. It purges blud, it takes away al agues of cold causes: the

the swelling and watery dropsy and all diseases bred of cold causes. To conclude
it is a most chosen remedy for the coseruacion of ye body. They give men to
drink there of half a philberd full in a cup of pure white wine.

IN the composed waters of life following, although Raymundus do not express
whether wine or burning water ought to be put to the medicines, that they my be
destilled together, yet we have thought good to rehers them in this place,
because Raimundus seems utterly to will these medicines to be destilled in
burning water, the in wine, as one that rusci, maidenheare, sperage, Rapes,
parsellye, eryngii, mill of the sun, scariolae, of every one like much mixed
them and destill them with a slow fire. (It appears that this liquor is good
to provoke urine, and against the stone.) An other.

What medicines be mixed with Aqua vitae, without any destillation, first
within the body, then without.
MAny times instruments, time or cost fails a man, that those medicines,
whose strength he wold have in his aqua vitae (as though it were by a certain
metempsy chosin, y^ is a transposinge of the souls or principal virtues) he
can not mixed them with it by destilation, which only way is in those bodies
that are called met^ora, that is things bred on hy in the firmamet, moreover
in many other things mixed ether by nature or by art, is easy to be
understanded. But for so much as in so diverse states of men, sum for one
hindrance, sum for an other, they can not always follow that which is best,
if quint essence can not be made, at the least the second or the third, or as
many as may be: let the destillacions be repeated with a slow fire. for any
mixture is done better by little aqua vitae, the medicines whose strength he
desires to mixed with it: yet at the least wise let them be broken and stiept a
while in it: for it draws unto it the virtues of all things that are put in
it. There is a book of Arnoldes de villa nova, or rather of Rogerius, which I
have written, where in is declared particularly, to what diseases and
sicknesses, what medicines ought to be put to sooke aqua vitae, for every part
of the body, which he doth attribute to the twelve signs of ye zodiak.

It is well known in many ages hitherto, that gentian is the best preservative
against certain most grievous diseases and poisons: but sum use to power the
powder of the same with burning water, as much more effectual, into the
throotes of beasts, whom they know or think to be hurt with poison in
their meet or drink, or else by biting or stinging: and if gentian can not be
gotten, certain sicknesses of man, specially of the bulk or breast apomeli may
be put. for both certain men cout it otherwise for dainty, to have apomeli mixed
with burning water, also a toost of breed mixed therewith, many take for a
breekfast. A ma may also against diverse sicknessess, give men to drink the
water of gentia artificially destilled with wyn, mixed after with apomeli or
swiet hypocras.

In the disease called the French pockes, sometimes the jaws and throat are
eaten with evil destillacios, which onles a man find remeadye for,
oftentimes the gargil is wasted, oftentimes the corruption passes to the very
bones. Therefore a man must purge them, and turn them aside. c. There is a
marvelous water made for Aqua vitae and vinegar in a limbeck a most clear
water issues out of many virtues, but chiefly it helps where the strength of
the medicine ought to be conducted sowhat diep, if therefore thou dissolve in
it bole armoniak or sphragida, and touche the parts that be freten, thou shalt
both kill the cause of that contagion, heal al the freting, Fracastorius, in
his.iii. book of contagions.

Of destilled vvaters composed, but with other, then with Aqua vitae.
SVche waters as are destilled of two or mooe medicines mixte together, I call
them composed. Of this sort some are used for medicine, some for smelling,
some are invented for garnishinge, trymmynge and deckynge. There be some that
will do two of these or all. Notwithstanding we will refer every one to one
kind, that is, wherein it excells most. Again, of them that be used in
medicine, some are received into the body, other some are minystred only
without other some both ways. We will make only two Chapters, for all
though some be used both ways, yet they are moore used other within and
without. Again, of them that be received into the body, some are moore
simple, Aqua vitae composed, where the spices are sooked in pure and only Aqua
vitae, we have written severally by them selves. Hear will we put the other
(as I said,) and those which they call waters of caponum, and one with certain
medicines put into swines blood.

To be sooked in wine and after to be destylled, it seems to be ordained,
chieflye for those Melissa called Balm, Wormewode. c. which more over by that
means, do retain more firmly their own savour, the wine drawing it and
drinking it up, that it canne not dispersed for the thinness, we shall speak
hear of certain things to be stieped in wine, but while they be new, for
we have entreated of.iiii. dry things stieped in wine other liquor, and so
destylled.

Betany, commonly called betany, and in dutch eerenbrey, is stiept in wine a
few days, likewise melissa. They shall have the same virtues strengthes, but
more effectually and more subtle, then the herbs by them selves alone.

Melissa, that is citraria (saith Lullus) let it be put in wine to be
destilled. Let a man drink a sponefull of this wine fasting. It sharpneth the
understanding and the wit, increases the memory. To a man that stutteth* lay a
linen cloth wet in this wine upon his tongue and he shall speak right, It is
most profitable against scabbednes cumming of could. It sharpens ye sight of
him that drinks it. It takes away duskishnes, tears of and superfluous
humours of the eyes. It is wholesome for the breast, profitable for cocoction
against evil humours that lets it. Drunken with good wine, it stireth the
appetyt. It puts away spots and frakenes of the face that is washed therewith,
if so be it a little baulm be put to it then the face washed tnerwith, it makes
also good colour. It Lazarus from increasing. It is good also for them whose
brain is perished, and for the frantik. Lullus. Melissa beaten is stieptin wine
a night. Men say this water destilled and drunk every day and holden in the
mouth, it cures the benumbing of the senses: all so the falling sickness, the
dropsy, the quartain ague, and other diverse diseases of black choler or fleume.
It is ministered also to the strangleng of the womb, and toth ache, Remaclus.
F.

destil a most effecual water therout.

WAters of virtues, which the Germans name golden, all are destilled with

in these waters composed) put first a few days into wine: they are in more

whereunto rain water must be sprinkled in a bottle.

blerednes both it a while in his noos.xvi. It helps the diseases of the
teeth.xvii. It cures the maladies of ye hart and breast when it is drier or
moyster then it should be, or is sick with the cough or short fetching of
breath. c.xviii. It increases the memory, and takes away forgetfulness in a
man, that receives oftentimes by times, no, but one drop thereof.xix.

A water of Swalowes is thus made. Yonge Swalowes when they are beaten to
powder, mixed them with Castorio, and a little good vinegar and destill them this
water drunk of one fasting, is a true medicine of the falling sickness, what
cause so ever it cum of. And although a man have had that disease.v. years, he
shall be heeled, if he drink Lullius and Aegidius.

VVaters of Capons.
WAters of Capons, as men call them, are

A water of a capon restorative and somewhat bynding. Thou shalt sis the capon
in water according unto the art, put to it a pound of rosewater: conserve of
roses borage, buglos, of every one an uncle. Of both kinds of coral: of ether
of diarhodon abb. a dram. Small rasins without y^ curnels an uncle: Coriander
prepared half an uncle: the fregmentes of all (precious stones) pearls, of
every one an uncle and a half. Let them be destilled.

An other. Let the Capon be sod with a pound of bief till it be sufficiently
sod: whereunto thou shalt ad Malmsey, Roose water, of ether half a pound:
white bread, that it may be sookte.iii. ounces: thou shalt beat these without
the bief, and put to these spices following Spices electu. of precious Diarhodon
Abbatis. Diamoschu that is swiet, of every a dram. Diamargariton, spices of
confection against the Pestilence, of ether two scrup.viii. leaves of pure
gold. Mixte them all with the juice or broth and let them be destylled with a
slow fire.

Some dres this water with saffron and cinnamon. c. for women in child bed,
which be both weak, and their flours or looseness of their body comes not
forward. It is ministered ether alone

Some also dres it without destillacion thus. They sethe very long an old
capon boiled dressed accordingly: then they beat small the guts and the bones, and
in a tinnen or glasen vessel well stopped set in a caudron full of sethinge water,
they sis it for the space of.vi. hours. Some cast gold into it, as rings or
coins of gold.

cosuming fever: not to be destiled, but sode only in a glase vessel, put in a
ketle of water.
