A water composed, the first amongst Aegidius waters.
HIssop, penny royal, Charyo. Cikory, of every one a dra: let them be pud
destilled. After take tutiae, persly of Alexan. Rue, Zedoaria, aloes, the stone
called calaminaris, of every one a dram, when they are pund, sis the in ye
forsaith water till y^ third part be wasted, the liquor strained with a cloth,
thou shalt kiep it in a glass diligently closed nyne days (otherwise.xl.)
Afterward let it be given in drink every day in the morning before day (other
wise, by the space of.x. days) to the sick fasting. It is proffitable against
the falling sickness if he that takes it continue fasting after it six hours.
And truly it is a most effectual remedy. It heeleth al resolution of the
sinews, and the members are Castorium, it is good against all goutes, which
hath not taken rout yet in the members. If it be drunk ix. days together
fasting, it puts away all manner of agues, what matter soever it cum of (if it
be drunk.ix. days every morning early. This water is also most profitable to
wash woudes in which the sinues are cut.)

An other of Aegidius, the.ix. in number, otherwise they call it double. The
siede of Apii, the sied Apii, ginger (otherwise, The sied of Apii, white Popy,
sugar, Carioph.) of every one equal weght. Pund in a mortar, put to it
conseruatiue water, (that is destilled of persely) and destill it. This is the
chiefest remedy for the cough and breast ill at eas. If a man drink it could
fasting, and in the evening as hoot as he can. If it be drunken hoot with
Castorio, it is good against the disease called Apoplexia, it heals also the
mebers sick of the Palsy, if so be the Palsy be not it chereth all the
members, it drives away il humours, and strengthens the heed and the brain.

An other, the six in number amongst Aegidius waters. Gladiolus Hyssop, Sauin,
Sothernwood, (otherwys the sied of Sothernwood, leaving out Sauin, I like it
not) of every one like much, beat them together, and let it stand a certain
days, the destill it. This water is of greet strength. It with stands all
agues both hoot and cold. It provokes women's flowers if it be drunk thrys, but
it is Castorio, it heeleth the palsy (if it be drunk daily very hoot)
within.iii. days. The same descriptio find I in the book of Lullus of waters.

Of pur ging medicines, composed destilled.
THey also are to be called composed waters, that wine, burning water or other
liquor. Certain copositions of spices to restore the strengtes of the hart and
the spirits, are mixed with waters of capons, dressed by destillations as I said
before: also with burning waters, or rather Quint essence of wine, against the
pestilence and poisons, as we declared before. But also purging medicines,
Electuaria chiefly in the which Dacry dium and other vehement things hurtful
to the stomach are received, mixed with the liquors, specially with burning
Lullius also praises greetly, and certain practicioners of any acquaintance
have used it with praise. I know Hamech, that which is of ye juice of Roses,
and gave to drink unto the weeker sort the liquor that he received by it
self: to them that were stronger, he myxt sum of the elctuary with it: and so
he said he purged sick men without any grief.

With Helleborum is a water made, that restores youth: such one saw I my
father have. But such waters vex the bodies, and make a fallible image of
youth. Cardanus.

Gold Potable or that may be droken.
OF potable gold, who so list he may read much in the book of Vlstadius which
he names the heaven of philosophers: in the book of Lullus of quint essence.
That there is virtue in gold, which comes of it made hot and quenched in
water, that may be an argument, that the water wherein a wedge of iron or
gold is slekt, is commended of Nicander Aconitum: for it seems to be
understanded of water, wherein these mettalles should be quenched, when as he
names none other liquor. Quench (saith he) red hat iron or the dros of iron,
or red hot gold or silver dip it in a troubled potion or drink. Where the
expositer saith: Quensh iron in water and drink it, and a little after: quench
the dros of iron in honey drink the intinction, so calling the liquor wherein
any any thing is quenched. Dioscorides bids to quech Auicenna also hath, and
Aegineta, and also Aetius, who saith that a miln stone so slekt is holsom, and
that the wine should be drunk hot) with these words: and the dros of iron,
or iron it self or gold, or silver red hot queched in wine, if yt liquor be
drunk. And trueli it seems that wine is more apt to receive ye virtue of gold,
then water. When as I on a time tasted water, wherein gold was often quenched,
I could perceive no quality of the savor or the taste to be altered in it.
Again, it brought unto quint essece doth draw more strength of the gold the
wine: the more if the gold be beaten into most thin plaits, most of all if it
be beat into powder. But the oil that coms of gold shall pas al these. As for
gold simplely sod, as in ye broth of capos, there is no strength in it all,
except a mas opinion cado any thing, as I believe withal learned men for the
most part. Of the virtues of gold, rede Auicenna in his second book. 78. cha.
But because ye purest is to be chose for medicines, I will Plinies words out of
his. 33. book a bout thend of the.iiii. chap. of ye purging of gould. Let gold
be roasted broiled with thrice as much in weight of the clots or lups of salt:
and again with.ii. procions of salt one of the stonecalled schiston: so it
yields his strength to the things burnt with it in an earthen vessel, it self
remaining pure uncorrupted. I conjecture yt Plini in this place did mistake
schistu the stone, for schistu an alu: for in an other place (the. 35. book.
15. cha.) he writes schistum, yea and the reason taken of the virtues, makes
more for Alum: for he saith gold is purged with salt only and schistu^ put unto
it: but Alum has more like effect unto salt then the stone Schistos,
whereunto the old writers ascribe none other virtue, but yt which it hath comun
with the Haematit (of which kind it is) that is to stop blood. But Alum is taken
and used in the purging of metals: also Aqua Forti (as they call it.)
Notwithstanding Plini may be excused, because the word stoone is more common
and of larger signification with him for he names both quick silver and many
other metally things, stones, wherefore he might call the Alum Schiston by the
name of Schiston. Albeit he should not have so done, for the difference of
that which is proprely called a stone Schistus. In the same place of Plini,
after the words now rehearsed is put: The rest of the ashes (that is to say, of
the salt, with the which being burnt together, ye gold Schistum) kept in an
earthen pot, and tempered with water, anointed upon the face, it heals the
disease beginning in the chin called Lichenes, or like foul breakings out:
which shall be conveniently washed away with Bean meal. It heals also
Fistulaes, and they that be called Hemorods. If so be it when it is beaten
Spuma be put to it, it amends corrupt and stinking biles and sores. Decocted
with honey and Melanthio, and anointed M. Varro saith it heals wartes. Here
in the first words, the ashes kept and anointed no man can dout, but he
speaks of ashes: but that which follows of decoction and anointing, seem to
be long to the gould it self. But in my indgemente, ought to be red so that
those words decocted and anointed be referred unto the ashes, as both the
consequency of the text, also the manner of the medicines do require. For salt is
used of phisicios (Diascordes other) against Lichenes, ringworms itches, against
al manner of vucums, red inflamacions, tetters: and broiled or parched with
honey, against running cankers. c. The men of our country rub children warts
with salt and soute. And alum, as Plini saith, asswageth rotten sores and
biles, with fat: the fretting cankers of biles with vinegar or burnt with as
much in weight of gals, with.ii. parts of salt (in which manner of proportion
also it is mixed for to purge gold) the diseases that sprede abroad. Moreover by
the name of Spuma which signifies some. Plini vnderstadeth Halosachne, as also
the. 31. of ye. 7. I find nothing noted by Hermolans or Gelenius upon this
place. But as gold is purged with alum, so is also Misy, as the same Plini
witnesses. 34. 12. bi an other means, with quick silver, rede Plini. 33. 6. That
it may be purged, it is sod with lead: the same. 33. 3. An other way to pourge
the same, Cardanus describes in his.vi. book of subtilty.

Potable gould. Take the honey combs of a ambra grisea, Sperma ceti. Agallochu,
log pepper, Cariophyllos nuce moschata, sauders pure gold. Let these stad in
horse dog. 30. dais. After destil the in a limbek in ye bath: then grind yt
matter yt is remaining upon a stone, pour again upon it y^ water destilled,
destill it again in ashes: This water dissolveth gold. If the potable gold be
hardened, take of it ye bigness of a pees, put it into an egg hard roasted, ye
yelk take out: so shall it be resolved: give

The chymistes make a liquor of massy gould, which drink, as they say
cheers the body. George Agricola.

It likes wise men, that to eat meat dressed in vessels of gould, or with the
decoctio whereof plaites of gould were adjoined: and to drink wine wherein
plaites or money of gold were quenched oftentimes, procures unto the hart good
state, hath great force. Arnold of conserving youth.

In the Quint essence of wine, gould, silver, peer Philip. Vlstad. the.ix.
chapter, and furthermore: where as who so list shall reed more of potable
gould.

Wine wherein plates of gould have bien quenshed.xl. or.l. times, is used of
certain in the steed of potable gould. Arnold de villa nova.

The same in his book of wyn: Wine that hath ponderosity and weight thereof to
incline the superfluities to the parts of expulsion, and to conserve youth. It
conserveth the virtues of the principal parts in his actions, and by his
temperatnes it louseth the urine restreined. It heeleth the falling sickness and
them that have lost their senses, it is wholesome also for lepers. Many at this
time rich men and princes will have certain parcels of gould to be sod with
their dishes* other use the in Panellis with electuaries, other in pouders:
Diacameron the fyling hoth of gould and of silver is used. Sum are wont to hold
a piece of gold in their mouth and to swalowe the spetle. It is plain that
silver kept in a mans mouth quenshed the thirst: and corrall comfort the
stomach, both holden in the mouth and hagged about the neck, so that it hang
toward the stomach: for I have tried that it lets the troubling thereof.
Other convert gould into a water that may be drunk, which way without doubt
dede and natural, not to chymisticall gould.

Elixir vitae which a certain friend of late communicated unto me by his letters
with this words The descriptio of this medicin was sent unto me from Rome,

Certain vvaters composed against the diseases of the eyes.
A Marvelous water to conserve ye sight, and against the blemish or spot of
the Lullius.

A water for all the diseases of the eyes that be curable, out of Aegidius and
Lullius, we have described it before amongst the waters composed for diverse
inward diseases.

A water composed for the eyes. About the beginning of May gather Selandyn,
Veruin, Few, Fenell, pun them severally, and take.iii. vnces of the juice of
every one of them, then mixed them, put to a little of the grien branches (as
the French men call them the Pampes) of Roses.iii. vnces of sugar candy.iiii.
vnces of the best Tutia, and as much of Dragons blood. When all this are pund
thou shalt mixed them together and destill them in alymbeck of glass. The liquor
that runs forth, thou shalt let stand.ii. or.iii. days in a receiver, then
use it. It is of great virtue for eyes that be ill at ease, red, or have the

Certain vvaters for the eyes out of Rogerius.
Fill a stilful of the leaves of Agrimony, Memitha, Leuisticus cut: sprinkle
upon it a little white and clear wine, and destyll it in claied vessels. This
liquor represses the swelling of the eye lids of a cold cause: it dries up
the blearednes: it stops the flowing of tears: it clears the sight:
breaks bleamishes or spots (I suppose he means corns or Pearls.) If
thou wilt have it stronger to break spots or perls, ad unto it Gallitricum,
and Morsum Gallinae (anagallis)

A man may get a water out of Fenell also for the same causes. For a liquor
gathered of ye rotes and leaves of Fenell sod in water, with a basin laid upon
the water while it yet sietheth, is kept in a phiall, and one drop is put in
the corner of ye eye every dai morning and evening for the forsaid causes, by
commun experience.

To break the spot or perle, mixed with the forsaid waters, myrhe, and Aloes
pund: put a drop of the liquor streined in ether corner of the eye

A water destilled of the flowers of white thorn and willow, puts away
prickinges, heates or rednes of the eyes: it stops tears coming of a hot
cause, and breaks the spots or pearls of the same cause.

A water of the leaves (flours) of Eufragia stops tears coming of a cold
cause, and makes slender the eye lids that swell of the same cause: it
breaks spots or pearls of the same cause, and Enphrag did not heate, but were
temperate, or else doth coule moderately in the first degree, and dries in the
second.

An excellent water for the debility of the sight, described by Gordonius. Take
Selandin, Fennel, Rue, water withy of the mountain, Eufrage, Veruin, red Roses
chosen, of every one a half pound, lib. s. Cloves, Lunge Pepper, of ether two
ounces. When they are bruised together destill them in a it every day in the
eyes.

An other of the same mans for Fistulaes, which it is certain, it will heal.
Two pound of good white wine destilled in the same vessel that Aqua vitae is:
the water of Rosemarye, Sage, of every one.v. pounds: Sugar.ii. pound: when
they are destilled again, put to them an ounce of Sage, and as much of
Rosemary. When they are stiepte together eyghte days, thou shalt strain it
and use it.

A water for the Cancar in what part of the body so ever it be. The herb
called Cancar, which is also called Dove foot, the flowers of Quinces, the
flowers of Cerifolium, the bows or leaves of the Breer Idaea (which the
frenchmen cal Frambosia) and a few white Roses, honey and white wine, and the
Alum which the French men name of glass. Let all these be destilled
together: Andreas Furnerius.

A water of a Moldwarpe, c. for all kind of Gutta or drop, noli me tangere,

hear are rehearsed. We will refer amongst the trimming waters also, those
waters wherewith whelkes and little Pushes or Biles in the face, are made hoale.

Of vvaters of svviet savour.
Diverse waters are made for the onli delectation of smell, to sprinckle upon

their bed clothes: whereunto it communicates the pleasantness of ^auour, not
only by sprinkling, but also when it is hot by the vapoure. Roose water also
comes in ur to sauces of meates: and only it, as I think of all

The Powder of the Flower Deluce, mixte with hot water, makes it to smell,
and is used of Barbers. The Flowers of Lauendula or Lavender, and much rather
of that which is communly called Spick, both grien and dry are put into
water, or wyn, or burning water in a vessel well stopped set in the sun, that they
may infect it with their smell. But if they be yet fresh moist, they turn the
wyn almost into vynegar, which if they be dry they do not so. The liquor
shall be made the more smelling if the flowers be dried in the sun in a glass
closed, and afterward whyt wine be put to it. If so be it a man desyer to have
a swiet Caphura, Agallochu, or meaner things as Assadubis, Styrap, and Stacte,
Myrh, or any other spices, chiefly cloves: or else things of les Arentii, Bay
leaves, commun swiet herbs, Rosemary, Amaracus, Basil. c. they shall be made
much the swieter. Saffron is to strong, and stuffs the heed.

Rosevvater with musk, Saffron, Cloves, Caphura out of Bulcasis.
WIth Musk. Put a croun of good Musk, beten in two poudes of Rosewater, in the
little, then put it in a glass well stopped. It is a water marvelous swiet, and
convenient for a king, that their clothes may be sprinkled therewith.

VVaters destilled called Cosmeticall,
DEstylled waters for garnishinge, are divided also into certain differences,
for some are for the face, to make the colour of it white, ruddy, bryghte, to
put away wrinkles, to preserve and keep it from Sun burning, or to abolish
the spots and and to the chaunginge of the colour in them. Other make the
ties white.

Al the use of Cosmetical and garnishing things, ought not to be taken for
dishonest and indecent for a man that is well instituted and godly mineded, for
Galen also the most famous of all phisicions, prescribes cosmeticall
medicines not a few in his worcke of composition according to places: and he
declares that the use of the is many times profitable and honest. For the
lewdness Galen of composi medic. sec. locos, the first book. 2. ch.

Such Cosmetical waters as be simple, we will not declare hear, as be the
waters of Bean flowers, Strawberies, Dew, Gotes milk: Read before and other in
Brunsvvick or Riffius. Yea Aqua vitae hath a certain cosmeticall and garnishing
virtue, as we rehearsed in his place.

Waters destilled of the leues of the peeche tree and Willow, of lyk weight
mixed together, do heal the red whelkes in the face, (the French men call them
rubiz) being moistened therewith.

A spyced wine for the garnishing of women, which makes the skin whyt, fine,
pure, and well coloured. Put Ginger and Cinnamon in wyn and Arnold in his book of
wine.

A garnishing water, wherewith women amend their thick gross skyn, black and
skaly or scurvy, with Quick silver sod in a raw eg, c. thou may find in
Nicolas Massa upon the french disease, in his.vi. book.ii. chapter.

A water for the bewtifying of the face. The spume of silver half a pound:
when it is beaten Caphura, Aphronitrum, oil of Tar taro, cloven Alum, of every
one an an uncle. strein it through a cors cloth, anoint the face and neck.
Epiphanius Empericus.

A french water for little moules, all scalynes freckmes of the face. A pound

A water procuring unto the face a Rooselyke and fair colour. Take a pynt of
Aqua vitae thrys Prasiliu: Cloves to the number often, as many grains of
Paradys: five Cubebas when they are all pund sifted, heet them a little with
Aqua vitae in a vessel diligently covered, that nothing breeth out by any
means. Afterward when this mixtur is could again destill it in a lebeck of
glass, with a very slow fire, and thou shalt have a good water a clear. When
thou wilt use it, wiet the face skyn of the person with a sponge moistened
therewith, for it makes without all this dying will continue a long time,
for.ii. or. iii years. If thou can not have Aqua vitae, take reed wine of
Rupella the best thou can find, about the measure of Semiloti, a dutche
wourd for half an uncle, for they call an uncle a loot, for there must be more
measure of wine, then Aqua vitae. But Aqua vitae is much better to the
preparing of this water. This water garnishes a mans skin subtly
marvelously. Out of the written book, the author whereof is not declared. A like
unto Gordonius. An other that takes away the wrinkles spots of the face, and
clarifies the skin, of whytes of eggs destilled: Reed before amongst the
simple waters destilled in Balneo Mariae.

A marvelous water that puts away Napas (little whelkes or pushes or little
teetes, sum call them Napas, I think the Italians) Lupinas acorns, kurnelles,
Porros, that is, wartes, what euel so ever grows in any place of the body it
takes Arabic, clear Tur pintyn, of every one.iii. drams: When they are beten
mixed altogether and destil them in a lembeck. And in this water thus destilled
put half a pound of Cineris terrae destil it again: and kiep this water as a
tresure. The author is nameles. But it seems that this liquor will be rather an
oyll then a water, save only that ashes is added unto it, it migth have bien
well ascribed unto Balms.

A washinge or Kings water which abolysheth all spots. Take water of clear
Turpintine, as much as can be gotten out of.ii. pounds of it: put therein
half an ounce of Masticke, Tragacantha. When they are pund mixte them with the
water, and destyll them, keep the water. Then mealte Swines grease of a male
Hog, vnsaulted, and strain it through a double lynnen cloth. Then take white
Ginger, Cloves, Cinnamon, Euphorbium, Spiknarde, Camphora, of every one two
ounces, three Nutmegs, when all these are pund, myxt with the strained Swines
grease: put unto these two ounces of quycke (la feuille ou de lauenre, as it is
written in French) of quick silver (de couppelle.) When all is mixte
together, destill them, and keep the water. Afterward take vi. ounces of the
water of the forsaid Turpentin, and of the latter water twice so much, mixed
them: and when you will use them wash the face first with a decoction of
Cerussa and wipe it.

Then pour about.vi. drops of this water mixed together into the palm of the
hand, and where thou wilt anoint, covering the place anointed or wet with a
linen cloth till it be dried. A marvelous effect and operation shall follow
thereupon.
