VVaters for the dying of
SPonsa solis beeten (otherwise the siedes of Solsosium beeten) put it in milk
of a woman that nurceth a boy, ten (otherwise.xl.) days, and then make an oil.
This oyll sod with leued gold, seething it gentely by the space of one day, is
marvelous: for if a man it shall be plain and clear, that it shall seem
angellike continuing for the space of.v. days. It clears the sight also,
and cures any disease of ye eyes within ten (otherwise. iiii) days: and al
kind of tothache within.iii. days: and if the jaws be well rubbed with it,
the worms fall out and die. Aegidius, amongst whose waters this is ye fourth.
If a man drink of this water.ix. days, he is healed of the Palsy, what cause
so ever it come of, although it have endured the space of.iiii. years. Lullius
in his book of waters. It appears that this water is not made by destillacion
but by expression, that is, wringing out only, as I shall declare amongst oil
of sedes.

A water destilled of lard, that the hears may be made long and yellow and
shining, the face more elegant. Scrape lard as much as ye will and shave it
very small: then beat it in a marble mortar, till it be like paste knoden. Of
this destylled in a limbeck, thou shalt gather a white liquor

A water destilled of honey makes the hears fair and lunge. Read here after
where we shall speak of simple quint essences: and amongst waters that be
destilled in Rosestilles.

A whitening water. c. of a Mouldwarpe: the vi. amongst Aegidius waters. Bring
a Moule into powder with brimstone and the juice of Selandine put to it, let it
stand a certain days, afterward any beast what so ever it be and it shall
be made white. If thou mixte water, otherwise (the word water is left out)
aloes and wax, annoynt the place diseased and thou shalt heal al manner of
Gutta: and as they call it nolime tangere, if thou lay a plaster made thereof
upon the soore. Likewise it heals the skalles of the head anointed
therewith, and cures the guttam rosaceam laid upon it in manner of a
plaster. But mixed with the stone called Calaminaris and Aloes, it heals
Lupum perfitelye, laying a plaster of it thervpo twice a day, if also the
superfluities be washed with the same mixture. It must in no wise be taken
inwardly within the body.

A water that dies a Griene colour. A poud of cuperoos (that is to say
Vitriolum.) Half a poud of Smerillum. Destil them and anoint Epiphanius
Empericus. The water of Capparorum, Capers destilled, makes grene hears.
Cardanus.

A water commodious for purging the teeth. Ammoniak, Salt Gemmae of every one
three ounces: Sugar Alum an ounce and a half. Let them be destilled, or
soked.viii. days in two pound of water, and strained, rub and wash the
teeth therewith. Epiphanius Empericus. Other.ii. like, for the same purpose
shalt thou find after, next to the descripcion of Aqua fortis.

Howe waters of herbs, flowers, and rotes be destilled by descencion, that is
downward.

A vvaye to destill svviete vvaters, and effectual, out of Flowers and
Herbs by descencion or going downward.
TAke an earthen vessel, upon the which strain or spread a thin and fine
linen cloth and upon it sprynckle Rooses, (for so the Roosecake will prove
marvelous swiet) or coueslops, or other Flowers or Herbs. Then must ye have
Cardanus.

Fresh Rooses laid upon a linen cloth strained upon a Basin, if they take a
vessel full of hoate coals, they destyll much water and swiet Syluius. In my
mind this kind of destillacion is commodious for all such things as be
cold, or ought to coule, chieflye if they lack smell, as the most part of
binding things: more also, such as be cold moist to. Yea also we shall have
much water in shorter time, with les cost by this way, nether is there any
ieoperdy that they should fume out. But a man must give diligent heed least
the vessel laid upon be to little hot, least the water taste of burning.

If.ii. vrinals be set together, the upper full of Roses and set in the sun
(with a linen clothe betwixt,) a most swiet water destilleth into the
nether. Syluius.

Roose water, Moschata, how it is made by the sun by descencion, we prescribed
before in ye Chapter of swiet waters.

The liquor of yelow violet flowers, that destilleth by it self into a vial of
glass, amends the eye Alexander Benedictus.

Take the tender buds of Fennel before they flourish or go abroad, full of
juice with the leaves. Put these in a Phiall of glass, but fill it not up:
turn it up side down and put the mouth of it into

Scillae whiles they be fresh and new, the utter a vessel full of hooles in
the bottom, covered above well closed with clay. The bottom of this pot is put
into another, put under it in a pit of the earth, and the joining of the pots
is compassed with clay cloos. Then make they a fire about the upper part of the
pot by the space of one night (.x. hours or more. So the water runs in to
the nether pot, which mixed with meel or breed, it kills mys quickly that
taste of it: the sooner if thou mixed a little Litharge or whyt Lead. Bulcasis in
his Syluius out of him.

Sumtimes certain waters and oils are made by descencion, like as of Roseny
tries when they are burnt pitch. Syluius. But of Oils which are made by
descention we will speeke hereafter. Certain are made by a middle way between a
dissention and ascention, as Oyll of Coperos, by a Cucurbita laid along
overthwart upon the side.

Destillation in Ashes, or Sand
IN Ashes are both waters and oils destilled. Waters with a most gentle
soft heet, which thou shalt discern by touching with thy hand both the ashes
and also the vessels of destillacion. And perauenture there is no difference,
for the destillacion of any kind of waters, in Balneo Mariae or in Ashes, if
so be it thou observe the measure of ye fire. For Balneo Mariae, the water that
ye stillatory is put in, may be scalding hoot: the ashes may not be to hoot,
that is when herbs, roots, flowers or any liquors are destilled to derive a
water of the. Men think them to be made so much the swieter les smelling of
any fyrines and brenning, being destilled in such ashes because the thing is
done more softly and with more leysur, so that the heed or lid be not hoot,
and sumtymes between the falling of the drops, a ma may tel swiftly till he cum
at fifty. But the Oils have nied of a gretter fire Quintessence.

The destillacion by ashes is easy and ready, if an earthen or brazen vessel,
diep enough, and able to receive the Cucurbita or body of the still, be filled
with fine ashes and sifted or fined sand, so full of every side is sufficient
to be one foot long, the height six fingars or there about. All this shall
we strengthen every where with clay (and pieces of shells mixed together) that
the heet may the better be kept in.ii. breething hooles left in the corners.
But this manner is for upon a soden and only miet for one vessel. I bielded once
a more laborious fornace on this sort. In a corner of the house I raised a
foundation of brick and lyme, a foot and a halfhy. Upon the foundacio or
harth,

This round place above the plait was filled with ashes, in ye ashes round about
was there set about five stills of glass, that at one time and with one fire
many waters or Oils might be destilled together. Round about the fornace was
well fensed with wrought clay with very salt water, wherewith sum Horse
dounge was myxt.

This kind of fornace is surely very comodious, because it niedeth little fire,
which also it self in this form endures very long: that it is almost
enough to see to the fire morning and evening. But it must be made only of
cools, which are put in to the fornace with a little fire shovel, only that
they may be gotten in at the little door hansumly: the edges of this fire shouel
must be a little turned up, that it may hold the cools ye better. There is nied
also of long iron fyershouel (as the fornace shall require) turned in at the end
according to a streigth corner, and there in the end to be somewhat made brother,
wherewith the cools within may be Gentian, Juniper beries, and Aqua vitae is
sumtimes repeated, in which I have observed this, that the better part always
the purest issues out first, what so ever is flegmatyck (or deed water, as sum
cal it, which is vnproffitable, hurtful, and almost stinking) follows last:
which is well perceived and known in a glass heed for the still: For while the
purer matter, that is, the fyry and aerie substance issues, no fleume comes
once, and tokens of the water following by straikes appear manifestly, as ye
see them, remove the receiving vessel, and what so ever remains in the
Cucurbita or belly of the still, pour it out as good for nothing: and begin a
new destillation of fresh and that must be repeated, until no fleume appear any
more, which chaunseth lightly in the fourth or fifth destillation. Afterward
there needs no more destillation, but circulacion, that the liquor be
rectified, as they term quint essence.

Things destilled in the vapour of hot water, retain more perfectly the virtues
and qualities of of plants, but because they have some corruption in them, they
can not be kept long. Those things longar which by the heat of ashes or dros
of metal beaten to powder are destilled in vessels of stone or glass, how many
so ever they be, upon a long or round fornace covered with slates or tiles
which may hold the hie sands compassed with Syluius.

Vlstadius prescribes a certain destillation in ashes with so soft a fire,
that thou maiste number one.ii. iii. between one drop and an other.

Any kind of fruit thou wilt, when it is well ripe cut small and beaten,
thou shalt destill it in sand with a slow fire. Vlstadius.

A certain way to make water by and by.

WAter is made also vppn a sudden, whiles the juice is made hot or scalding, a
glasen cup is set upon it, in the which the fume passes into drops: and the
drops gathered thereupon are driven together into water. By this means
Vinegar is easily turned into water. This is wholesome for spots and
blemishes in the eyes, and for the web, to put them away: specially if Rue be
sod in white Vinegar. Cardanus.

Some are put resinam larigenam in a trene vessel, whose bottom must be made
by a tornar as thin as is possible, so that a man may see through it, so in a
hot place the purer part and the thinner destilleth.

Of rosaries, that is to say instruments wherewith a destilled liquor may be
gotten out of Roses and other medicines, fire being put under (for the most
part without any
FIre in destillacion doth more procure the savour of adustion and brentnesse
unto the water that is to be destilled, then do cools or water, which thing
to avoid put sand in the pan in commun Rosaries: or rather see that sand be
betwixt the fire and the pan, and also about the pan, and that pan: for
it is made also of brass. The joining together of the pan and the lembecke
thou shalt fense with clay, that is to say with a lynnen clout perieted or
closed with clay and hansumlye laid about it: and the receiving vessel with
the nose of the lembecke likewise. Brunsvvick. Some now a days make these
Rosarie pans of bras, and upon the sand they lay the herbs that be to be
destilled: and round about the pan (and within it also except I be deceived)
they fill a void space Balneo Mariae, or in ashes.

Vinegar is made white with destillation, as well for other uses of Chymistes
and Phisycions, as also to be mixte with Sirrupe of Vinnegar, Bulcasis words.
Build an Atanor like unto that wherein Roosewater is destylled, and ever take
a stillatorye vessel of glass or of earth glased: fill three parts of it
with good Vinegar, that the fourth part of the Vessel above may be empty,
least when it doothe seethe the Vinegar be spilt and run over. Then cover
the vessel with some Vessel upon it, as ye know, having a noose, as the
manner is in rosewater, and make a the Vinegar shall not become so white. The
vinegar that is destilled must be of white wine, and very sharpe. So shalt
thou gather a white and pure liquor, which thou shalt keep in store. After y^
same manner may wine be destilled. This writes Bulcasis. I destilled it once in
glasen vessels in ashes, which I keep yet hitherto certain years, like to
Vinegar not destilled both in savour and taste, differing only in the colour
and substance, where in it is like to pure water to see Bulcasis seems to be
of this opinion, that vinegar should be destilled with fire or water, not in a
Balneo Mariae, but in that manner that he makes the first and second in
destillinge of Rooses, that is, without water with fire of wood or rather of
cools. I have destilled very excellently in ashes. Pearls, eg shells, stone
of the rains and bladder are dissolved with strong vinegar, specially when it is
destilled or with the juice of limos: so is also both kind of corals, and they
afterward praecipitatum and sublimatum and cinabrium, and they return into
quick silver. Syluius. The Chymistes say metals are resolved with vinegar
destilled: also with mans urine destilled.

The leaves of hemp turned into meal, because they pearse the head, make
drink which will make a man pleasantly drounke. Cardan. It seems water
should be poured to it, and when they are made dow together, then to be
Rosestil, or like instrument where the fire is put under without any men, and
perauentur the destillacion should be twys or thrys repeated. I wold say the
same of Ootes water, whereof likewise Cardanus writes: The Moscouites (saith
he) because they lack wyn, use water destilled of Ootes which enflames and
makes a man drunken no les then wine. For Ootes being of a more gros substance,
it must niedes when it is destilled make hoot, becum more subtle and sharp,
and draweny

The like may be done of any drink that makes drunken, as Ale, Bier, Curmus
and Meed, specially when it is old, and that which is sod with Mill.

Honey while it is a destilling is wont to rise up and run over, when it is made
hoot. But this is avoided, when a man destilleth in a comun Rosestill, by
putting upon it within the pan a wooden sieve (made with horse hear) so that it
touch the honey. Cucurbita or body of glass, mixed with it pure and well washed
sand, make a slow fire. The first water is cast away, but the second is kept
which hath a golden colour, and a little before the end reed. The use of this
water is for women to their hear, that they may grow, be soft and yelow being
moystened therewith specially in the sun. It amends the shedding of the hear.
It heeleth swelled and blered eyes, and puts away their watery cootes and
their duskishnes: and full of wheales. It heeleth notably the places that be
burned, specially them that be soft and ten der, so that no skar or blemish
shall remain. The latter water that is redish, purges corrupt mattier in roten
boils, if they be washed therewith, and lynnen clothes moystened therein be
laid upon them, and when it hath purged them, it raises the flesh also.
Ryffius. Reed Vlstadins..

Of oils destilled, and first generally then particularly.
Of oils of Plants, Flowers, Herbs, Gums, Rolines, Siedes, Barks, Woods.
Of those oils composed which they call Balsama.
Of oils of Beasts.
Of oils of Metalles.
Oils which are made in Chymisticall vessels ether by descencion or by
ascencion, sum be simple, sum compound: of this sum are composed of many, as
certain

Also certain are made of plates or their parts, Flowers, Routes, Siedes,
Barks, Rosines, Gums, Drops. Sum of beestes, or of sum parts things or such
like as Antimonium, Lead, Am ber. We will speeke of every of them by this three
latter places. Of other kinds of oils which are made by any other manner, as
by expression, infusion. c. we shall entreat after.

Like as oil Benedict is destilled by sublimacio of hoot burning till stones
quenshed in oyll, even so of Ladanu, wax, liquors, gums, rosins, boones,
marowes, and other abouding with fat humours may oil be gotten by sublimating.
Syluius.

An oily nature seems to be in al mixed things or copounded by nature
whatsoever they be, when as it is in salt also the most dry body that may be, as
Cardanus writes. (There is also in salt a fatness, which we may maruaill at,
Plin.) but in sum more, in sum les. For it is an oily and fat humour that is in
mixed things, not that watery and ea^y to be dried, but an aerie which also is
hoot. Boeth kind of elements have the moisture in the that belongs to their
kind. This liquor in certain Griekes call them opus: teeres, gumes, rosins,
Elaeomel: but also in deed bodies, as in fat metally things, brimston, quick
silver, also in diverse kinds of pitch, Naphtha, aumber, ambra, P^r^l^on,
which hath his name because it flows runnethout of stones. But most
manifestly in beests, both by other parts of the, also chiely ye fat marow,
blud in the yt have no blud, sum fat humour of ye natur of blud. in the massy
parts both of beestes and plants, in all such chiefly siemeth it to be
which have thriedes or veins manifestly, by the which they draw
nourishemet. For unto nourishment swiet things are most of all ordained,
which by a moderat heet are turned easily into a fatness, ye y^ very fat
things them self are somewhat swiet. And the abundance of fat juice is tried
found most in those things which are nourished with many swiet things.
Notwithstanding fat things Gnaiacum because it sinks in water, abounds
therewith. Thinner and lighter woods convert and turn the fat juice in to Gum
or Rosyn, wherefore they have les plenty thereof: such as be Gnaiacum, the
walnut try, the Ash.

Moreover the very ashes show that Oyll is in all woods: for the ashes of
every one have their fatness, which it leeueth in lie. So durable is the aerie
fatness and yet more the fyery, as when earth is burned, the water goes out in
vapours, the other remain, part in the ashes, part in the Sout: although in
both those also sum substance of earth remains. But this things perteine to
Philosophers.

How oil must be dravvn out of spices, as Cloves, Nutmegs, Saffron, Mace,
and other.
MOysting mesurably in Aqua vitae rectified circulated, any kind of spice what
ye will beaten and broken somewhat gross, and when they are stiept together, at
the last destil them with a very slow fire. And when as the Aqua vitae once
drawn out clean, the oil begins to still, the cucutbita and put it in a
little bag well knit and tied with a thread: in a pres for ye purpose pres it out,
both ye iron plates of the pres well made hot first. The oil pressed out so
must ye destil, rectify and circulate: that the pure oil may be separated
from the grosser matter. The dregs afterward may be digested or putrified again
with the Aqua vitae first separated from them (and be destilled again.) And
this waisemeth to be the most commodious and most Ryffius: but we have
traslated it as well as we might, somewhat darker then it should be.

Howe oil is drawn out of vvodes and other like, as Cloves by destillacion
out of Cardan.
THe things beaten are put in a vessel of glass A. are destilled with as easy
a fire as may water put round about the Vessel, it shall be much the
better. A head of glass. B, is set upon the top of the vessel, and is
cloosed with clay least it brethe out, whereunto a pipe. C. is adjoined as
the fashion is: to yt an other pipe of glass D. is joined, so compassed with
clay that nothing can breethe out. This pipe passes through a trene
Vessel. E. which ye must have always full of very cold water, drawing out by
the spigotte so much water as hath gotten any heat and putting in other water
again late and new drawn D. is made coule, and the fumes are not brent, but
pass into water or into oil, and being turned by that crooked part of the
pipe which stretches beyond the vessel with water, fall dropping into the
vessel.

Therefore as I did once see it, at the first a troubled matter runs out,
caryinge forth the more subtle substance of the thing: after that a more
clear water comes out, at the last oil, which declares the savour
rather of a thing burnt, then of that thing wheroute it ran. In this manner
the destillacion of waters also (of herbs and flowers) may be done: but that
way is far better which is by descencion downward c. Some put Musk about.
F. and other precious smelling things, and the water that rettineth the
smells not only of the own proper matter, but also of the Musk and of other
things that be put to it.

Of destillation by descencion or going downward. Vlstadius in his.xix.
Chapter teaches of this way of destillinge vniuersallye: and specially or
severally of oils of Juniper sticks, of the yelkes of Eggs, Nutmegs, and
Benedicto: where through I will go about to declare the matter moore
clearly and more shortly. He teaches to make a fornace meet for this
destillacion, like to such a Chunney as is used to be made amongst the
Germans, of brickes vnbrente of a Cubit and a halfhie, on every

This fornace shall be meet also for an other use, that is to seal with the
seal of Hermes, as they call it: when the mouth of a Glasen Cucurbita being
put into the whole of the fornace, the length a hand breadth, that is.iiii.
fingers, and if any more about it be open, that being stopped with clay, as soon
as it is red hot, it is nipte together with a pair of tongues likewise red
hot. When thou will destyll therefore in this fornace, take a Cucurbita of
the best earth: or if not, of Coper or Latin: syll it unto the third part
with the matter that is to be destilled, and shit it with a very thin iron
lid of iron plate full of little Cucurbita of glass (for the putting in and
taking out whereof, and that thou may see when the whole matter is
destilled, the foore part of the fornace must Cucurbita must be joined, (but
not with clay.) Then let a fire be made above about the vessel that coteineth
the matter on every side. But the fire at the first must be little, and as
far of from the vessel as is possible: afterward by little and little to
make it bigger and nearer the vessel.

Ye must understand that of every matter, at the first water destilleth,
moore or less according to the difference of the matter.

When it hath left running, and now the oyll hath begun to destill, thou shalt
empty the Cucurbita of glass that stands vnderneth, and set it under aagain:
and continue in encreesing the fire, and to make it ever nearer and nearer the
vessel, until no more oil destille. For then by and by the fire must be
removed far of that the upper Cucurbita may be couled. Then take away the
nether that is the receiver, and keep the oyll. But as I said, a little fire is
required at the first, and is to be encreesed Cucurbita, burn it, breeke it, or
infect the oyll with sum vnplesaunt Benedict, thou shalt increase the fire only
at the end of the destillacion: and thou may prepare such a place for the
destillacion: Dig a pit in the earth, long, brood and diep, accordining to the
measure of the pot that is to be put into it. Let the pot be glased, washed
with water, and upon the mouth of it let a thin iron plate be put, bored
through with many holes, so that the plate be a fingar thickness within the brim
of the pot: upon this set an other the mouths of them be joined together, and
all well clayed, then let a fire be made about the upper pot, which shall be
hool without the pit save ye mouth. But this second way is not so comodious as
the first: because the water can not be separated from the oil: nor easily
know when the hooll matter is destilled, except it be known by use and
custom. The fornace may also be made in a stiep hill: as the same Vlstadius
shows: I let that pas that I be not to long.

Oil of the leeues of Citrum, or Orange, Arantia, or Limons when it is
destilled, first the water is gotten out (then the oyll) and both ought to be
kept severally: the oyll is very swiet and good. It is made on this wise. Take
the leeues of any of the foresaid tries which so ever ye will, when they are cut
beet them in a mortar, and as ye destill them receive the water: and when ye
see any drop of diverse colour swym upon the water, the receiver being changed,
encrees the fire and gather Ambra, hoot Styrax, Ladanum, Cinnamon, Benzoum: not
the substance of them, but the fume or smoke only: for they put this into
Rose water upon the fire, and with the vapour of them they season the leues.
When this are so diligently certain spices are stiept, as Cloves, Galangal, Put
megges. c. for the space of one day, and the powder of the leues moistened
therewith, they destill it it ashes with a slow fire, till the watery liquor be
separated: then they encrees the fire and receive the oyll, which is of a
wonderful pleasant and swiet savour. Furner.

A certain practicioner showed me, that the oyll is separated comodiously from
herbs routes in this manner. Put the herbs or routes in Turpentyn Larigna,
rosin washed (eigth or nyne times) till it wax white, and let it stand in hoot
sand q. s. Then destill them with a slow fire, the Turpintyn shall run out
first* by and by at the beginning: or if it move but a little, being shaken
together it shall then run out hoolly, first in a colour white as milk,
afterward yelowe. Thou shalt perceive by the taste when the liquor that thou
desire begins to run out: when the colour is changed, thou shall change
also the receiver, that

Such destilled oils as be to be ministered with in the body, al must be mixed
with sum liquor, wyn Meed, or Syrup with sum destilled water, or other
medicine: As we shall declare also hereafter among the balsameles. And this is
it oils as unto spirits or souls, sum body ought to be added.

Of oils of flowers.
OYll of Saffron is prepared thus: Thy matter is digested by sprinckling Aqua
vitae upon it, and when the Aqua vitae is drawn out by destillacion, the other
liquor is wrong out with a pres, as it is written Ryffius.

Oil of Spick, out of the French book of Furnerius. Set the herb (the flowers
rather) of Spik or Lauendar a while in the sun, then draw out the water in a
lebeck. This set in the sun in summer in a very hoot place brings forth an
oil in the superficial or upper part of it, which being separated now and
then fro the water, thou shalt reserve. For it both smells most swietly, and
is

An other way of the same mans. Thou shalt stiep in a glass the flowers of Spick
well rypened in as much as thou think good of Oyll of swiet Almondes, in
horse dong.xl. days. Then destill them with a slow fire: at last rectify them
in the sun, the vessel diligently stopped. But if there be any smell of adustion
or brentnes, make.ii. or.iii. little hooles in the cover. So shall it remain
purged

The third way of the same man. Fill a glass with the flowers of Spike
well ripened, shit it, and every day put as much to it as ye can. Do this
continually for the space of.viii. days: then stop the vessel and putrifye it
in horse donge iii. months. At the length destill it a lembek with great
diligence, and when the oil is drawn out set it in the sun, so that thou
seperat always that is clearer and reserve it Some as soon as the matter is
drawn forth from the fire (so is it in Furnerius.

The same oil Ryffius saith is made, as oil of Roosemary, the flowers being
cut and stiept in old wine, then destilled, so that the spirits of the
lembecke be couled. c. Read before in the oil of Roosemary. Some sell this
oil of Spike commonly and name it Balm, with tables wherein the virtues of it
are described. It is very hot and dry. I knew a woman which received into her
jeopardy: but therewith she avoided many worms. It is mixte with many
things, chieflye for the smells sake. One drop of it chafed with a great
quantitye of water, makes it all swiete smelling. Physicians also mixte it
with Oils and hot oyntmentes, both because of the savour, that it may
increase the pleasantness of things that be somewhat swiet of them selves,
and to conserve them, or else to hide and cloak an evil smell: for it
ouercometh all smells, and perauenture

Oil of Roosemarye that is in stied of Balm. Take a Phiall full of the
flowers of Roosemary: bury it in sand (shit with a double cerecloth, or
with wax and a cover so that it may brethe, Atnold: with a double linen, and
then also with wax to be closed, Hollerius:) till the middle of the vessel:
and let it stand so a month or more, until it heats them for the most
part, and heals them sometimes. It resists salsfleem. Fistulaes and
Cancars that give not place to other medicines, it heals them thoroughly.
Aqua vitae destilled of wine, wherein Rosemary is decocted and sod, doth the
same things. Lullius. I would not sethe the Roosemary: for the strength of the
wine and quintessence doth fume out in vapours: but I would stepe them in a
vessel closed, or putrefy them: then first in Balneo Mariae, afterward in
Arnold de villa nova, which in his book of wine, (where he writes of the
oil also of Rosemarye the very same things, which we rehearsed now out of
Lullius, if so be they be Lullius words.) Of tentimes (saith he) have I tried
that Aqua vitae, made of wine wherein Rosmary hath been mollified, cures
salsfleum, scabs, cankar, and the fistula, which cannot be healed otherwise.
But this Verbascum not by destillatio.

Oil of Rosemarye should be made of flowers cropped of with the tops of the
tender buds or bows wherein they are sprung, pund and stiepte or putrified in
very good old wine. They ought to be destilled with a slow fire, so y^ the
spirits of the lembek be now and the couled, afterward to be circulated, till
yt all the gros matter be separated from the subtle as much as is possible:
Gualterus Ryffius. It may be destilled, as I conjecture, ether in a Cucurbita,
specially somewhat short, with a limbeck: or in a crooked still with a
receiver of a diverse figure and fashion, or rather in two crooked stils, whereof
the one may be the receiver, or else in brazen vessels which the Apothecaries
call bladdars as water of Cinnamon, or burning water.

In like manner be oils destilled, as the same man teaches of Vernix or Gum
of Juniper: of the flowers of Spike or Lavender, of Anes sedes and many other
sedes.

The.iiii. essence or fire, of Selandin, which is like a liquor of oil. c.
how it is prepared and to what use, read before, where we have entreated of
quint essence. It is possible also to draw out.ii. manner of oils, an aerie and
afyerye out of the most herbs and other medicines, after the same manner it
is prescribed there in Selandine.

Oil of Rue. Cardanus in his second book of subtlety, writes that there be
certain poysones which slay with the only touching. Against these Lullius:
but that whereof Cardanus makes Few it self, that is of the extreme tops of
the bows thereof which flourish.

Of oils of siedes and fruits,
OYll of Cloves did I taste once in Italy wonderful swiet and strong: howe it
should be drawn out, we declared before with Catdanus words, where we write
generally of drawing out of oils: also another Ryffius (that is) the same way
yt ye may out of what spyce a man will, which stiept in burning water, they
destill the till the burning water be separated, and as soon as the oil
begins to run, the matter taken out of the Cucurbita is pressed. c. as before
it is written out of Ryffius, who describes also particularly the virtues of
this Oyll.

Oil of Nutmegs how it may be gotten out, fee before where as we entreated
general of the Ryffius, who describes the virtues of ether of them severally.

Oyll of Anis sied is draw out (as out of other dry things which be in the
kind of plants) the siedes well beeten stieped in the best wine, then
destilled by little and little by ascencion, so that the spirits in the Lembeck
be continually refrigerated The virtues of it Ryffius rekoneth, we leave them
out as also of other for the most part, where no peculiar effect or working is
attributed to the waters and oils, yea the same are agreeable to this medicines
also wherout they be drawn, or ever they be destilled, saving that by
destilling they are made more subtle and more effectual or stronger
operation.

Oils of the beries of Juniper and of the beries of Baize, are made one way:
that is pund, Cardan, to be gotten out of woods and cloves: or else as out of
spices, by Ryffius description. I think it makes no great matter, so be that
the spirits which be carried about the lembeck, may be couled commodiously in
it and in the noos.

The virtues of this oils Ryffius rekoneth Agyrtae or iugleres also are wont
with us to sell oil of Iunipers, with tables imprinted coteining the rehearsal of
the virtues thereof. This amongst other heeleth them that be sick of a tercian
as I am informed.

Sum make oyll of wheet pressing it out between two iron plates reed hoot:
other think it to be nothing worth thatis made thus, and that it should be
made as oyll of Juniper, that is of the sticks of Juniper by descencion or
dounwarde. Mesue saith that wheet after it be husked is destilled in a vessel
of sublimacio, as the Philosophers oyll. It cures ring worms, and ruggednes
of the skin which springs of drynes, it mollifies and moysteneth, and other
infections, also of the skin it remedies them. It is described also by Rasus
in his Antidotarium. Loke within emogst the oils not destilled. There might be
made also of Barley and such both by sublimacion, and also by descencion.
Monachi upon Mesue.

Oils of Musk ben, (that is Balanus my repsica,) Fnrnerius calls Retraban
or retraha, of most precious smell. Like as of Almondes, so shalt thou press
out an oyll of the fruits of Ben (which are found about Genua pleteously
enough, of the same price almost that Almondes be) thou shalt let it stand, and
put a good quatitie of musk into it, and so in a glass well stopped, thou shalt
set it in a ketle that it may sees a while, then receive the oyll by
destillacion, which shall be most swiet and most odoriferous.

Certain oils of siedes which I found in a book of Waters of Raimund
Lullius.
THis oils I thought good to write severally, because I doubt whether the
author would have them made by destillacio or simpely by pressing out: in
every one he adds this words and let an oyll be drawn out after the manner
of the to signify, simpely by the pressing out of the siedes in a pres, as the
commun people is wont to do, so is oyll to be gathered, specially when as he
would have it drawn out of the same dried in the sun. Notwithstanding it is
out of all doubt, that the same things destilled accordingly, should be far
moore effectual: But for destilling, the liquor wherein the siedes pund or
stiept ougth to be left with them and putrification to go before. c.

Stiep the siedes of Sponsa solis in woman's

Oyll of the siedes of Few. Mixed the siedes of Few pund with leued gould, and
put it in vynegar a day. Then dry it lightly by the sun draw out an oyll after
the manner of the lay men. It is precious and resists poisons, so that they
shall avoid by vomit, if so be it a man drink of it the second time, the
other humours also that be infected wound and cure be had accordingly. Also
being drunk fastinge and puts away the Gutta or drop and all scabbednes:
and the dropsy sprung of a cold cause, if it be taken but two days. To
conclude it stirs up and restores all sliepy and benumbed members, (taken
with the Palsy, being anointed upon.)

Ruberta (if it be right written, perauenture of the colour he so calls it:
I would rather read Sperma,) that is oil of Nasturtium (I would rather
Nasturtium, as in other in the same place, before and after the oil) moystened
in sharp vinegar, dry it in the sun, and draw out the oil after the manner
of the lay people.

This mixed with Aloes oil of baize, cures all scabbednes and the drop, the
places being anointed. Druncken with Aloes and cools of an elm, it heals
the Tercian and quartaine, and all breches in the body. Soden with a ptisan and
drunken, it stauncheth the bloudye flux and binds

Oil of the seeds of Pimpernell. Put seeds in read wine a few days, dry
them, punne them and make an Oil after the lay manner. This oil drunk
fastinge, dissolveth, breaks and expels the sand (and any stone of the
bladder, yea if it be finished (gathered to y^ form of a stone) what matter so
ever it be of. It lighteneth the wearied members of a man body. These

Of oil of the beries of Juniper, destilling first by ascencion, then by
descencion as I learned of my friends.
TAke a pint or a quart full of the Bearies of Juniper, when they are beaten
steep them two days in well spring water: then put those beries together
with the water wherein they were sooked into a Cucurbita or bladder, as they call
it, Alsatia, about iiii. ounces of oil. Other to separate the Oil from the
water, put it into a drincking glass that has the foot taken away ether by
coming with a wire of iron red hot, or else by chance so that a little
whole in the bottom be left for the oil the whole is stopte, and then the
oil may be poured out into ye vessel where it shall be kept. So destill it in
like manner, but they move the matter oftentimes in the still, least it be
burned. And they say spices beaten, as Cloves and cinnamon my be rightly
destilled likewise. Some, as I hear say, destill in a crooked vessel not of
glass, but of Copper, tinned in the inside: which is put into a long pipe of
coper, as they do for burning water.

An other way of preparing the same by descencion

Then being well beaten, thou shalt put them Aqua vitae,) specially the
arthritical parts and joints, this juice is easily conserved, neither is it
corrupted lightly, it is also swiete and effectual or strong. Some get out
the waterye humour not by a pres, but separate it in a commun fornace, (Balneo
Mariae.) The reast of the matter well dried and beaten, put it in a Cucurbita
that is hable to receive a galon, but thou shalt not fill it up. In it (shit
with the clay of wisdom least any thing brethe out) shalt thou putrify the
matter, about.xxviii. days in horse donge: or if it be winter (in Germany) ny
unto the fornace, for to putrifye it in horse donge a and make it sure with clay
(and the hooll also of the bottom shalt thou fill every where with clay) then
let down that part of the vessel by the hooll in the middle of the fornace so
far, that the nether vessel also (that is) the receiver may be put with in the
nether skirt of the plate, fensed with clay. The clay ought to be tempered with
salt water flox: this) sum cal clay of wisdom. The manner of the fornace is
this: In the midst must a floor or chamber be made with a hooll, as I said:
above Cucurbita is sand laid almost till the third part of the Cucurbita: above
the sand, cools burning, more then the third part of the Cucurbita, nether
needs it to put away the ashes: the rest of the Cucurbita, the fourth part
perauenture, or little les then the third part must be above empty and at
liberty. It so be thou feer that the upper Cucurbita perauenture shall not be
able to abide the force of the fire, thou shalt pariet it with clay, at the
least wife in that part that the cools oyll certainly drawn out by discretion
in a glass, siemeth to me to be les infected with ye faut of adustion or
brentnes. Hitherto although me thinks I have described the matter plain enough
as my friend rehearsed unto me, yet will I ad sum figure, that I may lay the
thing before the eyes most clearly: A. is the Cucurbita containing the
beries: B. the cools: C. the sand: D. ye plate E. the floor whereupon the sand
lies. F. the Cucurbita, that receives: G. the round wreeth of straw whereupon
ye receiver is set.

Oyll of the nuts kernels of the pyn tree for the polishing putting Syluius.

Of oils of gums, teeres or liquors, thickened or congeled, and rosines.
OYll of Mirh. Look before amongst the swiet waters of Furnerius, where we
have described one which is made with one part of Mirh, and the half part of ye
juice of Roses, most odoriferous.

Liquors thikned by nature, and gums as they call them, of a hoot and dry
temperatur, that they may be prepared unto destillacion, when they are pund,
put them in a vessel well stopped (wyn also, except I be deceived, may be a little
sprinkled upon it) and dig it in a could and moist place so diep as a man is
hy, without putting to it any hoot matter, and it must be left a good long
space: notwithstanding it should sooner be resolved if thou put to Caphura also,
seeing they contain a fat liquor and whyt, that which by destillacion is drawn
from them, do easily savour of the injury of the fire faut of brentnes, that
although they be destilled in vessels very men, yet issues forth a liquor
full of dregs, gros, brent, of an vnplesaunt as power forth any gums or
teeres.) This for the most part writes Ryffius in his first book of
destillacion.

The same Ryffius in the treatise of the same work of oils prescribes no
peculiar way to draw out oil of gums: but sends men unto his first koke. He
describes severally the virtues of oils of Ammoniacum, Belzoum, Camphora,
Cloves, Euphorbium, Galbanum, Ladanum, Myrh, Opopanax, Sarcocolla, Sagapenum,
Stirax liquid Calamita. In the only oyll of Mastick he wills the Mastick when
it is pund, to be resolved with old wine (and to be degested) then destilled.
And the gum of Juniper likewise to be resolved and stiept in wine, because of
the drynes of the substance thereof, and afterward to be destilled.

Oil of Belzoum. Thou shalt water a pound of Belzoum or more groos beeten, with
burning water: and in a crouked still with a receiver set under thou shalt
destill it in ashes, with a slow fire Furnerius.

Oyll of Styrax out of ye same. Thou shalt beete somewhat groos Styrax Calamita
that which is full of juice and fat, water it with the best Aqua vitae: then
destill it in a crouked still as the oyll before, and kiep the water by it
self. This oyll excells with a marvelous fragrat swiet savour

Oyll of Camphora. Look before in the wawater of camphora, amongst the simple
waters destilled.

Of oil of Turpentine or larigna resina.
PVt.iiii. pound of Turpentin Rosin or of larix in a large crooked still or
cucurbita of glass, and destilling it, get out an oil, so that the cucurbita or
crooked still be put in sand, first of al with the water shall an oil issue
Valerius Cordus. More of oil of Turpentin, and of the preparing of it and of
the hertues thereof writes Ryffius, which I for shortness sake let pas. This is
chiefly tobe taken heed of, that in the destilling, it sis not, as in honey
also: for they rise and swell quickly these liquors when they are made hot:
wherefore at the first the fire must be made very light scleder Benedict yt by
the same means of the oil incresed. The descriptio following makes with me,
which I found in a certain written book. Take pure sand, or little white and
clear flintes and put them over the fire in a vessel till they wax red hot,
then quench them in turpentin, that they may drink well, and that sand quenched
destil it in a lembeck. Some commend oil of turpentin for the grief of the
stone. Also those, oil of Turpentyn, of a pound of Turpentyn, an ounce of old
tile slates, (or as Albucasis saith, new tile slates because they may drink
the more oil) and Mastik and Styrax, of ether an ounce. The tiles made red hot
are slekt in oil: when they are queched and pund they are mixte with the other
in a lembeck of glass. Three liquors run out, whereof y^ third is the best. Iac.
Hollerius amongst oils without smell for could greues.

Otherwise out of a written book. In a cucurbita half full of Turpintyn, put a
handful of glass pund, and.ii. sponges of the quatity of.ii. fingers
cucurbita, let the fire be continued.xxiiii. hours: when the first destillacion
is finished, destill it again, renewing y^ glass, the cucurbita and the sponges.
To put awai skars, or rather to assuage and mollify them, oil of Turpentin
doth chiefli profit, except those that remain after warts. For they that
commend this oil for the putting away the marks of wartes, they are deceived.
Brasalonus.

Oil of Tartarum sublimated. Put Tartarum beten in a vessel (a cucurbita of
glass parieted with clay, or an earthen cucurbita) and when it is put inalembek
of glass destil it. First of all water will run forth, then oil, which thou
shalt receive by it self increasing the fire by little and little till it leave
running. The dutch written book: Certain practicioners commend the spirit or
quintessence of Tartarum against inward impostumes kings evil.

Oils of barks.
Oil of Cinnamon is made, as we declared before out of Cardanus, how oils be
drawn out of woods and like things, as Cloves: where is also described the
instruments. Or else as we described out of Ryffius of the drawing out of oils
of all kinds of spices. Cinnamo may be stiept about viii. days in burning
water.vi. times destilled, and the be destilled, as I was informed of a fred.
Here will I rehearse also the way to make water (as they call it) of Cinnamon:
for in the destillation or rather longer, an other showed us he had seen in
the destillacion of this water: but peraueture it shall be les nied of such a
long one, when no great plenty of water is destilled.) It may also be destilled
in a Cucurbita of glass parieted with clay, after the manner of Aqua fortis, and
perauenture it wold be best that way. Put a pound of the best Cinnamon pund not
sifted, in the bottom of a still warily, least the powder s^ir abroad or cleue
to the sides, by and by pour to it a.iii. pintes of freash and put linne
clouthes dipped in could water about the pipe, and about the cover likewise if it
be too hoot. For it skills much that this parts be couled that both more
plenty of water and also better may run out. This water of Cinnamon I have scene
my self and tasted, very pleasant and swiet in savour and in taste. The liquor
which I saw was sharp, almost of the colour of milck and troubled: whereupon
certain somewhat red drops of oyll did swim, which were les swiet then the
water, Cardanus writes generally, of liquors of swiet wood things as Cloves.
c. This liquor restores the strength excellently: the other virtues thereof
Ryffius describes: but every man may know the manifestly by the nature of
cinnamon it self.

In like manner is the oyll of the husk of Nutmegs which they call Mace,
prepared, which also is described by Ryffius.

An oyll marvelous swiet smelling, wherewith Furnerius. Thou shalt take in the
month of May the.iii. parts of the second barks of the tree of Almon (so is
it red says Furnerius in the book out of which he writ it, but doubted
whether it ought to be understanded an Elm or a Limon a kind of Orenges) and
the fatter parts of the flowers of the same tree (Les espis de la fleur qui
sont grasses,) in like weight, dry them in the sun. That kind of this tree is
thought best that the third part of the beries of Juniper new, and destill
them dounwarde, with a little fire at the first, then greater and greater
increasing it till al the Oyll be cumd down into the receiver, which should be
of glass and closed with clay. Afterward thou shalt set it in the Sun, with the
mouth of the vessel open a while, or shit with a parchement pricked through
with a few hooles, till the smell of adustion and brentnes brethe out. Sum to
encrees the smell, put to it Belzoum, Spick narde, Agallochum, the shauings of
Cypres, Styrax, Ambra,, Mosch and destill all together, repeting also the
destillacion the second time.

Of oils that are dravven out of Woods.
Oils of wood are all destilled by descencion (as they call it) dounward. Of
the preparing of oyll of the wood of Juniper, Vlstadius, where we entreted of
destillacion dounward generally. He that desires more, specially perteyning to
the virtues, let him search in the duche bouke of Ryffius, who writes that
oyll is gotten out of Guaiacum also after the same manner utterly: and also out
of the wood of Rosemary. I will ad heer that I found in a certain dutche book.

The wood of Juniper to make oyll thereof ought to be cut in ye beginning of
May, or harvest, which is grien or fresh, the barck being red. When y^ barck
is taken of, thou shalt dry it diligently in an oven straight after the bred is
taken out, then cut in pieces with an ax, put it in a pot that hath the botom
full of hooles, set hansumly in a pot vnneth it. The upper pot thou shalt fill
with ye chips of Juniper and also of that whyt Popler which they cal
trembling, with a part of the whyt stones whereof lyme is made: after when thou
hast fensed the cover well with clay, put it into the nether and Brasauolus
commends the same against the pestilence. It puts away cold causes, and
represeth the typum of the quartain. Being anointed from the navel of women to
privy Rogerius.

Oyll destilled of the sticks of Juniper most dry, in two earthen vessels
with the mouths joined together, or also in a lembeck of glass, if it be holden
in y^ mouth, it takes away marvelously the touth ach sprung of a could reum,
likewise it is good against all oher grieves coming of could humours, as the
griefs of the synewes, the conuulsion or cramp of the joints, the palsy, and
like, Matthaeolus. Oil of Juniper (saith Mesues) helps the infections of the
skyn, crieping diseases, the cancar, angry boils, such as in sommens legs the
late wryters call the deed evil. Fill a glased vessel with a narrow mouth,
with the small chippes of Juniper: join a thin plate of iro boored full of
hooles after the manner of a meell sieve, to the hooll mouth: daub the same with
the mouth both of the said vessel and of the lesser, Mesues as Siluius
interprets him. In the same place Siluius in the commentaries. By this art
(saith he) oil is made of the kurnelles of Pynaples to put away the wrinkles of
women's skins, of the wood Gnaiacum, and stronger of Lignum sanctum for the
grieves of the Spanish disease as boils (unto which oyll of Juniper also and
of Broum, and of ash and of like, is holsum) and of other woods, specially
that be fat and full of juice as fruits beries and seeds. The biggar vessel
also may be of metal, or if it be of glass or ston, let it be well fensed all
over with clay of wisdom, an erthen vessel because it is spongi not so cloos,
when the oyll seeks to breeth out it would give it way: therefore let it be
glased within, the nether only in the circuit but somewhat more thik, it may be
also of metal: it may be at length of earth, so be that it be glased and oiled
as they call it: else that most thin oil wold run out here and there by the
pores of the vessel: much les may that oil be received safe in the pit within
the ground having the sides walled with potters earth, (which notwithstanding
Mesues commanded.) Take this withal, that it shall not be needful to dig the
lesser vessel within the ground, if thou can find a means Syluius.

Oil of ash tree helps them that be sick of the spleen, besides that it can
do all that oil of Juniper can, and is made after the same manner Mesues. Where
again Syluius saith. That oil anointed upon the left side under the short ribs,
and drunken, delivers mightily the obstructions stoppings of the spleen: it
mollifies also digests.

This oil (saith Rogerius) represses the cold greues of the joints: it
heals a wound brings skin where it lacks: it delivers from the white
morphew, and blackeneth it.

Io. Manardus in his epistles. 16. 4. giving counsel for ye health of the
cardinal Campegius sick of ye gout: I praise (saith he) both the manner the use
of the some of the decoction of lignum sanctum or of Iudicum to the place where
the grief is. But I think the oil of the same wod, destilled after y^ manner
more effectual, anointing the places therewith yt swell and where the grefe is.
They be wont also of the shauings of raw wode, sodden in some noble wine,
putting unto it old oil, to prepare an ointment which is very good to be
anointed upon the places where the grefe is.

Take ye wode cut like bordes, therwt fill a new earth vessel, with a cover of
the same matter board through with little holes: yt diligently claid let an other
empty earth pot digd in the earth up to the mouth, Andreas Mat in his book of
the frensh disease.

A ma may also, as I hard of a friend, destil guaiacu after the same way that
Aqua fortis is destilled, best in an erthen vessel glased yt will abide the
fire, such as they make at Haganoa. This oil is good for the fauts of ye
joints through the frensh pocks, if it be anointed upon the places with the best
burning water. Oil of the wode guaiacu, more mightilye of lygnum sanctum, is
wholesome for the spanish diseases biles. Sylu. A certain practicioner even so
also yet is the best oil destilled of.

Take the wod of ivy dried, the beries gum also if you may have it. When they
are cut in pieces let the be put in an erthen vessel bored through in the bottom
in.ii. or.iii. places: then let an other pot be set under it. Set the in the
earth, join the botto of the upper unto the mouth of the nether with clay or
paste: the upper pot must stand holly above the ground. At length make a fire
on every side, and the oil shall destill black into the nether Rogerius.

The preparing of oil of capuistu that is smoked because it is made by
descencion, like as the oil of woods, I have rehearsed it in this place out of
the first book of Aetius, where he entreats of oils: from whence Nicholas
Alexandrin{us} also borrowed it in his treatise of oils. Vngues aromatici that
be black (melancho, Fuchsius the expositor of Nicolas reads Megalco, that is
great) thus Masculu, styrax bdellium pure Costus, of every one. v dras (of
every one.ii. vnces a half, saith Nicolas which I like better).v. sextaris that
is about. iiii pints of the best swiet oil, (ii. a half saith Nich.) Hypni as
much as need requires. The cost{us} cut in pieces sowhat gros, likewise ye
styrax bdelliu mixed together, put the into an earth vessel (a new one:
Nycolas) without eres, ye mouth whereof you shalt stop with the hypne, without you
shalt defece it with the slips of aspalath{us} or some odoriferus thing hasomli of
the other vessel that contains the spices agreing aptly with the mouth, into
which thou shalt put.v. sextaries of sweet oil. Afterward dig the ground and
bury the earthen pot that holds the oil unto the neck, lest it wax red hot
after with the fire that shall be made about it: then turn ye other vessel with
spices the mouth downward upon ye head of it, soioyn y^ mouths of the both, yt
they my be closed together most exactly. Then close ye hole vessel roud about
with clay, on every side where the mouths are joined together, by by thou shalt
kindle a fire putting under coals about ye earthen vessel, blowing. When the
fire is kindled let the spices being consumed, yt being set on fire theimai sed
out their vapor by the mouth of ye earth vessel into ye oil set under: For ye
which cause it is called capnisto y^ is smoked. The second day after remove away
the oil, and put it in a glass to keep. Women use this whose flours are stopped,
anointing the nether part of their belly loins therewith. It is convenient
tenesimus, if it be received in wol (hot as Nicolas adds) folded together
and laid to the lower part of the belly loins. The same seems to be called
capnelaeu or smoked oil: but the author of Kiranidum expounds it naphtha, in
his.iiii. book where he makes mention of the fish Ecleneis or Remora.

I have scene also of paper rolled together in manner of a hod, set on fire over a
dish of tin, holden at the very extreme top with a pair of shears (or tongues)
certain drops of black oil run out, which are praised for the spots or
whytneses of ye eyes.

Of trevve Balm, and antibalm, that is Oils composed by art, which are used
in steed of the true Balm both within and without the body.
BAlsamum is a word used almost in Syrian word without dout: for in only Syria
in one only garden did it ones grow. Panag ye hebrew word in the.xxvii. of
Ezechiel, Dauid Kimhi after certain doth erpound it a proper place of Iudaea:
other Apharsamon, that is Balsamum. The later wryters of the Iewes wryt
Palsamon: Mycander Balsamo bycans of the verse. Opobalsamon is a liquor:
Carpobalsamon a sied, which sum call also Casamum: Xylobalsamum, bows or the
wood. I imagined once that it was called Balsamum of the excellency, because
it was most precious most noble of all spices: for Bowl signifyeth lord and
master with the Hebrewes and Sam spyce. Balsamon, is an aromaticall flower,
Varinus. And in died it is reason, that as the thing it self is proper to
Syria, so the etimology and interpretacio of the word should be taken of the
same tong. Peraueture Belsuin also (other writ it Beniuin, other Belzoum) a
liquor of most swiet savour hath the same Origin of his name. Not same only
with the Hebrewes but also Bosem signifies a swiet smell ling spyce, whereupon
the Germans may seem to have called Mosch Bisem.

Balsamum or Balm sum in our age think it is lost, the juice of it was of the
colour of gould, it he led wounds, and toke away wrinkles, and kept the faces
of deed men from corruption: it brake also the stone in the reins, it did put
away the spots Aconitum, Hemloke, and the shaking of very fevers, and in all
this things the virtues of Balm excelled. But now there is another kind of
Balm brought out of Spain, of a red colour teding toward blak, weighty,
sauouring somewhat of adustio or brentnes, and by and by it stryketh pierces the
heed, and the tongue also sharply, but somewhat lunge first as pepper. And it
also heeleth new wounds more quikly then any other thing: and takes away
Goacomax, of the form of a Pomgranat tree and in bigness almost like: but it
has a lief a little biggar and a thinner barck as though it were dry, the
wood of it burns like a torch, the fruit is as the clusters of grapes, but
the kurnelles are somewhat biggar, of a wyny colour, which together do yield
that kind of oyll, Cardanus.

Certain merchants affirm that the gardeines of Balm were destroyed, what
time as Zelimus the Turck, the predecessor of Solyman, wan Chayrum, when the
Turks killing the gardeners, did not forthwith put other in their places the
year of our Lord. 1516. Moreover they say there is a Balm tree of an
indifferent bigness laid to keep to be compared unto many thousands of Ducats,
with the most famous ma of our time, Lucullus, May. Antony Fuggerus: Ioan.
Agricola in his commentaries upon Galen, of the diseased places.iiii. ix.

Antibalsama. Dioscorides prescribes certain notes and tokens of the true
Balm. In the collection and table of those medicines that may be used one in
steed of another, which is joined with Galenus works, and in the end also of
Aeginetas books, we reed: In the steed of ye liquor of Balm, Mirh that
destilleth, that is liquid and running Stacte. In the same place is red also:
In the steed of Opobalsamu the liquor of Myrti: but it seems it should be more
rightly ye liquor of Mirh. Auicenna in his second book in the chapt. of oil:
In steed of Balm (saith he) is liquid Mirh used, or else like weight of the oil
Adhii, or Aldadi, or Dadi as Bellunensis traslated it. Rasis in the latter end
of his Antidotarie separated, in oil Benedict or of tylstons: It is of like
force (saith he) yt oil of Balm: but it is more subtle hoter more Monaci upon
Mesuen. And a little after, in the tretis quid pro quo, what for what, which is
imprinted with the books of the sun of Mesue, it is red. In the stead of Balm,
Turpintyn destilled, or oyll of Baize, or the gum of Ivy is put. But what Dadi
is, I can not easily say: it is described of Auicen the. 213. chapt.
Bellunensis nether there nor in the glosses expounds what it is. It is a grain
(saith Auicenna) like unto barley longar, more strait and cloos together, more
massy, bitter, could, declining to heet, dry CiTum or Ladanum, for it is could
also, so measurably yt it hath a warm heet, according to Galenus mind: it
binds, stops ye belly, and dries in the second degree: that is hooter that
grows in hooter places, the same mollifies meanly and dissolved, and
concokteth or digests. Furthermore in Antiballomenis Graecis, Propolis to be
used in the steed of Tragopogona, that is Ladanum: and Auicenna, in the
resolving (saith he) of hardnesses, ii. third parts in weight of kur, that is
Propolis, and the half of the weight of abhel. Dioscorides says that Ladanum
is mixed with medicines without smell: Auicenna, that it is good for the griefs
of the fundament hemrodes. Now that Auicenna hath, that Dadi is like unto
barley, but longar, straiter. c. I plainly suppose it to be corrupt out of
Dioscorides, who makes Ladon that is the bush of Ladanu, like unto Cisthum,
with longer leues only and more black. Last of all the names Ladon and Dadi do
not much disagree nor be far unlike.

Oil made of the flours of Rosemary (after the same manner as it is made of the
flours of Verbascus) may be used in stead of balm, and a drop of it put into
water, doth likewise go to the bottoe: Arnold de Villa nova. They put this also
amongst other to be a toke of true balm, y^ it will gather milk into courds: I
going about to try on a time, whether Ryffi{us} oil of cloves. In ye greek
Antiballominis it is red yt the rotes of white violets Xylobalsanu. The
Antiballomena or rehearsal of things y^ may be used one for another, which was
once imprinted with Mesues works declare in this wise. In sted of Xylobalsam, the
wod of ivy or Leucoradix. For carpobalsa, the fruit of ivy or Xylobalsamu in
like weight. The same: for balm it self, the gum of ivy. But peraueture in al
these ye expositer or traslater was deceived, when as no writer ever compared
ivy in any wise with balin: therefore I thought Arabic tong to have erred, or
rather the Arabians them selves, confounding Cisson, that is ivy and Cisthum
together: for Ladanum is the juice of Cisthum, which Auicenna did put in stead
of Balm.

In the Antiballomenis ascribed unto Galen I find these also: for the liquor
of balm, ye liquor of Carpasus: and for the liquor of Carpas{us}, the liquor
of Myrtus: and for the liquor of willow, the liquor of black iuye, which the
Arabians leaving certain things or changing them, translated them into Cassia
called Balsamodes because of the smell. Sisymbrium a kind of wild mint, growing
ni waters for the most part sowhat red, ye late writers many of the cal it
Balsamita. There is an other Balsamina as the Ligures about ye river of Poo cal
it: The Thuscans or Florentins cal it the apple of Ieru salem, with the leues
of the white vine, ye flower of the Cucumer, the fruit atboth ends round sharp
like to a little cucumer. c. the apple with ripens Lentil, most red, wheroute
oil is pressed chief for wounds. Some season the apple first in oil set it in
the sun a few days, then they bery it in horse dong or in the earth so long till
it be clean putrified: so they affirm it will get the virtue of balm in
closing of wounds, and thereupon hath it gotten the name of Balsamina: Ruellius
out of Her molaus. Brasauolus calls this oil of Cochirs: and and sharp husk,
Cochia or Momordica. The oil (saith he) is prepared diverse ways, both by
infusion and being digd under the earth many years, and by seathing: and it is
made of Echinus, of the leaves severally and of the sedes. That which is hid in
the earth, helps the greues of Hemrods. Generally, that oil asswageth all
greues, who so desires to know mo very marvelous effects of this oil and
herb, let him read Matthaeolus Senensis commentaries on Dioscorides the.iiii.
book

Balsamaeleon, that is the oil of balm, of a plant a cubit high or.ii. cubits,
like unto wild Rue, by the bark whereof toward the East, being scarified rased
and wounded fat tears destill, that is the fatness of the balm. Other do beat
in a mortar the slips of this plant in the beginning of Vere, sethe it in
water, press them in a press, and call it oil Mesues, Syluius translating
him. Where Syluius also in his scoles saith: let Xylobalsamum Carpobalsamum most
new be stiept one day in old oil: after let ye oil be drawn out by the art of
chemists, it shall be of no small estimation: or set Xylobalsamum new be putin old
oil and set The virtues of this oil so much praised of al me nothing spoke of
Mesue, it is marvel, for it heteth moysteneth, extenuateth and makes slender,
digests, scours, closes. This virtue of closinge she wed unto the old
writers, makes it at this day precious. Opobalsamum is most rare and gesen:
and therefore most precious. Wherefore Petrus of Abanus, Guilielmus Placectinus,
Bartolemaeus Montaguanus, composed an oil of balm, nothing inferior in virtues
to balm. Other put the leues and sede, and wode of this plant in oil and set
it Opobalsamum: This saith Syluius.

The Egiptians make a counter^aite kind of Opobalsamum, of ye bark new most
swiet smelling heating it in oil of Almondes, of a singular smell when it is
fresh mixed: Alexan. Benedictus.

The oil that they cal communlye Balsaminum raises up suddenly them that be
fall of the falling ficknesse, being heide to their nose that they may
Balsamum.

Of balms made by Art.
THe commun intentand purpose of all Balsams or balms, to speak of their
virtue, seems to be, to close and heal wounds biles, that in a short space,
to avoid and turn away the evil Symptomata or diseases which the sores wold
otherwise parts: hereupon it is that all of them have Turpintyn Rosin, sum
also other rosins, as the rosin of Pyn tree, Mastik: here upon also gums are
added Frankincense, Elemi, gum of Ivy, Bdellium, Sarcocolla, Mumy, c. Unto sum
diverse spices are put to smell the swieter: whereupon they are made apt for the
moo uses, and miet for diverse diseases even within the body also, as to those
also, unto which moreover diverse plants or their parts are added. They are
destilled the most part of the They do al season the vrin with their smell. They
must all be received within the body mixed with sum liquor, chiefly wine, so
that to a sponful of liquor, one drop of the oil or.ii. at the most be put.
The dregs remaining in the bottom are good for nothing, saving that they may
be used in steed of Colophonia or Scammonium. I same of late a practicioner
destilling I can not tel what kind of Balsam in a pan, with ashes laid upon
slates, as I described before in the mention of destillation Cucurbita of glass
so diep set in the ashes, that they were not above the matter contained in the
vessel. The vessel was full to the middle, able to receive, if it had been
filled up perauenture, iiii. pounds: he continued this labour.iiii. or.v. days
nights also, never abating the fire. He separated only.ii. liquors, ye first
whyt, which was more plentiful and in gretter abundance: then a redish,
which was yielded lesser by the.iii. part. The clay wherewith the Cucurbita,
the lembeck it was good to chronicall agues, for the most part he mixed with
it sum spices, as Ginger and Sugar with wine the drop of the oyll, that the
sick should les perceive that he drunk only a drop of the oyll: sumtymes he
would mixed nothing else with the wine but one drop of this oyll, specially to
amend the defaut of a stinking breeth. Sumtymes he gave it in water, other
times he dropped it into a shyue of breed: sumtymes to phlegmatic and gros men,
with a sauce made of musterd and

An other moore noble of the same mans which is put in steed of Balm. A pound
and a half of oyll, Myrh, Xylobalsamum, Opoponax, Bdellium Aloes,
Carpobalsamum, Ammoniack, Serapinum, the nut of Inde, Hypericon, Mace, gum
Arabic, Tragacantha, of every one an uncle: broken till sherdes that never
touched water* red hoot and quenshed in three vnces of comune oyll, vii. vnces
of cleen and clear Turpintyn. All pund and knoden together in a mortar, destil
them like Rose water. This water is proffitable against ye stoon, being mixed
with medicines against ye same. Hardnesses a^d skares it makes them even and
is used in every thing in steed of Balm.

An other of the same mans more noble. Myrh Carpobalsamum, the nut of Inde, of
every one half an uncle. Hypericon or saint Iohns wurt a drame (otherwise two
drames.) When they are pund somewhat groos, let them be mixed with five vnces of
oyll, and an uncle and an half of Turpintyn. In the end put to five grains of
Musk, and. iii of Ambra, and an uncle of oyll of till stones, and let them be
destilled as before. It hath the same virtues that Balm hath may be used in all
things in steed of it. This Nic. Stokker also an excellent if mifrend sent me
the descriptio of it right when it was ready pressed, he adds at length the
Musk and Ambra, with oil of tile stones, and destilled them not as I think. He
has the former description in Luminari Maiore, as also the next following of
Montaguana.

A balm composed of Bartolomeus Montaguana out of his Antidotarie the first
chapter which is of oyntmentes. Turpentin a pound: white frankincense. iiii,
ounces: as much of bay beries: gum Elemi. vi. ounces: Mastik: Galangal, Cloves,
Cinnamon, Zedoaria, Nutmeg, Cubebae, Lignum Aloes well beaten, of every one an
ounce. Let all be destilled together, first with a slow fire: and first shall
run out a water called of Balm. Then when the fire is increased thou shalt
gather an other water by it self. And do so the third time. The shall destil
forth balm in all trials. It shall be the stronger the oftener it is destilled.

Balm of Peter Aponensis in his addition unto Mesues in the treatise of
oyntinges (for the diseases of the heart.) Mirrh elect, Aloes Hepaticae,
Spieaenardi, Sanguis Draconis, Frankensece, Mumiae, Opopanax, Serapinum,
Crocus, Mastik, Gum Arabic, liquid Styrax, of every one two ounces, two
ounces and a half of Ladanum elect, or Castoreum: half a dram of Muscke.
Turpentyn as much as the weighte of all the rest break them that be to be
broken and when they are all mixte with the Turpentyne, destyll water of
Rooses. These saith Peter, as the munkes that write upon Mesuen, said they
write it out of written books, far truer then in the Printed books, and it
is had in Luminare maiore. Aponensis saith, we find no mention made by the
old wryters of the annoyntinge of the back bone, perauenture not because they
were ignorant in so profitable and commendable a thing, but be cause they would
keep it secret. For this is an excellent help, preseruinge the subject of
life Nucha, it springs of the brain. c. Therefore things anointed with this
shalt thou comfort the cloking substance (that is the cauls and cotes of the
brain) and the spiritual substance and sinues, and al the bones helping also
the Palsy all the diseases of the sinews, also the panting trembling of the
hart, manifest werines, and it is the chiefest medicin of all other in the
swift comforting of the

When thou wilt comfort bodies that be extenuated and brought lowe, thou
shalt mixte Roose water with it, and annoynte it upon the lower mansions, and
from the Nucha unto the rains. somewhat warm, an hour before the fit, leaving
upon it the token of it with Pecia, thou shalt put away the shaking of
wandering Agues, and of any simple agues. But quartaines and wanderinge Agues
it helps at the beginnninge of the course (this place and they which follow
seem unto some to be corrupted in the printed books) in the swoundinge or
debility, annoyntinge the extreme parts of the back boon: that the
instrument for the purpoose may speak with voice, Aponensis.

The same manne wills to mixte this composition in the steed of true
Opobalsamum, with Triacle, Mithridatium, Diacurcuma, Aurea Alexandrina. This
Oil (saith he) Epiphanius Empiricus to all diseases of the sinews,
anointing twice a day therewith the Nucha, the back and inynts, for it is
plain by manifest proves, specially in a cold matter. The same man commands
to still this oil in Balneo Mariae, which I like not.

A quickeninge water and one that procures youth unto an old man, out of the
book of Lullius of waters. Turpentyne a pound: honey half a pound: Aqua
vitae thryse or four times destilled Lignum Aloes welbeaten, Sadali
mustatelli, of ether.iii. drams, gum Arabeck (perauenture a dram): Nutmegs,
Ambra, ofether.ii. drams. When they are all pund destill them with a slow
fire, till ye have the first water clear. And when the second begins to
run out, whithe shall be like to a burning cole, increase the fire by and by and
keep that by it self. The encrese the fire again, gather the third, which
shall be black and thick like honey, till al the liquor be run out. Of The first is
called mother of Balm, the second oyll of Balm, the third Balm artificial. The
first is ministered in drink, with warm wine. The second a^d the third are good
to remove maladies which newly gnaw the flesh of mans body. The first drunk
with warm whyt wine, purges the stomach from al il humours: and withholds
the water that it cum not at the hart or principal parts, as it is plain by
often experiment. A fine lynnen clooth moystened in this water and thrust into
the Bombase, dipped lightly is this water, is very good to put in the ears
against any kind of deafnes. Being anointed it cures the rednes of the face:
the palsy of the tong, and all cold diseases. The second and third water Noli
me tangere: against the kings il and also the disseases of the neck and
throot. Also against the fistula and the ill disease called Malus morbus,
specially if it be yet but new: for by washing it and wetting and oft laing a
linne cloth moystened therein upon it, it is made hool. They help also if a mabe
beten with stones, or clubes or a staf. No poison can approach ny unto them,
and a spider touched therewith dies. They be anointed upon most proffitably
against all palsyes. They strengthen all the

In the same Lullius a marvelous water is made in this wise. Cloves,
Nutmegs, Ginger, Zedoaria, Galangal, both sorts of Pepper, Juniper beries,
the pills or barck of Citri or Oreges, Basilicum, Roosemary, Maioram, round
Mint, Bay beries, Penny royal, Gentian, Calamint, ye flowers of Elder, Roses,
Ammens, Spick nard, wood of Aloes, Cubebae, (here seems somewhat to be left
out) as well wyld as domekical or growing in gardines, Cardamomum, Cinnamo,
Calami aromatici, Stichados, Chamaedryos, Chamaepity os, Melissae, Mastick,
Olibani, Aloes hepaticae, Anis siedes and flowers, she siedes of Mug wurt, of
every one an uncle. Put unto this dry fat swiet Almondes, of every one an uncle.
Whyt old honey half a pound. After twys as much Sugar as all the forsaid be. All
this shalt thou put into Aqua vitae. v. or.vi. times destilled in a lebek of
glass, ye Aqua vitae shall be as much as thrys ye weight of all the speces
besides. After thou hast lest them stand.ii. days, thou shalt destill the with
a slow fire. The first water is most clear precious. The second differs in
colour, and must be received in an other vessel: it is whyt, good towhit Aqua
vitae of the same weight. And ye first water shall run furth odoriferous and
marvelous, which thou shalt receive by it self: then an other of the colour
of safron, the third at length like blood.

The virtues of the first and of the second water are this. If the one of the
be poured in to a would while it is new, there needs none other remedy: But
within a natural day and a half at the most it shall be made hool, so be that
it be no deed wound. All ill soores or boils, Old, roten Cankered, Fistula,
Lupus, Noli me tangere, and like to them, let them be washed with ether of this
waters, and they shall be heeled within a few days. One drop only dropped upon
a Carbuncle quensheth carnosity bred upon it, drop one drop of this waters
upon it every third day, and within nyne days it shall be hool, except it be
utterly destroyed. A drop of them drunk with a little good wine breketh the
stone in the reins or in the bladdar, or in the yard stopped and that within
two hours, delivers from the grief. If deed flesh be washed away therewith,
the place is shortly made hool. If a woman be sick of her womb or belly, let her
drink a little of them with sum juice. If a man have any

The third and bloody water, which surnamed holy and blessed, is so excellent
in virtues, that if one use half a sponefull of it.xv. days, he Astma or
disease of short wind, the dropsy, palsy, Ischia or Sciatica, the swooning,
the falling sickness, the drop in the joints called the gout, ye consuming
fever, the strangury, and many other diseases, and that within two months. It
recovers youth unto old men: a man that lies a dying, out of all hope of the
physicians, it restores him, if one drop of it let fall into his mouth, be
swalowed, so that it may cum to the hart. If so be quantitye of a wheat corn
of this liquor with a sponefull of water of borage, destilled like Rosewater,
after the year is ended, he shall seem as though he were made new, in his
flesh, blud, and hole body, both in form and strength.

first and second. If a ma put a drop of it in his hand, it pierces straight

artificially. First will a most subtle clear water run out, which burns

of Baize two vnces. Galbani, Gummi Elemi, of ether six drams. Gum of Ivy,

destilled.

A Balm for skares. c. If by the means of a struck (saith Lullius about the
end of the second book of Quintessence) any great syne remain in the Gummi,
Cyperi, Carpobalsami, of every one.ii. vnces. Saffroan uncle. Turpintyn.ii.
pound. Oyll of Olives of the eldest.iiii. vnces. Pun the that be to be pund,
and sift them, and mixed them with the Turpintyn and Carpobalsamo, and together
with the oyll destill them with a slow fire. The destilled liquor pressed vynes.
Afterward thou shalt use it, as true Balm, whose tokens and properties to
know it by it hath every one, may be sold in steed of it.

A marvelous water or oyll for strumes and swellinges of the throote, whereupon
men are said to have swollen throotes. Oyll of Bays.vi. vnces. Olibani,
Mastick, Gummi Arabici, pure and clear Turpintyn of every one three vnces. Mixed
them in a mortar and destill them in a Lembeck. Afterward, put to ashes to the
water drawn (De la cendre Gallice) except it should be red De la Cedre, that
we may understand the C^der fry. Then destill it again, and this second
liquor keep it like Balm. The swelling being anointed therewith oftentimes in
the day, ass^ageth by little and little.

An artificial Balm, not to be destilled, but sod only, out of the French
book of Andreas Furnerius. Olibani, Galbani, oyll of white Poppy, oyll of
bitter Almondes, clear Turpintyn, of every one (Vng quarteron, Gallice: but
this quantity seems to much.) Oil of Olives.ii. pounds. Thou shalt heet the
Oil in a leeden kettle upon the fire and when it shall begin to sis, put in
the Galbanum piece meell, and over a slow fire stur it softly now and then:
then put to the Mastick and the Olibanum, and stur it till they be melted by
little and little. Afterward the Pitch and Turpintyn, (but he left out the Pitch
before) with a slow fire fire, and put to the other two oils, and mixed the by
stirring, and again set them to the fire a little. At length put in the grienes
of the bras, mixed them thoroughly and streyne them through a new clooth into an
other vessel leeded also. When thou wilt use the forsaid oyll, thou must see
before that no sinew, or vein almost be cut. Then purging the soor diligently
and making it cleen, thou shalt make warm a little of this oyll in a vessel or
spoun of bras, and lay it upon so hoot as the sick may abide

Sum mixed Turpintyn and certain gumes together in a Cucurbita of glass, and let
it sis softly,

Oyll also of Hyperico is compared of sum unto Balm: which because it is not
destilled, I will describe it hereafter.

A water that brings out boones, and preserves that the wounds chance not
to root. Turpintyn pure and white, but vnwasht, Zopissae, honey, of every one a
pound: Half a pound of Rosin

and spices. c It is rehearsed befoore, in the second order amongst the waters

in the third order.
