Of certain other not Alchymisticall or not destilled or sublimated medicines,
but cunningly and wittilye prepared by other and diverse ways.
THe medicines that we have hitherto described are all comprehended under the
name of lyquors Corollarium or addition which are not very common, nor to be
met with every where. But no man ought to look hear for a full or exact and
general way of composition at our hands: when as we shall teach only a few,
and such as shall come to mind by the way, and all these as it were by
matters besides our necessary purpose. diverse kinds and ways of making and
preparing of medicines, let him goo to the books of Iac. Siluius and other of
this matters.

Of diverse Oils.
I Se the most part of odoriferous and precious oils to be sod in a double
vessel It is an easy matter to pres out an oil that shall be like the plant in
savor Dioscorides the flours were poured into pure oil.iiii. dais, were set out
in the sun, then the oil being pressed out, most diligently, as much more of
the new flours was added, and again it was set out in the hot Sun,

The second way it stands in pressing out, in some things without any help
of Art, as Myrobalano, in other that be dry and odoriferous by their own
accord, as the Nutmeg, that is light stiept in wine and pres out with a pres.
So may a manne draw oil out of spices. But they that be odoriferous, and lack
the substance of oils, as flours, upon Almondes and under them, and lay in
order the leaves of Almondes and of the flours by course, and keep them so
lunge pressed together, till the smell of the flowers may be vanished away, then
put them away and in their steed lay other flours after the same sort, and
do this so oft (for there is not one way in all) till the Almonds have gotten
a very great smell, then have them to the pres. A man must first blanch them
and parch them a little at the fire. By this means the oil

The third way is by destillacion (whereof it is said before.) Cardan.

Unto the most part of oils that be to be sod and made by fire, they put some
wine, by the which means they smell les of adustion bretnes, they be sod till
the wine be consumed. But flowers and such as be of a sclender substance, it
is Hyperico or S. Ihons wurt.

Iasminum is made of.ii. ounces of flours of Iasmin oil, which afterward is
sod with a slow fire.

Oil of swiet Almondes, hooll Cloves being added thereunto, let it be set in
the sun.viii. days, it shall be marvelous swiet smelling. But thou shalt reed
diverse ways in Mesue, and in the annotacions of Syluius upon the same, of the
pressing out of oyll of Almondes, which also may be applied to diverse other
fruits and siedes from the which oyll is pressed out. We use the oyll of
Almondes am. (that is bitter) in stead of oyll of ye Brassauolus.

I am wont to make an oyll of siedes and the reed codes of Capsicum, or
Cardamomu Arabicu: other of the codes thereof alone, put in oyll, which is wont
to be used in place of oyll of Pepper, or also of Euphorbium, if it be put in in
more abundance, for it is far more vehement then Pepper. With us (they call it
reed Pepper, sum of the comun people call it Siliquastrum, but not ryghtly) but
few of Cardan: which I praise not. Nether was the^ ever any man said that
fire was venomous, burn it never so much: when it hath no venomous quality
besides. I have my self used both the siedes of this Capsicum and the codes,
without harm in potage but in a small quantity.

An oil of the kirnels of cherries cleanses ye face, takes away spots and
frakenes: it is good also for the gout, and the stone of the reins bladder. It
is made as oil of swiet Almondes. Furne.

Oyll of hay is thus made. Let the hay be set on Rogerius.

Oil of whet is made between two iron plates moderately made hot (or as Rasis
hath, between a marble and a thick plate of iron,) but much better as I hear,
by destillacion: it is commended Furnerius and other. Sum say also that it is
good for fistulas, the chames or chinks of the skin: Mat. Read before emogst ye
oils destilled.

Oyll of Ivy beries, drawn out like as out of Bay beries, is good against the
gout of a could cause, or of obstructio or stopping: and it restores the
astonished mebers take with the palsy. Rog.

A marvelous oil of Hypericon or saint Iohns wurt. Three vnces of the tops of
Hypericon are in sufficient, afterward sis it in a double vessel well stopped,
and pres it out strongly. Likewise stiep new Hypericon, sis it and press it,
put to it.iii. vnces of Turpintyn, six vnces of old oyll, a scrupull of
Saffron. Sis it till the wine be consumed. This oyll (saith Syluius) no man
knows who was the author of it: Yet Galen writes that Tart or harsch wine,
wherein the leeues of Androsaenni or Ascyri (which are kinds of Hypericon) are
sold, doth cloose great wounds: and as sum Hypericon hot, dry and of subtle
parts, is wholesome for them to drink that be diseased with ache in the hippes or
Sciatica. Of the which, the virtues of other mixed heer, thou may gather, y^
which oil strengthens, digests and sclendereth. For it is composed of
contrary substances. Sylui. upon Mesuen. Brassa. puts the same discriptio
in Examine simplice. 519. chap. Sum (saith he) put simpely the flowers into a
glased vessel, y^ the oil may be gotten out by it self with the strength Syl.
Thus prepared in a glased or glass vessel, they keep it. But this things are
clearly expounded of Mesue newer authors. This Brassauo. Sum put to it worms
or bowels of the earth, and let it stand a month or more in a wine cellar in
a pot stopped with clay or past, the they sis it by little little in y^ same
about That is the best that is streined first. The colour of the oil is almost
red, the taste is somewhat sour. The use of it is for wounds, prickinges and all
kinds of offensions and the swellinges that cum thereupon. Sum use them to the
grievous soores of the legs.

An other composition of oil of Hypericon, which heals any wound great or
small within.xxiiii. hours, out of a certain book imprinted in french without
an author. A handful of Hypericon: Comun Axungia, that is swynes grees,
clarified streined. Turpintyn of Venice the third part of a pound. A dram of
saf fron. Cut all small and fine and mixed them in a great phiall of glass,
which when thou hast stopped diligently with wax and otherwise, set it in dong
two foot diep or more, in a place that the morning sun beemes and evening may
cum to it. At the length when the year is gone about, take out the phial, in
the which thou shalt find an oyll like

An oyll is made also of Juniper beries, by expression, as of other siedes.
Brassauo.

An oyll of the beries of Baize and Juniper, stepped in wine, is beat out with a
miln, weary profitable for the that have grief in the hukle boon, and cold
diseases of the joints: Iac. Hollerius.

Oil of Bays. The beries of Bays grene and beten, let them be sod in oil and
streined. An other sis them and strain them. Otherwise, set the beries beaten
in wine, and when they are stiept.iii. days, let them be pressed out in a pres.
Or else fresh and ripe beaten, let the be put in a sak, and the oil drawn out.
It is wholesome against the colic, the disease of the great guts the Sciatica:
Rogerius.

Oil out of the Myrepsical Nut, pressed with an Anuil and a red hot iron is
gotten out. Iac. Hollerius.

Oils to assuage griefs are made of certain Calen dulae (Mary goldes)
Rosemary, Maioram and other. Iac. Hollerius.

Oil of Nutmegs. Cut them in smalpeces, and when they have stand.iii. dais
infused in Malm sye, dry them in the shadow.ii. days, then warm them
moderately in a fryinge pan: and thou shalt sprinkle them in the mean season
with Rosewater, and pres them out in a pres Cardan.

I hear say some do not stiep them in wine, but a finger may suffer, and so put
in a little bag must they be by by tied, and pressed in a little press of wod,
with a little lake or gutter of wod, having a spout inclining downward, the
little bag put there in. c. They yeilde for the most part, the.viii. part of the
weight of the nuts, so that one ounce of the nuts give a dram of oil, if the
nuts be sufficiently moystie. A man may warm ye same matter again pres it as
before: but the second time yields very little oil and not so good. It is
better therefore to two extreme irons or ends whereof, consisted of. ii hollow
half spheres, so that when they were pressed together they might hold a Nutmegge,
or a little les, as occasion of the pressing served. The one of the half
spheres was bored through with.iiii. or v. little holes, that the oil might
run out, when the ends of the tongues moderately made hot were pressed together. I
found also this descripcion in a certain written book. Take.ii. pounds of
Nutmegs. Three ounces of Malmesy, wherewith the Nutmegs

There be many ways to make oil of Rooses. It is made ether with oil and ripe
Roses, or both of them unripe, or the one ripe, tother unripe and so there
is.iiii. diverse ways. Some in stead of Rasis in his Antidotario seperato
puts.iii. ways. First. Take a pound of comun oil washed, wherein thou shalt
put the fourth part of Grene Rooses in a glased vessel (of glass rather)
which thou shalt set in the sun for the space of.iii. days (ye.xl. as Aegi
neta hath). Then strain it and put it in a glass. This way is better then
the other. The second: Take oil and Roses as before, and hang the vessel in a
well so that it may be touched of the water: and keep it. The third: Oil and
Roses as before, put them in a glass anointed within with honey, which stopped
thou shalt let it diep in ye earth, where it shall not be touched nether with
water nor other moisture. ii, months. This oil will be better smelling then the
other: These writes Rasis out of Aegineta as it appears. Aegineta bids
in the xx. chap. of the seventh book, unto a Sextarium or wine pint of oil
Omphacinum (made of olives not fully ripe) to put.iii. ounces of red Roses the
nails Mesuae in the. 411. chapt. describes.iiii. ways. First that fresh and
new red roses be set in the sun.vii. days, then let them be sod in a double
vessel.iii. hours: then (the Rose leaves wrong out) let other be put in, and
let them be set in the sun and sod as before. Which when thou hast done thrice,
put to the oil water of infusion of Rooses, Syluius traslateth it, as much as
the oil is, which I like not so well.) So when it is set in the sun.xl.
days, strain it and set it lunge again in the Sun. The second, mixting
with the oil washed the juice of Roses, and the water of their infusion, and the
leaves beaten together: then setting it in the sun with swiet Almondes
blaunshed, exactly beaten in a mortar, leaves of Roses be beaten again, the
make them in little lumps or caakes, and keep them in a hot air.xxiiii.
hours. Then beat the again and kneed them in the mortar very exactly, pouring
unto it a little hot water of infusion of Roses. At length prees out the oyll
with a press, put in a glass covered set it to su. The fourth, y^ it be made
with Sesamum blaunshed after the same manner as with Almondes. But Almondes are
Sesama for ripe. This hath Mesue, where Syluius had it. The first composition
(saith he) of the.iiii. now rehearsed, is used of many: but of the Parisians,
the composition of Nicolas, which shall be declared in his Antidotary. And
again, I hear that oyll of Roses is is made most odoriferous, by putrefying
the roses one month in dung in a vessel well stopped. After the same manner of
commun Mastick, and Roses incarnate and Musk Roses and such like, Cardanus
out of Symeon. There be sum that when the Rooses are beeten, and sod in only
water, say there swims a certain fat foom, which may be streined or
gathered me, that the leaves of Roses new should be sod in water till they be
thick as honey almost, then crushed with a spoon, that the oyll or foom may enter
in to it: but sum water will be mixed also with it, wherefore when it is gathered
in a glass, it is set in the sun ye oil swimming above in ye top is separated.

Oyll of the flowers of Elder purges and makes smouth the skin, strengthens
the sinews, and helps the griefs of them. Furnerius.

Oil of Spick most wholesome for the that have Antidotary of Arnold de Villa
nova.

Oyll of the flowers of Verbascum is made, by settin them in the sun in a glass
(as also of the flowers of Rosemary) most commended praised for the gout of the
fiet of other griefs, specially hot.

Oil of violets is made as oyll of Roses, but of grien oy^l, or oyll of
Almondes or Sasamin: Mesue. Paulus Aegineta makes this oyll of purple Violeth
or Leucoio that is yelowe: or he sets them in the sun covering the vessel
exactly that it breath not through, only ten days, the Violettes in the mean
season thrys changed, and at length he adds dry Violettes.

Of oyll of Tartarum, that is the dry Lies of wine.
OYll of Tartarum devised by Peter Argil lata, serves to cleanse the face and
to smouth it. Tartarum cleaving to the sides of the vessel, white rather then
red made into powder, is stepped in vinegar, after it is folded in a linen
cloothe, then let it be put in Tow moystened with water under the ashes:
after that let it be laid in a dish hielding towad the one side.iii. days:
then shall a certain humour somewhat red destill.

Nicolas way to make oil of Tartarum cleaning Tartaru that is of good wine
beaten, folded in a linen cloth, moysten it well with strong white vynegar:
sis it under hot ashes (burn it) till it wax black, beet it again, keep it in
a vessel lying on the one side inclined eight days, in a cold place, till it
be resolueed into oil, which if it do not, pres it out and keep it. The same
ways doth Mesues make oils of eggs. It were better, when ye Tartaru is calcinated
and put it in a Hippocras bag, as they call it, into a vessel set under it.
Syluius. I find an other certain manner in Furnerius in a French book of
decking, where as he bids to take Tartaru burned and calcinated, as much as
can be taken at two handfuls and tied strait in a linen clout to be burnt
and calcinated in a furnace of Glass, Lyme or Brick: the to be powered into a
good quatitie of water with as much Alum as a nut and to be much stirred
about: then when thou hast let rest.xxiiii. hours, strain this water
(casting

Sum put Tartarum to be calcinated in a new pot in a potters oven, and when
the vessels are all baked then take it out. But I think it should

An other way. Take equal porcions of Tantarum Salnitrum pund, burn them in a
large pot: then grind or break them with a hot iron and put them in a little
bag in a wine celer, that they may destill.

An other. Beat Tartarum and Nitrum in equal parts, mixed them and burn them
that the Nitrum may be consumed. The Tartarum that remains: put in a bladder
(that is a glass like a bladder) hang it in hot water, and it shallbe straight
resolved into oil. An other. Tartarum well washte from the dregs and very well
dried shalt Filtrum: then let it gather into a Iellye: when it is so,
calcinat it again that it may wax more white. So at length shalt thou hang
it in a little bag, as an Hippocras bag in a moist place: lay on some
broad thing for a cover, that no unclean thing fall into it, and setting a
glased pot under it.

Some destill it in a lembeek of glass (that is to say in ashes) from whence a
water runs first, then the fire increased by little and little, the oil.

The use. It is good for all scabs and Ringwormes, it makes the skin white,
clear youthlike. I have scene it used to running soores of the head: to the
which I think that is better where with Nitrum is burned together. It makes
coper also silver white, and puts away spots steins in linne clothes. It is
put to colours to make the ink, of the colour of the bresill wode and other.
Rogerius also. 4. 9: teaches to make oil of Tartarum. This (saith he) cleanses
cloths and spots remaining after birth of a melancholy cause, purges the face.

Of oils of the yelkes of eggs, worms, and Scorpions.
Oil of eggs, out of Rasis. Put the yelks of eggs in an iron kettel upon the
coals till Mesues) is proved with much experience to purge the skin, to heal
thoroughly tetters, ringwormes and other faults of the skin, to breed hear, to
sores boils and fistulaes. Thirty eg yolks or there about hard roasted,
crummed with the hands, let them be parched in an earthen frying pan or
skellet leaden with Mesues. Whereas Syluius in his annotacions saith. It takes
away marvelously the foulness of the skin, and skars, specially that be left in
burnt places, for the most part it smells somewhat strong, yet at the last
destilled by sublimacion, les. It increases hear, as Serapion saith in his
Antidotarie.

Oil of eggs of Nicolas fashion. Fry sodden yolks of eggs with a slow fire
made of coals in an iron skellet, continually sturringe them with an iron
rod, till they be well roasted, press them Syluius) to fry the yelkes raw,
and move them continually with a spoon, till they being roasted, and pressed with a
spoon, give an oil in a vessel hielding, which put in a glass reserved good a
great while. Out of xx. yolks thou shalt draw out in.ii. hours.iiii.
ounces or there about.

Matthaeolus wrytinge upon Dioscorides, praises this oil for the roughnes of
ye skin, for ringwormes,

In a wound upon the brain pan, pour in Abhomeron, Abynzoar teaches,
which we have also tried with good success: Marianus Sactus.

An oil for the sores or boils of children.xvi. yolks of eggs sod: an vure of
Mirrh.iiii. grains of black Helleborus, let them be mixte together with an iron
spoon in an iron skellet set upon a few coals, then pres the yelks, and get out
the oil, till it begin to be clear without skin: put yt oil in a glass and keep
it for thine use: anoint the sores twice a day, and the scabs shall fall away
by their own Alexander Benedictus in his.xxx. book of experiments.

I have scene this oil made.ii. ways: first, frying the yelks in a frying pan
till they wax red and yield an oil: secondly, siething them much lodger, till they
appear blak, all the moisture gone out of them: for at length they yield an
oil fodenlye: which is separated with a spoon removing y^ frying pan from the
fire streightway, and pressing ye yolks with the spoon: this some men think to
be

This oil makes skars subtle, dissolveth Tetters and ringwormes: Rogerius. But
Brassauol{us} writes that the Apothecaries do falsely attribute unto this oil
the virtue to put away skars, to the intent they may get eggs from foolish
people. Thou shalt read more of the virtues thereof in the Antidotary of
Arnold. Some tie the yolckes so fried in a linen cloth, and pres them in apres


Oyll of worms or bowels of the earth is commended for the assuaging of
griefs, specially of the sinews joints, also if they cum of the frech
diseases. It is made, the worms purged fro their earthy excrementes, ether by
styping these excrementes through the fingars, or rather by letting them stand
alive in sum vessel where hay is, to crepe: then sod with a little wine in oil
and strained: or set in the sun before they be sod. Sum sis them in water and
gather the fatness that swymmeth

Sum get out the peth of biggar elder pypes somewhat strong, put the worms into
the pouring in also oil: and so stopped, they let them stand in a furnace for
the space of half an hour: then they take away the oil and keep it.

Oil of Scorpions (saith Mesues) breaks the stone of the reins and bladdar,
and expels it, being anointed upon the loins, the privy parts where heer
is, and betwien the tuel beneath the Aristolochia gentian, Cyperi, the barks
of the rotes of Capers, of every one an uncle, let them be set to sun in a
Sextar of oyll of bitter Almondes, xx. days in a glass stopped: then put in the
Scorpions from ten till.xv. in to the oyll: stop the glass and set them in the
sun again a month. He that as he cummeth out of the bathe, in the.iii.
forsaid parts, and let a little portion of it be put in at the yard every
hour. It is marvelous. Sum had rather used the first, sum ye latter as more
effectual and better wurking. This Mesues. But Siluius in his annotacions
saith, it is prepared also now with old oyll putting to simple medicines good
against poison: and it becummeth an oil of marvelous virtues against al poisons
and pestilece. I know a man with the help of this, that seteth Syluius.

In the secretes of Variguanus, we reed that certain have been presently and
out of hand delivered oyll of Scorpions.

Oyll of Scorpions whose operation is marvelous against poisons specially of
Serpents and other beats, and peculiarly against Napellum, is written by
Matthaeolus Senensis in his sixth book of his commetaries upon Dioscorides
imprinted in Italian, from whence we have translated it into Latin. This oyll
(saith he) anointed upon the pulsing veins, where they appear most, as of
the temples, hands and fiet, and in Hypericon or saint Iohns wurt fresh, (with
the herb and flowers.) Put the oyll into a belly of glay, twyse as big as for
the measur thereof, and when the Hypericon is a little beaten put it in upon it.
Then the vessel being stopped, set it in most fine sand up to the midst where
it may be made hot the hool day of the sun, for the space of ten or twelve
days. After thou shalt put it in Balneo Marie four and twenty hours. Then
pres out the oyll from the herb and put to Hyperici, Chamedryos, Calaminthae,
Cardui sancti, of every one a handful moderately beeten: and put them again
into the bath for.iii. days. Afterward thou shalt strain them and pres them:
and putting to them.iii. handfuls of flowers of Hypericon well picked from the
stalks, and well beeten, set it again.iii. days in Balneo Mariae, and pres it
out as before. This shalt thou repeat.iii. or.iiii. times, till the oyll have
gotten a colour as red as Hypericon, or the husks like to the corns of barley,
wherein the sedes lie hid, in such plenty that it may be equal with the three
handfuls: beat this sprinkling upon them a little white wine, power it into
the said oyll. then bery it in sand in the sun eight days, and straight after
in the bath for.iii. days. Strain and pres it as before. And do this three
or.iiii. times putting in new tenderlynges of Hypericon, till a very sad or
dark reed Scordium, Calamint,, the les Centaury, Carduum sanctum, Veruin,
La Creticum, of every one half a handful. When they are beaten, put
them into oyll: and put them in a bath for two days. Strein them and pres
them as before. Then take Zedoariae, the root of white Dictamni, Gentian,
Tormentillae, Aristolochiae root, of every one three drams: of fresh Scordium a
handful. When they are beaten together, power them in and let them again, put
into the oil Styracis Calamitae, Belzoi or Laserpitii, of ether.vi. drammes:
the bearies of Juniper.iiii. drams: Nigellae. iii. drams: odoriferous Casiae.
ix. drammes, white Saunders.iiii. drams: Scoenanthi, Cuperis of ether a dram
and a half, when they are beaten pour them in, and put them into the bath.iii.
days, strain and pres. After, take.xxx. live Scorpions, gathered in the
Caniculer days, and put them in a belly of glass upon hot ashes, and when thou
see them sweat Rhabarbi Electissimi, commun Mirh, Aloes Hepaticae, of every
one.iii. drams, Spiknard.ii. drams, one dram of Saffron: Triacle elect,
Mithridatii perfecti, of ether half an ounce. When they are beaten pour
Napellum, wherewith those.ii. thieves of Cor^ica were infected, whose history we
recited in ye fourth book, where we made metion of Aconitum, to which place I
send the reader. These writes Matt.

Cardanus thinks that the oil which should be anointed without the body
against poisons, upon metal, as of orpment or drawn out of Myssi, see before
in the beginning of the tretise of oils destilled of metally things.

Of oil of Serpents or black Edders, also of oil of frogs, rede Mesuae. c.

Oil of Castoreum is composed with many other spices, and hot gums. c. as
Syluius describes upon Mesuen: it may also be made simple and single, and for
the hard and Massy part of Castorei the fattines cleauinge to it to be added,
or rather both, as I counsel.

Brimston is made hot in oenostagmate (I understand burning water) till a
certain skin swim above (they call it an oil) which a man must take in a
shell, sometimes the water must be changed, till oil enough be gathered,
wherewith they say Hydargiron, that is quick Silver sunken in a body is
enticed out, if it be anointed straight way when a man comes out of the
bathes. Iac. Hollerius. See befoore amongst the destilled oils.

Of fomentations and perfumes*
FOmenta are called of the Greeks Pyriamata, all things that be laid hot to
the body without, ether to assuage the grief, or to draw out the matter,
both otherwise, and also to dissolve swellings: this perauenture is done by
dri fomenta^ions rather, now the one now the other both dry and moist by
course, as in gouty greues ether in the feet or other joints, where as it is
jeopardy least the more subtle part of the matter drawn out, the groser be
left behind and made more hard. Moist fomentacions seem to prepare the matter
for the dry, to extenuate and make sclender, to mollefy, to digest, to make
them vapor out: some perauenture do none of these, according to the diversity
of the matter. But dry fomentacions do dry and Filtrum, that is a shred of
woollen cloth. Bladders or like things full of hot water or oil. Cataplasmata
also may be numbered amongst these, which are ministered hot, that is to say
herbs sod, and Milium, Salt, Sand, bran, Otes made hot in a kettle, and
stirred about, are put into a linen clothe or bag. Celsus in some places
commends the juice of hot Salt, and in an other place he saith it is most
wholesome to make fomentacions with moyste Salt. You shall, as he expounds
put a little bag into hot water, and lay it hot to the place diseased:
putting into the bag now and then an iron s^ise hot, sprinkling water
lightly upon it: therefore you shall have.ii. slices redi, that while the one
slis is put into the bag the other may be heated in the fire. In the disease of
the neck called Tetanus, which is the stifnes of the sinews (saith Celsus)
it is necessary to have a moyste and warm fomentacion. Therefore the most
part of men do pour often upon the necks much hot things. That procures
presently ease, but it makes the sinews more apt to receive cold: which ought
to Paronychia or whitflow, or ilgnawing sore upon her out, and many medicines
used in vain, she laid unto it a fomentacion of the mos of a walnut tree sod
in wine, so that she held the sore place a pretty while in that vapour: then
bound part of the mos hot thereupon, and was by and by made whole. A fire brand
also brent, folded in a moist cloth is in stead of a hot fomentacion in
Celsus. I have put Caret or Cumin sede beaten in a bag moystened with burning
water hot, unto the navel of them that were sick of the colic, and have had
oft a pipe. Hyppocrates describes a peculiar vessel for this purpose. Let
them put out their head the while, which are to weak for to abide it, or such
as it is to be feared lest they swooned or chance into over great thirst. c.
Sum in bathes power water or wine, simple or mixte with medicines upon reed
hot tyle stones or dros of iron, or stones. Sweet in bed is provoked with hot
tyle stones folded in moist linen clothes and put in to the bed, or with
tinnen bottles filled with Celsus. Sum put hot ashes, or burning cools
rather in a vessel Matricaria. be laid unto by course. Celsus wills men in
sum place to lay about the places diseased will perfumed with the smoock of
brymston. Hereunto may be referred all such things as Fuchsius other wryt of
fomentacions, apposicions, embroches, little bags and insessions. The greek word
aeonein signifies the water upon and power upon, whether it be done because of
fomentacion, that is with heat, or other cause. Galen doth praise ye perfume of
the fire stone or miln stone sprinkled with vine Cinabrio that is made of
quitksiluer, sum also with orpmet Marchasita. c shalt thou find in Nic. Mass.
certain other which have taught ye heeling of this disease. The leeues dry of
Tussilago made in perfumes so ye smok may be drawn in at the mouth upon, doth
help ye congh and Orthopnaeu, and breaks the impostumes in the breast. The
same operation also hath the rout Dioscorides.

Of certain iuices.
THe iuices of certain herbs wrong and pressed out, are sod at fire or dried in
the sun, as Bulcasis teaches severally of the juice of Hamsig, Plantain,
Lettis, Singrien, Purselan, Rostrum Purcinum, or hogs snout, Scariola, Fenel,
Smalage, Volubili, Sorrel, and other.

A marvelous way to draw out the juice out of black Elleborum, which sum use
as a secrete would not hyde, lest I should seem to be sorry that our posterity
should have any excellent knowledge. who found this way first I can not say I
learned it of certain my most secret friends I mean that black Elleborum
which communly all men in Germany name in like manner, like unto Consiligo,
very many keep it in their guards: but that which grows upon the moutaines,
to be found in our country Heluetia is best. A man may try the same way in
Colocynthida Esula, Laureola, c. Elleborum cleen and washed, set infused in the
same first a night or more, small cut. Thou shalt take heed both in this and in
the other decoctions that thou skim away diligently al the foom that swymmeth
above, as venomous. This water shalt thou keep and again power other unto it
warm, and heet it a while moderately, changing the water so oft till the routes
retain none or very little bitterness any more: which shall cum to pas when thou
hast Filtrum or through a brush: and at length sis all with a slow fire, or
with burnt cools rather, so that they boil not, yet let them be always at the
point of boiling, until they be as thick as honey in an earthen pot glased, or
of bras tinned, the pot covered or little open. When as now a little water
remains, about the end of the siething, stur it about gently now and then with
same time for a pound of rotes of Elleborum, thou shalt put.ii. drammes of
Mastik pund, and cease not to stir it, other continually, or by little
distaunces, till the juice seem out and out sufficiently thickened, which will
chance suddenly for the most part: and that the matter may be the les
burnt, the ne'er unto the end and to the thickening the juice is, so much urge
it with les fire: nor be not weary of the time: for thou shalt have a most
excellent and exquisite medicine against diverse Asarum or Asaraba^cha, and
Cloves, but stronger, ye burning as it seems to the taster: yet is not burning
in deed, that is because of the tenuitie and sclendernes of the parts, as I
wold have tried. It is ministered an hour after supper, in the moutenance of a
peas, in all diseases whereunto Elleborus is convenient, and where it is good
to lose Elleborum alone, but infused and decocted with other diverse medicines,
as I shall declare by and by. taken grene or dry. But a man must take a poud at
the least of the rotes. Lullius and other (Cardan) have made mention of the
quintessence of Elleborum, where unto I think this juice to be nothing
inferior. This truly is worthy to be wondered at, that the juice so lunge
decocted is not only nothing weaker but also much stronger: for it seems,
(that I may speak of conjecture) that one part of this juice to be ten
times and more stronger then Elleborus it self, and yet nothing Mastik is
added at the last, specially for more hard and grievous diseases, where
necessity requires extreme medicines: and for Frantic persons and
otherwise mad and doting, which when Acatia and of Berberies in Bulcasis.
For the straining of it only is decocted, not that which is depressed out:
also the juice of Galbanus and Licoris: but the water in them

Other iuices are made all by expression, and aresod till they gather into a
Ielly, as of S^landine, Poppy, Wormwod, nightshade, Vinae Acerbae, Memithae,
Agremony, Hypocisthidis, the barks of the rotes of Mandrag. The same Bulcasis
also, wills to put to a little gum to certain iuices that be pressed out which
are sod by them selves at the fire, that the parts of the may better cleue
together, as the juice of wormwod gasid, that is Eupatorium or nightshade: also
of Centaury, and Mastik is mixed with ye juice of Elleborus, not only for this
cause, but also to amend the venomous virtue thereof and contrary to the
stomach. Moreover in certain other medicines, where a ma list to increase the
strength and virtue of any medicin which is to be left and laid away in some
liquor, we change not the liquor, but the medicin: that is ever when the first
is strained, putting in new medicin into the same liquor, as in many decoctions
Elleborus we do contrary: For keeping the same rotes of the Elleborus, we
change oft the liquor. I may tel the cause, because ye rote of Elleborus
above all other things, hath most strong virtues, not in ye superficial other
parts only, but in ye hole substance imprinted vehemently fastly: wherefore it may
be also kept most long of al other. I my self used it, when it was.x. years
old, in his full strength. And certain common Practicioners bid men in a
part of it hath sooked a night and the next day is strained, and the rotes
to be dried again: for they are nothing or very little weaker therewith although
they be somewhat often repeated to the same use and dried again. But of
Elleborus it self and what we have tried and experimented thereof, perauenture
we shall once have an other place and occasion to write of.

Pills of blak Elleborus, or rather of his juice, is to be taken every seventh
day in the curing of the Matthaeolus couselith. Three drams of the roots of
black Veratrum or Elleborus fresh and new: Dauci or yelow-Caret, Anisi, Pepper,
of every one a dram.vi. grains of Mosth. ii scrupuls of Epithy mii or the
harder time: let all these be lightly beaten: then when they have stand to
soak a day and a night, v. pounds of Malmsye (hear seems to be some fault of
the printer, for it is to great a measure of wine) let them be mightilye pressed
out. Take.iii. Cochiarum, Aurearum, of every one. ii drams and a half, knede
them together, and then let them be dried. Moreover when they are dry made
again in powder, thou shalt steep them again in the same Malmsy, as
before.iiii. times. The pills thereof, must be taken at one time, to the
weight of one dram.

Our juice composed and made of Elleborus. Two pounds of Black Elleborus
new cut: stiep them a night in a good quantity of water. The next day after
sis them an hour a half with a slow fire, or.ii. hours, power it out
straining it, and put in to it other water warm (which shall be ready in a chafer
by the fyr for this purpose. Repeat this seven times or more. Then casting away
the routes, take the hooll water streined or destilled by a Filtrum, and sis
it by little and little and when as yet a little of it shall remain, power unto
it this decoctio: Seven handful of Betain, Anisi. iii. vnces, stiep the in
water and sis them till the consumpcion and wasting of the third part: pres
it out, strain it twys or thrys, at the length put to five vnces of Agaricum
elect cut small: flower deluce two vnces and a half: Cinnamon six drams, Ginger
half an uncle: sis them to the half, pres them out strain the water as before.
Then power this decoction to the decoction of the Elleborus boiling moderately:
and let them be ^od together to the thickness of honey. Scammonium: this
dissolved together in a little of the decoction of Elleborus, that it may be
like the substance of honey, not much before the end of the decotion power it
in, (when the juice shall be moderately thyck, or begins to be thick) and thou
shalt stur it about till the juice be consumed. Thou shalt try now and then a
drop of the juice, let it fall upon a tinnen ^rencher, and when it shall be so
thick that it the trencher on the one side or lifts it up, then is the juice
perfect. But by the space of an hour or more before the end thou shalt now
and then stur it about with a slow fire, lest it burn too. Of this decoctio I
had almost.xi. vnces of juice: I think I should have had skarsly ye fourth
part, if I had sod Elleborum alone. This juice have I used already sumtymes:
for I ministered it unto a yonge man sik of the falling evil, with good success,
who now of long time God be thanked, is well: but Ascarides or little round
worms breeding in the long gut, many medicines tried in vain, was restored
with this juice once received, and a few days after taking hearts horn burnt.
Pills of the bigness of a pees, from.ii. to five may be ministered after supper,
that is from half a scrupull or there about to a hool. But a man may try this
better in after sopper, ye next day I felt no small grief about my stomach, I
avoided sutymes downward, but I vomited not. A man may more safely give it to
the yt be sufficiently full of flesh or fat, of a moist stomach, and a stomach
of moo exquisite sense.

Again of the juice of black Elleborus: and of the drawing out of the virtues
out of purging medicines and certain other, as I learned of a certin friend.
THis drawing out was invented for delicate Ellebor{us}. iiii. poud of blak
true Ellebo. new fresh, let the be straight ways washed: the cut small power upon
the this manner of streining, ye juice of Buglos, Borrage, of ether.ii. pounds:
let the be purified strained most diligently, y^ they may be as pure as gold,
let the be laid or set up for a time. The take fenel roots Cichori, sperage,
persely, of every.iiii. vnc. Iuiuba ru, sebesten, of ether.ii. vnces. Melon
siedes, cucumer siedes, gourd siedes, citrul siedes, of every an uncle, sis
the accordig to art in. 16. pouds of rain water. Unto this straining power ye
forsaide.iiii. poudes of juice: let the boyll a little at y^ fire: afterward put
in y^ cut beat Ellebo. sis the lightly by little little till yt the roots
appear above, the pres the out again, strain the through a filtru, sis this
strainig to ye thiknes of honey with from half a scrupul to a hool or further. It
is ministered to louse ye belly, folded or moulded in a little moystned dow
unleavened, mixed with a drop or two of oyll of Anis.

The extraction of Rhabarbari. Cut a pound of the pieces of Rhabarbarum elect
small, and beat it somewhat gros, and power to it ye clarified iuices of Borage
and Buglos, of ether two pounds, let them stand.xxiiii. hours. Afterward
sis them at a slow fire, till the Rhabarbarum appear and Balneo Mariae till
it be thick lest it should put to which is better, as likewise in a double
vessel. In purging give it to be swalowed down mixed with a drop of oyll of
Cinnamon and Anis, folded in unleavened dow, making a trial Rhabarbarum by it
self doth not purge greatly, do sharpen the hooll extraction with a little of the
best Dacrydium, which hurts not.

An extractio of pills. Take any lump or mas that y^uo will of pills composed
most diligently, of the best medicines: when it is broken into little pieces,
stiep it eight days in rain water juice of Borage, juice of Fenell, in equal
porcions, as much as suffices. Then sis it in a glasen double most clean
wullen clothe, that will lose none of his hears, and sis it again in a bath
and double vessel diligently to a convenient thickness, and frame little pills
whereof thou shalt give.ii. scruples or there about. A matter drawn out in
this wise is most pure, very tender and melts easily in a mans hand. In like
manner may also the virtues of other purging medicines be drawn out. But heed
must be taken, that ever softening and slippery iuices be mixed therewith in the
decoction,

A description of a purging Electuari extracted, which a certain excellent
Physician at Norinberg did use.xiii. drammes of Colocynthidis, blak Elleborus,
senae Alexandrinae, of ether an ounce, of most white Agaricum an ounce:
Rhabarbarum Electum half an ounce, or if the Rhabarbarum be not very good, an
ounce: xiiii. drams of Dacrydium: Turbirh, (if I rede right) Stichas Ara. of
ether Lignum Aloes, Mastik, red Mirrh, Asarum, spiknard, Styrax liquid, of
every.v. scrupuls. Pour altogether and digest or putrify the, as they cal it.x.
days or.xiiii. in warm Aqua vitae thrice destilled. The juice pressed out thereof
shall be joined and mixed with.iii. vnces of prepared Aloes. But the Aloes did he
prepare in this wise. About apoud of Aloes is put into a basin, caudron or pot,
putting thereto vinegar made with Rosewater, and Roosewater of the vinegar of
Roses, then of the water of Roses. Let them boyle together with a slow
fire.ii. or.iii. boylinges: then strain them pressing them strongly. When it
is strained, let it boyle again with a slow fire to the thickness of Aloes
stirring it continually with a slise. When it is could, let it be kept for
use. The species or particulers rehearsed are first cut small, then beaten,
infused in Aqua vitae, as much as is sufficient, they are let stand in the
infusion warm in Balneo Mariae or sand.xiiii. days, stirring and chafing
them in Diacy donion of the said medicin. If there remain yet any moisture let
it be put upon burning coals in some vessel as much as is sufficient.

Of the juice of the Flour Deluce and Rape. c.
OVre Flower Deluce is more strong in operation in the dropsy, as seems me,
then the Florentines: of the which opinion a friend of mine also a very well
lear^ed Phisicion is, who of late sent me these his experiments and trials
of the juice of the root of Flower Deluce and Rape. Take a hens of the Flower
Deluce in place, and mixte it diligently with the yolke: and when thou hast a
little warmed it in the ashes, give it for a mornings draught, which shall
avoid downward above mesure the dropsy water.

This also (saith he) have I amongst my secretes for them that be sick of the
dropsy and can not sweet: take the juice of a round Rape beaten in a mortar and
pressed out, seasoned with Sugar or cinnamon, that it taste something else then the
Rape,

The juice of Purslan, Singreen or Houslike, Cotyledonis, and of other likewise
that be slimy and Clammy, because of the clammines can not be pressed out
alone: The herb well punde with Omphacium is pressed out. Other when they are
punde heat them at a flow fire. Other beat them and let them stand in a cold
place, as a wine cellar, put in a basket of wikars, to destill down into
Syluius, in whom thou shalt read moore of the preparing and preserving of
iuices, in his book of the preparacio of simples.

Of Lignum sanctum and any other, we draw out tears, which is moore
precious then the wood it self, in this wise. The Lignum is cut in pieces,
the thickness of a finger, which are put in the fire: thereby is gathered what
tere so ever is in the Ligno. Cardanus.

Milk is gathered of Esula Selandin, cuttinge the highest bows, and laid
hielding in a vessel, pressing (milking or slipping* the one by one. The liquor
so gathered shalt thou dry in the sun. But the juice is gathered when the
herbs are punde and pressed out: Bulcasis.

Gummes that be purging and other, (if any refuse to swalowe in the form of
pills) put them in boiling water, let them be dissolved, strain them: and
put in oil of swiet Almonds, and give it in drink with rob.

Put any strong purging medicine in water or wine: Then in the same liquor
steep dry prunes, or dry Figs, or Rasins till they swell, then wash them
in wine. These fruit received within the body do maruelouslye and without
grefe loose the bealye: Arnold in his book of wine. Sis.iiii. ounces of
Passularium with water of Violets half an hour, lift them through a sieve: the
sprinkle in.ii. drams of Scammonium, let them be dried. Then when thou wilt
give.xxx. or.xl. Panatella. Epiphanius Empericus.

Some stuff a fat Goose with medicines, with well liking Kitlinges chopte
small, with Salt, and roastinge it softly upon a brooch, gather the liquor
that drops therout, to annoynte gouty members therewith. Io. Goenrotus. I
harde say howe a certain practicioner roosted a gose stufte with Mise, chopte
small, and ministered the liquor gathered there of to the anointing of the
crooked vunche upon the back.

Of decocted things.
WE cal decoctions liquors, water, wine, or vinegar: in the which any medicines
be sod at the fire, and then streined. c. of the which for shortness sake at
this present we will speak nothing, saving that we will admonish, that when
they are beaten or chopped they ought to be first stiept which should be sod and
decocted in a vessel diligently stopped and cloosed.

A decoction in vinegar against the pestilence. Sis two handful of the
biggar Salandin, the herb and root together, in.iiii. pound and a half of the
best vinegar, upon cools in a glased pot with a cover the mouth whereof shall be
fensed with clay of other. And of late I red almost ye very same medicine in a
certain book of a certain practicioner, written in french, and a few years
past when the pestilence was here, a certain friend of mine sent unto me a like
description. Many would have the Selandine sod in the vinegar, but other put
other things to: sum, as I said, Few only: other also Sage noble, Rosemary,
the leeues of flower deluce, not the root, Zedoaria and sis it in a pot well
covered: thereof they give a few drops

Otherwise.. Wormwood, Few, the younglings Selandin. iii. parts. Sis the in
white vinegar q. s. that is as much as is sufficient in a pot clayed, till the
third part be consumed, let it be given as soon as ama is infected, after let
him sweet.

Otherwise.. A pound of the roots of Seladine, Brimstone, Saffron, Turmentill,
an uncle, Triacle.ii. vnces: pimpernel, Gentian, of every an uncle and a half:
Pilosella or mous ear, with the root and all. Few, of ether an handful, Sage
with.ii. quarts of whyt vinegar till the third part be consumed. In this
decoction dissolve electuary of an eg (or triacle) to the quantity of a been
let it be given to the patient as thou know.

Of made vvyne, and mixed with medicines.
LAst of all we will teach and declare here certain things of made and
counterfeit wynes, yet not of all of purpose, Dioscorides, Aetius in his last
book, and to the book of Arnold Villa nouani of wynes. Wynes mixed with
medicines are made diverse ways: first siething the medicines with the wine
while it is must and new, for the most part to the consumpcion of the third
part, skumming in the mean season straining afterward. Sum sis the wine
alone: other till ye Zedoaria, with Inula, also with Borrage and Melissa, in
Arnold. Sum sis a little new must and when it is couled, they power it upon
the herbs in the vessel. Secondly putting the wine, before it be hot, that
by the heet of it in the vessel, the virtue of the medicin may be mixed with it:
So the most men with us prepare wine of wormwood, and they let it stand a
hooll winter in the vessels, ever filling the vessels again, if they draw any
thing out of it, in the beginning of vere they take the herbs out: sum leave
them in al summer also, but yet it is easily corrupted, waxes hoor and mouldy,
and sower specially if the vessel be not full. Sum set a plate of iron tinned
boored Cariophyllata dry be put to, y^ wine shall be preserved the logar. Other
sis simpely with wine the medicine whose virtue they would have to pas in the
wine, at any time of the Inula. c. Wine may also be mixed and streined with the
iuices of herbs clarified: or hanged in a little bag in it a few days, to be
pressed out and when it is strained to be mixte with the wine, and new
medicines to be put into the bag, c. for if the matter be pressed out often and
new put to, the wine shall be the stronger, and we shall fill the vessel now
and then for that that is drawn out: for if not, the wine will become every
foot weaker.

Wines medicined (as we have said) may be made, leaving the medicines put in
a bag, or Arnold in his book of wine. Such as a ma list to sethe, it should be
best to sethe it in a double vesselor in Baln. Ma.

Aromaticall made wines or with spices may be kept for.iii. or.iiii. days
clear, afterward they are troubled. Plinius teaches the confections of wines
of diverse simple medicines. 14. 16.

Wine of Wormwode howe it is made, I have Wormwode: other mixte with it other
diverse herbs, specially hot: as Hyssop, Rue, Sage, Cardnus Benedictus, Penny
royal, Costus Hortensis, Phyllitides, the flowers of Eldar, the Barks of
Ash. I make sometimes in a glass forthwith, putting the leaves of Wormwode dry,
into Malmsy and burning water, thrice destilled of ether like much. This may
be long kept: a little of it put to a great deal of wine, doth season it holly
with the quality of Wormwode. It is good for a cold stomach, Arnoldus in his
book of wines. A better way of making it saith he, that the Wormwod grene or
dry be beat, and that the wine warm be straind upon it certain times, till it
receive the savour and virtue of it, and to season it with Sugar or Honey: this
way to make this wine is better then other, because all the strength of the
Wormwod is in the superficial Galangal or Anise may also be added, or any
other thing as a man thinks good. Wine of Mugwort is made in the same manner
that wormwode wine is.

Wine of the kirnels of Alkckengi or Haliacacabus, is made the same way,
straining the wine up on them beaten, for one Dosis, of it thou shalt take
from.v. to.x. it brings out stony matter fro the Arnold in his book of wine)
whereof I my self also made a trial of late, straining together Anise, the rote
of Carlina, the kirnels of Peches, and y^ little stones of crabs, whereupon the
vrin holden now of long space, followed within an hour. Some put the grains
of Haliacacabus hole into new wine in a little vessel, that they may sis
together, and keep it for their use.

Wine of Betain is good for the griefs of the stomach. Alexan. Benedictus,

Wine of Buglos of the rotes of Buglos stieped in wine, is maruelouslye
commended of Arnold against the diseases of Melancholy, with a story of a
certain woman healed with the drincking thereof, which through anger, became
oftentimes a foal. Wine of Buglos, wherein the rotes of Buglos well washed have
ben a day and a night or the syrup of it, it brings mirth and is good for
melancholical persons. Certain me have ben cured by the use of that wine from
madness, and the Arnold, of the coseruacion of youth, the.iii. chap. An other
wine composed with the rotes and flowers of Buglos, with Sena or without it,
shalt thou read in Arnold in his book of wine, with this title. A marvelous
wine for Melancholik persons and cardiacal. c Wine of borage is made, if in new
wine y^ flours of borage be put till the perfect fining: or else if Dia
borraginatum or conserve of Borage be dissolved in new wine, and kept for the
use: Arnoldus in his

A composition of wine against burnt humours, and for Choleric persons and
Frantic, inducinge mirth. Take a pound of the rotes of Buglos made clean:
Red Rooses. Flowers of Violets, Borrage and Buglos, of every a pound and a
half: Been white and red, of ether an ounce. When they are beat together
put them in a bag, and the bag in a vessel conteininge.iii. burdens of good
wine (new I suppose) fresh and newly pressed out white and clearest that may
Arnold, of the coseruacion of health.

Wine made with Inula. The Roots of Inula cut small as herbs to the pot,
sethe them in a new earthen pot glased in.ii. pintes or more of Inula
together with the new wine wherein they sod, and when as yet they have sod
a little while together, ye shall set it down from the fire and keep it.

An other way. When the new swite wine is skimmed and sod to the
consumpcion of the put in y^ roots of Inula. (for.xxv. congees or there about,
which is about.xv. galons of our measur, take.ix. vnces:) and when the must or
new wine hath yet a little boiled, take it of and when it is cold kiep it, and
hang the roots of the Inula (the very same that were separated in the
streining of the wine) in a bag within the vessel. The roots should be
gathered in October in the spring of the moan, or in the beginning of summer,
to be dried in the sun. This did I translate out of a certain Dioscorides,
which names this wine Nectarite.

Wine Arceuthite. For.xx. congees or there about of must, take half a fourth
deell of Juniper beries (a fourth deel we call of the measure of Heluetia, but
that varies also, the fourth part.) First thou shalt straw the bottom of the
vessel with thin chipes of hewed ash, lest the beries may stop ye entrance of
the tap: then straw the half part of the beries in upon that: thirdly the
rest of the chipes, furthly

Our contrymen make wine in summer, with ye Visula, putting them hool into the
vessel, leaving the fourth part empty, pouring in upon them white old wine,
which in short space will have a reed colour, and sooner if all the stalks be
cleen taken away, more slowly if they be so cut that part of them be left in,
but yet thus, they keep the colour logest. But they are wont when they have
drawn any wine out, to fill the vessel again by by. It strengthens the hart
and stomach, asswageth thirst, couleth, dries, binds. Other put them not in
holl but

Wine of wild Plums Bulies or Sloos, is made in the like manner in heruest,
when they shall be now sufficiently tender and ripe enough.

Wine Raspoticium (dutche men call it rappis) Raspish wine, that is, which
bites the tongue with a certain sharp biding, it provokes appetite byndyng the
hears of the stomach. It helps the body dissolved with heat, chiefly in
summer it is proffitable for Choleric and Sanguine persons: it is made in
this wise. Some sower grapes together with the ripe are put in the wine pres to
be pressed out together. Or yt is better, let the grapes be kept and brooken
together with Raspaciis, put into the vessel with the Must: That Must or new
wine, by the juice of this Raspacia (Scapos French men cal grappes, our
cotrymen rappen, whereupon the wine takes the name) or kirnels in the grapes
which are sower, doth get a certain ponticitie or taste like wormwood and
bynding: Arnoldus de Villa Nova. In our country they make it otherwise, they
fill the wine vessels with holl clusters well rypet, and power old wyn in to
them, and as often as they draw any wine out of it, they fill it again. Sum
put in clusters and Sage leeues in course. It refreshes the stomach, asswageth
thirst. Nicolaus My repsus in the letter D. the. 85. Chapter, describes the
making of a certain Dysentericall drink with good Raspe, or pure Marathratum,
that is wine made Elelisphacatum wine, that is wyn made with Sage. Fuchsius
says, for Rhaspe, perauenture ought to be reed Rhoites or Rhodites In the
same author, the first preservative is made with good reed wine or Rhaspe: and
likewise the last preservative. I understand that to be Rapysh wine, which
always is made with Sage. c. and reed always.

Sum put spices also to the Raspish wine, as Galangal five vnces. Cinnamon,
Cloves, of ether two drammes, Zedoaria half an uncle, Coriander iii. vnces,
make somewhat a gros powder thereof.

Wine of small raisins, which Arnold calls honied, is made siething* the
Rasins in wine (in must) till it wax swiet, which is put furthwith in to a
vessel, and the small rasins beaten are cast in to the same, which go to the
bottom into ye dregs.

But the same Arnold, describes an other also very excellent, which he calls
Passulatu or made of small Raisins, the Raisins sod with Cinnamon Must not
much, which afterward is powered to the other Must put in the vessel already.

Of Aromaticall vvynes that is made of Spices.
ARomaticall wynes are wont to be made two ways, ether hanging the spices only
in a little bag, within the vessel which is let stand in a wine cellar: or also
putting Mulsum or Melitum, they cal it communly Claret and Nectar and
Melicratium but improperly, with white wine. If they put to sugar for honey,
with reed wine, they cal it Hippocras. There are made at the Apothecaries this
spyced and aromatical wynes for the honey be put to.vii. drams of spices:
and.iiii. poudes of white odoriferous wine. Or to.vi. drams of spices, half a

honey. But before ye wine be streined, it should be let stand in a hot place or
in a stoue with ye spices infused, certain hours.xxiiii. at the most.

Wine called Hippocras is of the kind of made wines, after an easy way:
most acceptable to the French men, specially on this side the Alps.
Cinnamon, Sugar and Carpesium are beaten bruised in a bag, through it the wine
is poured, that in passing through, it may dryncke and soak those qualities.
This is brought forth in principal feasts, with Escharite pane in stead of
banketing dishes, the which fashion, but with an other kind of wine, we read,
was peculier to the Athenians. Hermolaus Barbarus Corollarii. libr. 5. in the
chapter of the Grape of oenanthe.

An other. The inner barks of Cinnamon. vi drammes: half an ounce of white
Ginger whole* Nutmegs elect.ii. drammes, Cloves, grains of paradise, of
ether a dram: Cardamomum, Pepper, Calamus Aromaticus, Coriander prepared, of
every one a scrupull, mixte them and beat them somewhat groose. Eight pounds
of wine, clarified honey.xxvi. ounces, mixte all, and strain spiced wines with
Almond milk.

An other that seems to be ordained for the defaults Cardamomum, Flower

Hippocras Laxative. A wine against the Quartaine, Quotidian, and bastard
Tertian, i^ concocteth and prepares the humours and leads the same by and
by out by the siege. Take a pound of Esula: Epithy mi. vi. dras, Polipodii,
cinamo, small rasins, of every.iiii. dras, Mastik, gin. Zedoria, cloves, of
every one an once. Sugar as much as needs: Arnold.

Nectar in Arnold. For a pint of wine, take Ginger elect scraped, Cloves,
Cinnamon scraped, of every.ii. drammes, grains of Paradise, a dram. Let it be
made wine, or Grekish, which is better: and in stead of honey put Sugar, with
a gran of Musk, and it is most noble. A syrrup or Iulep is made of wine to
conserve health and youth, putting into.iii. pounds of good wine, two pounds
of Sugar. Let it be made a Sirup, the use whereof is with water. It may stand in
stead Arnold in the book of conseruacions of youth.

Wine made with Sugar decocted, is good for old persons, cold and feeble,
and in whom the natural moisture and heate are diminished: for it norysheth,
and breeds blood, and fills the principal parts with spirits. Take the
best wine of Vernacia, or Grekish, or like unto them.iii. poudes: a pound of
white Sugar Caffetini. Let the be sod with a soft fire in manner of Syrrup,
Rabi Moysses in his book of the manner of diet for old men and such as are in
recovering their health, doth approve and allow.

Of svviet wines spiced.
OF wine made with sugar spices it is already Mulsa or wynes made with honey
and spices. A man may in all the forsaid composicions, both in steed of Sugar
put honey, and in steed of reed wine, white: in those that follow contrary
wise. So of Hippocras wine, may thou make claret or clear: and of clear,
Hippocras. Sum make no difference of the colour of the wine.

The honey ought always to be sod first with a little water and to Diosc. Alchandicu
is a wine sod with spices sugar Ebenesis.

Certain composicions of Nectar of claret, out of a certain book written in
Latin, when a man would have a greater quantity thereof to be kept in his
cellar in a barill or vessel of wood. Lotum, as it appears, is a name of
certain mesure of wine, in Spain perauenture.

Claret or good Nectar shalt thou make in this wise. Cinnamon a pound,
Galangall, Ginger, Cardamom. Grains of Paradis, Cloves, Cubebae, Macis, of
every an uncle: Long pepper, and black Schenoanthum, of every one.iii. dras,
Saffron a dram: Let all be beten very small and mixed. So hast thou spices for
half a horse lood, yt is for one barrel, (perauetur a. 128. pounds. The nectar
shalt thou make thus. Put all the wine in the vessel, then put the spices in
sum linen cloth large enough, and let the clothe with spices into ye barill
wherein thou wilt make thy nectar, in such sort that the sides of the cloth may
hang over the Lignum Aloes of the best, Foliu, and in the stead of honey put
sugar finely beaten in a mortar, then dissolved in the wine.

An other very good wine with ye same species, but in other weight. Take.xii.
vnces a half of cinnamon, ginger, galangal, grains of paradise, cloves,
cubebae, macis, Cardamomu, of every.ii. dras: log pepper a scrupul: spikenard,
Schoenanthu, nutmegs, of every a scrupul a half: saffron to ye weighte of a
(Deranii) of Turona or a little more. Mixed al these together when they are most
small Lotu Nectar of the city Burgens. Then take a pound of honey with a pint of
springwater sod to the consupcio of the water, set it aside to cole. The take
about a Lotum, of good wine and when the juice with the forsaid spices is put
into the vessel, streining the wine through it, pour it in by little and
little, the honey also mixed first with a little of the wine shall be poured in by
little and little, so that the juice be not pressed together. If so be it ye will
make it more preous, Agallochum of the best Folium of ether.ii. grains, a little
Musk. Otherwise: hang the species in a bag with in the wine mixed with honey or
sugar, after.ii. or.iii. dais take it out pres it well. But the former way is
better finer.

A drink named Manus Dei.. Cinnamon an ounce and a half, Ginger.iii. drams:
Cardamomu ii. drams, Folium, Galangal, of ether a dra, spiknard, Carui, of
ether half a dram: long Pepper or Cloves, Nutmegs, Maces, Grains of paradise,
of every.ii. drams, clarified honey a pint: a Burges Lotum of good wine. And if
thou can in stead of honey, put a pound of Sugar. If this drink be rightly
made, it shall be profitable against many diseases, specially cold, and in old
men, and them that be somewhat weak feeble unto copulation. It shall be convenient
for Fleumaticke Melancholik persons marvelously, that not without a cause it
may be called Manus Dei, that is the hand Saxifrage of the stone called Lynx be
added

Of spiced wines with burning water.
THat burning water doth drink in easily all ye odour and virtue both of other
medicines and also of spices, if they stand to stiep in it a few hours, Arnold
in the descripcion of simple Aqua ardens or burning water. The confection or
making of the wine which they cal commoly Hippocras. Put into (the name of the
mesure is not expressed) of burning water, destilled.iii. or.iiii. times or
more.ii. vnces of Cinnamon, Ginger half an uncle, grains of paradise, Furnerius.

A way to make Malmsy.. Galangall of the Let al this somewhat gross beten stad.
24. hours in a vessel of wood well covered infused in water. Then hang them in
a linne cloth by a thried into a vessel of a soom as they call it, (which is
about the bigness of a hogshead) or half a Soom of clear wyn.iii. days. And
thou shalt have wine so good strong as is the very natural Malmsy or Traminuum:
A dutche written book.

After the some manner is the taste of Muscatello wine made: take a Nutmeg with a
little Macis: mixed them beaten as is before said.

Cloued wine. Beat half a dram of Cloves with a little Cinnamo tie it in a linen
cloth, as is aforsaid. The taste of Elseter wine: bind sugar ca^y vessel well
stopped, when thou wilt use it, wring out a linne cloth dipped in this liquor,
into ye pot. All this have we borrowed out of a dutch book write.

Of certain other Aromatical vvynes, specially such as are made by hanging a
little bag in the vessel.
CLoued wine is made hanging the cloves in a little bag within the vessel with
must. It dries Arnold in his book of wine.

After the same manner any spices ( medicines) both cold dry, may be hanged in a
little bag put Must, which we would have seasoned with their virtues quality:
In the same place.

An aromatical wine is made to coserue youth, if spices be put in a linen bag
vnbeten, but cut in to parts, so that the substance of the spices be
wandering in the little bag, and let it be put in the wine, the mouth of the
wine diligently closed. Arnoldus of the conservation of youth.

An other way of aromaticall wine, for the confirming of the temperature, and
youth. Cubebae, iii. drams, let them boyll in a little bag in.iii. pound of good
wine, till the cosumpcion of ye third part: put to Sugar, and thereof morning and
evening give an uncle or other about at every time to drink. Or else let it be
made like claret: Arnoldus in the same place.

Saffrond wine brings mirth, and takes away Melancholines: Arnold in his
book of conserving youth, the.iii. chapt. The manner of making it teaches he
in the second tretise of the same book,

Of Artificial vv^nes which resem* ble the taste of strange wynes, because of
the spyoes hagged in a little bag within the vessel.
HOw diverse Aromaticall wynes be made and a counterfeit taste of certain strange
wynes with burning water and spices, it is showed before: Here will we ad howe
witty men may inuitate certain strange wines without burning water: not to
the intent cove tous men may learn disceit thereby, but that physicians may both
gratify sumtymes proffit also the sick specially such as be somewhat delicate.
We have taken al y^ follow out of a dutch write book.

Red Muscatello shalt thou make thus. The slowers of Sambuc{us}, iiii. vnc.
Must. All such things are thought to make ye smell of Muscatello, that do
resemble Musk in their own odour, and such things as are surnamed of Musk,
as the nutmeg, and the bark of it that is called Macis, also the flowers of
Sambucus or elder chiefly, and Coriander. Many also hang the herb called
Sclar^a, in wine, the flowers with the leeues: whose savour is vehement not
unpleasant yet sum think it to be vnholsum, and to grieve the head. But such
